Return for Defect: Court Order vs. Consent
Commentary explaining when return for defect is rescission and when it is a new sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between return for defect by court order (qada) and by consent (rida), and state the Hanafi ruling on the nature of each (rescission vs new sale) with the exception for currency exchange.

Core Idea
A court-ordered return rescinds the contract only for the future; a consent-based return is a new sale.

This passage from Ibn Najim's al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a critical distinction in the Hanafi law of defects (khiyar al-'ayb). The key is whether the return is effected by a judge's ruling (qada) or by mutual agreement (rida). The ruling changes not only the buyer's right to compensation but also the obligations of third parties. We will explore the evidence and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modes of return
Recognise that return for defect can occur by court order (qada) or by mutual consent (rida). The legal nature differs.
The passage states: 'If he accepts it without court order, he does not have the right to return it to his seller because it is a new sale regarding the third party'.
2
Locate the dalil for prospective rescission
The evidence is that the accessories (zawa'id) of the sold item belong to the buyer and are not returned. If rescission were retroactive, they would have to be returned.
Example: if someone gifts wealth and takes it back after the year, zakat for the past is not owed. This proves rescission is only for the future.
3
Examine the objections against prospective rescission
Three cases are raised: (1) pre-emption right not invalidated, (2) paternity claim after return, (3) assignment (hawala) not invalidated. These seem to suggest rescission is not retrospective.
The author answers these in al-Mi'raj: rescission is prospective, so past legal effects remain.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The rescission by court order makes the contract as if it never existed only for the future. Past transactions remain valid.
Shaykh al-Islam explains: 'It makes the contract as if it never existed in the future, not in the past.'
5
Note the exception for currency exchange (sarf)
In sarf (exchange of gold/silver), any return, even by consent, is a rescission because the items are not specified in contracts.
Hence, the buyer can return the defective dinar to the original seller even if the return was by consent.

Source Text

البيع باب خيار العيب كما في الكافي وصورة الإقرار أن يقول اشتريته وبه ذلك العيب ولم أعلم به وقضى به ثم ادعاه على بائعه وبرهن ببينة أو استحلف بائعه ؛ كذا في الولوالجية. وليس المراد منه أنه بيع بمجرد القضاء عليه بإقراره يرده فليتأمل وإن قبله بغير قضاء ليس له رده على بائعه لأنه جديد في حق الثالث وإن كان فسخاً في حقهما والأول ثالثهما وأطلقه فشمل ما يحدث مثله، وما لا يحدث مثله وهو قول العامة، وتقييده فسخاً مسائل : الأولى لو كان المبيع عقاراً فرد بعيب لم يبطل حق الشفيع في الشفعة الثانية لو باع أمته الحبلى وسلمها ثم ردت بعيب بقضاء ثم ولدت فادعاه أبو البائع لم تصح دعوته ولو كان فسخاً لصحت كما لو لم يبعها . الثالثة لو أحال البائع غريمه على المشتري بالثمن ثم رد المبيع بعيب بقضاء لم تبطل الحوالة ولو كانت فسخاً لبطلت وأجاب في المعراج بأنه فسخ فيما يستقبل لا في الأحكام الماضية ولهذا قال شيخ الإسلام : قول القائل الرد بالقضاء يجعل العقد كأن لم يكن تناقض لأن العقد إذا جعل كأن لم يكن جعل الفسخ كأن لم يكن لأن الفسخ بدون العقد لا يتصور، فإذا انعدم أصله انعدم الفسخ من الأصل، وإذا انعدم الفسخ من الأصل عاد العقد لانعدام ما من العقد ينافيه ولكن يقال يجعل العقد كأن لم يكن في المستقبل لا في الماضي اهـ. والدليل على أن الفسخ إنما هو في المستقبل أن زوائد المبيع للمشتري ولا يردها مع الأصل ولهذا لو وهب مالاً قبل تمام الحول ثم رجع الواهب بعد الحول لا تجب الزكاة عليه فيما مضى؛ كذا في المعراج. ولو وهب داراً وسلمها فبيعت دار بجنبها فأخذها الموهوب له بالشفعة ثم رجع الواهب فيها لم يكن له الأخذ بشفعة؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقد كتبنا في الفوائد أن الرد بالعيب بقضاء فسخ إلا في مسألة وإذا لم يرده في صورة الرضا لا رجوع له بالنقصان أيضاً كما في المعراج. وإذا كان له الرد فله الرجوع بالنقصان كما في التهذيب يعني لو حدث عيب ورده بقضاء فله الأرش ولو برضا لا. وقيد بالمبيع وهو العين احترازاً عن الصرف فإنه يجعل فسخاً إذا رد بعيب لا فرق القضاء والرضا لأنه لا يمكن أن يجعل بيعاً جديداً لأن الدينار هنا لا يتعين في العقود، فإذا اشترى ديناراً بدرهم ثم باع الدينار من آخر ثم وجد المشتري الثاني بالدينار عيباً ورده على المشتري بغير قضاء فإنه يرده على بائعه لما ذكرنا كما في المحيط والخانية. وفي الكافي: المبيعان هنا واحد لأن المعيب ليس بمبيع بل المبيع السليم فيكون المبيع ملك البائع فإذا رده على المشتري يرده على بائعه، أما هنا المبيعان موجودان فإذا قيل بغير قضاء فقد رضي بالعيب فلا يرده على بائعه اهـ . وذكر في الظهيرية ثم قال بعده وعلى هذا إذا قبض رجل دراهم له على رجل وقضاها من غريمه فوجدها الغريم زيوفاً فردها عليه بغير قضاء فله أن يردها على الأول اهـ. وخرج عن قوله بقضاء مسألة ذكرها في المبسوط : لو أقام المشتري الثاني أن العيب كان عند المشتري الأول ولم يشهد أنه كان عند البائع الأول فليس للمشتري الأول المخاصمة بائعة إجماعاً لأن المشتري الأول لم يصر مكذباً فيما أقر به ولم يوجد هنا قضاء على خلاف ما أقر به فبقي إقراره بكونها سليمة فلا يثبت له ولاية الرد ، ولكن لم يذكره محمد ؛ كذا في فتح القدير . والمعراج . اعلم أن القن إذا حكم برده بعيب الإباق على بائعه فاشتراه آخر فأبق عنده فله الرد على بائعه بالاباق السابق المحكوم به كما في الظهيرية وإقرار المشتري الأول بإباقه لا ينفذ على من لم يشتر منه من الباعة بخلاف إقرار البائع الأول بدين على العبد فإن للمشتري الآخر المبيع ملك البائع حق التعبير أن يقول فيكون المعيب تأمل . قوله : (وعلى هذا إذا قبض رجل الخ) قال في المبسوط : وإذا كان أجر الدار عشرة دراهم أو قفيز حنطة موصوفة وأشهد المؤجر أنه قبض من المستأجر عشرة دراهم أو قفيز حنطة ثم ادعى أن الدراهم نبهرجة وأن الطعام معيب فالقول قوله لأنه منكر استيفاء حقه فإن ما في الذمة يعرف بصفة ويختلف باختلاف الصفة فلا مناقضة في كلامه، قاسم الدراهم يتناول النبهرجة، واسم الحنطة يتناول المعيب وإن كان حين أشهد قال قبضت من أجر الدار عشرة دراهم أو قفيز حنطة لم يصدق بعد ذلك على ادعاء العيب والزيف، وكذلك لو قال استوفيت أجر الدار ثم قال وجدته زيوفاً لم يصدق ببيئتة ولا غيرها لأنه قد سبق منه الإقرار بقبض الجياد فإن أجر الدار من الجياد فيكون هو مناقضاً في قوله وجدته زيوفاً، والمناقض لا قول له ولا تقبل بينته اهـ. كذا نقله الإمام الطرسوسي في أنفع الوسائل ثم قال: وإذا تقرر لنا هذا في الإجارة والأجرة عديناه إلى استيفاء الأثمان في البياعات والديون في المعاملات فإن العلة تجمع الكل فنقول إذا دفع إليه دراهم وهي ثمن متاع ثم جاء البائع وأراد أن يرد عليه شيئاً وأنكر المشتري فإن كان البائع أقر بقبض الثمن لم يقبل قوله ولا يلزم المشتري دفع عوضه، وينبغي أن البائع لو اختار تحليف المشتري أنه ما يعلم أن هذا من دراهمه يحلفه القاضي، فإن نكل يردها عليه، وإن كان البائع لم يقر بقبض الثمن أو الحق الذي على المشتري من جهة هذا البيع وإنما أقر بقبض دراهم مثلاً فالقول للبائع لأنه منكر استيفاء حقه، ولم يتقدم منه ما يناقض دعواه فيقبل قوله مع يمينه، وكذلك اليوم أيضاً. وهذا كله إذا كان الذي يرده زيوفاً أو نبهرجة، فإذا كان ستوقة لم يقبل قوله لأنه ناقض أنه من دراهمه
The book of sales: Chapter of Option of Defect. As in al-Kafi: The form of acknowledgment is that he says, 'I bought it and it had that defect and I did not know about it,' and he (the judge) rules accordingly, then he (the buyer) claims it against his seller and provides evidence or takes an oath from his seller; so in al-Walwaljiyya. It is not meant by this that it is a sale merely by the judge's ruling upon his acknowledgment; he returns it, so let it be considered. If he accepts it without a court order, he does not have the right to return it to his seller because it is a new sale regarding the third party, even if it is a rescission between them two, and the first is their third. He (the author) made it absolute, so it includes what produces its like and what does not produce its like, and that is the general view. Its restriction in al-Jami' al-Saghir to what produces its like is to know the ruling of what does not produce its like by greater reason. In some narrations of the original, what does not produce its like, consent to it is like a court order. The author omitted another condition: that it be after taking possession of the sold item, because if it were before taking possession, it is a rescission for all, whether by court order or consent; so in al-Mi'raj cited from al-Mabsut. Another condition: the sale must be before becoming aware of the defect, because if after, he does not have the right to return it to his seller, even if he returns it as a rescission; so in al-Sughra. Objections were raised against it being a rescission: First, if the sold item were real estate and he returned it due to a defect, it does not invalidate the right of the pre-emptor in pre-emption. Second, if he sold his pregnant slave girl and delivered her, then she was returned due to a defect by court order, then she gave birth, and the seller's father claimed him, his claim is not valid; if it were a rescission, his claim would be valid as if he had not sold her. Third, if the seller assigned his debtor to the buyer for the price, then the sold item was returned due to a defect by court order, the assignment is not invalidated; if it were a rescission, it would be invalidated. He answered in al-Mi'raj: it is a rescission for the future, not for past rulings. For this reason, Shaykh al-Islam said: The statement of one who says, 'Return by court order makes the contract as if it never existed' is contradictory, because if the contract is made as if it never existed, the rescission is made as if it never existed, since rescission without a contract is inconceivable. If its origin ceases, the rescission ceases from the origin; if the rescission ceases from the origin, the contract returns due to the removal of what contradicts it from the contract. But it is said: it makes the contract as if it never existed in the future, not in the past. End. The proof that rescission is only for the future is that the accessories of the sold item belong to the buyer and he does not return them with the original. For this reason, if someone gifted wealth before the completion of the year, then the donor took it back after the year, zakat is not obligatory on him for the past; so in al-Mi'raj. If someone gifted a house and delivered it, then a house adjacent to it was sold and the donee took it by pre-emption, then the donor took back the gifted house, he does not have the right to take by pre-emption; so in Fath al-Qadir. We have written in al-Fawa'id that return for defect by court order is rescission except in one issue. If he does not return it in the case of consent, he has no recourse for the deficiency either; as in al-Mi'raj. If he has the right to return, he has the right to recourse for the deficiency; as in al-Tahdhib, meaning if a defect occurred and he returned by court order, he gets compensation (arsh); but if by consent, no. The restriction to the sold item, which is the specific object, is an exception for currency exchange (sarf), because it is treated as rescission if returned due to defect, with no difference between court order and consent, because it is impossible for it to be a new sale, since the dinar here is not specified in contracts. So if one buys a dinar for a dirham, then sells the dinar to another, then the second buyer finds a defect in the dinar and returns it to the first buyer without court order, he returns it to his seller for the reason we mentioned; as in al-Muhit and al-Khaniyya. In al-Kafi: The two sold items here are one, because the defective item is not sold, but the sound item is sold, so the sold item belongs to the seller; if he returns it to the buyer, he returns it to his seller. But here, the two sold items exist, and if it is said without court order, he has consented to the defect, so he does not return it to his seller. End. It is mentioned in al-Zahiriyya, then he said after it: On this basis, if a man receives dirhams owed to him from a man and pays them to his creditor, and the creditor finds them counterfeit and returns them to him without court order, he has the right to return them to the first (debtor). End. Included from his statement 'by court order' is an issue mentioned in al-Mabsut: If the second buyer proves that the defect was with the first buyer but does not testify that it was with the first seller, the first buyer does not have the right to litigate with his seller by consensus, because the first buyer was not rendered a denier of what he acknowledged, and there was no court order contradicting his acknowledgment, so his acknowledgment that it was sound remains, and he does not have authority to return. But Muhammad did not mention it; so in Fath al-Qadir and al-Mi'raj. Know that if a slave is ruled to be returned due to the defect of running away to his seller, then another person buys him and he runs away from him, he has the right to return him to his seller due to the previous running away that was ruled upon, as in al-Zahiriyya. The first buyer's acknowledgment of his running away is not effective against those who did not buy from him among the sellers, unlike the first seller's acknowledgment of a debt on the slave, because the other buyer (has claim). The seller owns the sold item; the correct expression is to say 'so the defective item is' reflect. His statement: (On this basis, if a man received dirhams, etc.) He said in al-Mabsut: If the rent of a house is ten dirhams or a specified qafiz of wheat, and the lessor witnesses that he received from the lessee ten dirhams or a qafiz of wheat, then he claims that the dirhams were underweight and the food defective, his statement is accepted because he is denying receiving his due; what is in the dhimmah is known by description and differs with difference in description, so there is no contradiction in his words. The name "dirhams" includes underweight ones, and the name "wheat" includes defective ones. But if when he witnessed he said, 'I received from the rent of the house ten dirhams or a qafiz of wheat', he is not believed thereafter in claiming defect and counterfeitness. Similarly, if he said 'I collected the rent of the house' and then said 'I found it counterfeit', he is not believed by evidence or otherwise, because he has previously acknowledged receiving good ones, since the rent of a house consists of good ones, so he is contradicting himself in his statement 'I found it counterfeit'. A contradictory person has no say and his evidence is not accepted. End. This is what Imam al-Tarsusi transmitted in Anfa' al-Wasa'il, then he said: Since this is established for us regarding ijarah and rent, we extend it to the collection of prices in sales and debts in transactions, for the cause (illa) unites all. So we say: If someone pays dirhams as the price of goods, then the seller comes and wants to return something to him and the buyer denies, if the seller acknowledged receipt of the price, his statement is not accepted and the buyer is not obliged to pay its replacement. It is appropriate that if the seller chooses to have the buyer sworn that he does not know that these are from his dirhams, the judge administers an oath; if the buyer refuses, he returns them. If the seller did not acknowledge receipt of the price or the right owed from the buyer due to this sale but rather acknowledged receipt of dirhams generally, then the statement is the seller's because he is denying receiving his due; he has not previously contradicted his claim, so his statement is accepted with his oath, and likewise today. All this applies when what he returns is underweight or counterfeit; if it is of inferior quality (satuqa), his statement is not accepted because he contradicts (that it is from his dirhams).
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the chapter of Option of Defect (Khiyar al-'Ayb) in the Hanafi school. The author, Ibn Najim, explains the ruling regarding a buyer who discovers a defect in the purchased item and wishes to return it. The central distinction is whether the return is effected by court order (qada) or by mutual consent (rida). The author clarifies that a return by court order is considered a rescission (faskh) of the original contract, but only prospectively. This means that past legal effects (such as the validity of pre-emption rights (shuf'a), paternity claims, or assignments (hawala)) that occurred before the return remain intact. The text provides three illustrative cases: (1) if a returned property was subject to pre-emption, the pre-emptor's right is not invalidated; (2) if a returned slave gave birth and the seller's father claims paternity, his claim is not accepted; (3) if the seller had assigned a debt to the buyer before return, the assignment remains valid. These examples demonstrate that the rescission does not retroactively cancel prior transactions. The author also discusses the case of return without court order (by mutual consent). In such a case, the return is deemed a new sale, not a rescission, because the buyer's acceptance of the defect implies a new transaction. Consequently, the buyer loses the right to claim compensation for the deficiency (arsh). However, an exception is made for currency exchange (sarf), where any return (even by consent) is considered a rescission, because currency is not specific in contracts. The passage also includes practical examples involving counterfeit dirhams and defective food items, and the rules for accepting or rejecting claims of payment. The marginal notes (السوادة) clarify that the return must involve litigation and that the seller's acknowledgment of receipt of good dirhams prevents him from later claiming the dirhams were defective. Overall, this passage reflects the Hanafi approach of balancing the interests of parties while maintaining the stability of transactions.

Key Terms
الرد بالعيب (al-radd bi al-'ayb): The act of returning a purchased item to the seller due to a latent defect discovered after purchase.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of the contract, annulling it from the time of the rescission forward (prospective), not retroactive.
قضاء (qada): A court order or judicial decree; in this context, the ruling of a judge effecting the return.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for the diminution in value due to a defect, paid by the seller to the buyer.
صرف (sarf): Currency exchange (sale of gold or silver for like or different denominations); treated specially because the items are not specific in contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal effect of returning a defective item by mutual consent (رضا) in the Hanafi school?
A) It is a rescission (فسخ) of the original contract, making it void ab initio.
B) It is considered a new sale (بيع جديد) between the parties.
C) It has the same legal effect as return by court order.
D) It is invalid unless approved by a judge.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a court-ordered return rescinds the contract only for the future, while a consent-based return creates a new sale, except in currency exchange where any return is rescission.

Cancelling the Term of a Debt by a Void Condition
Ruling on void conditions and term cancellation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a void condition nullifies the cancellation of a debt term and distinguish a valid acceleration condition from a void one.

Core Idea
A void condition invalidates the cancellation of a debt's term; a valid condition can accelerate the debt.

This lesson examines a critical distinction in Hanafi muamalat: the effect of conditions on deferred debts. You might think any condition on a debt is invalid, but the Hanafi school distinguishes between void conditions (shart fāsid) that nullify term cancellations and valid conditions that legitimately accelerate payment upon default. The passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq explains this rule and provides an example from al-Khulasa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings in the passage
First, the passage states the rule: cancelling a term by a void condition is itself invalid. Second, it gives an example: a condition that makes the debt due immediately upon default is valid. Identify both.
The void condition invalidates the cancellation; the valid condition accelerates the debt.
2
Distinguish void vs valid conditions
A void condition (shart fāsid) is one that contradicts the contract's purpose or leads to ribā/gharar. A valid condition, such as acceleration upon non-payment, does not violate Shari'ah principles.
The example in the text: 'whenever an instalment falls due and you do not pay, the debt is due immediately' is valid.
3
Apply the rule to the guarantor (kafīl)
The passage notes that if a debt had a guarantor and the term was cancelled by a void condition, the guarantee does not revert (i.e., the guarantor remains liable as per the original term). This shows the cancellation is null.
This reinforces that the void condition does not actually cancel the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حالاً‌ منفعةً‌ وجد بالمبيع عيباً فرده بقضاء عاد الأجل، ولو كان بهذا الدين المؤجل كفيل لا تعود الكفالة في الوجهين. وفي الخلاصة: وإبطال الأجل يبطل بالشرط الفاسد، ولو قال كلما دخل نجم ولم يؤد فالمال حال صح والمال يصير حالاً.
Immediately, a benefit. If a defect is found in the sold item and he returns it by court order, the term returns. And if there was a guarantor for this deferred debt, the guarantee does not return in both aspects. And in al-Khulasa: Cancelling the term is invalidated by a void condition. And if he said: whenever a star (instalment) enters and he does not pay, then the wealth is due immediately, it is valid and the wealth becomes due immediately.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage from the sharh discusses the effect of a void condition on the term of a debt. The base ruling: if a buyer returns a defective item via a judge's ruling, any previously cancelled term is reinstated. Similarly, if a deferred debt had a guarantor and the term was cancelled, the guarantor's liability does not revert. The author then cites al-Khulasa: a void condition renders null any purported cancellation of the term. For example, if the debtor says 'Whenever an instalment falls due and I do not pay, the entire debt becomes due immediately,' this condition is valid and indeed the debt becomes due upon non-payment. This is because the condition is not fāsid—it is a valid stipulation of immediate acceleration upon default. The passage guards against the misunderstanding that any condition on an existing debt is automatically void; here the condition is valid because it does not entail ribā or gharar.

Key Terms
إبطال الأجل (Ibṭāl al-ajal): Cancelling the deferred payment term of a debt, making it immediately due.
شرط فاسد (Sharṭ fāsid): A condition that is invalid according to the Shari'ah, often because it leads to ribā or gharar.
كفيل (Kafīl): A guarantor who is liable for the debt if the debtor fails to pay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a void condition is attached to the cancellation of a debt term?
A) The cancellation is valid and the term ends.
B) The cancellation is invalid and the term remains as before.
C) The debt becomes immediately due.
D) The condition is ignored and the term is unchanged.

Key Takeaway
A void condition nullifies the cancellation of a debt term, while a valid condition can accelerate payment without involving ribā.

Excusable Contradiction in Lineage Claims
Ruling on excused contradiction in lineage claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that contradictions in lineage are excused, identify its scope (asl/far' only), and apply it to distinguish between when a contradictory claim is heard and when it is not.

Core Idea
Contradiction in lineage is excused because lineage is hidden; but only for parent-child and grandparent-grandchild relationships.

This lesson explores a unique Hanafi principle: a person can make contradictory statements about lineage and be believed. This is not a loophole but a recognition that lineage is often unknown to the claimant. We will examine the core example, the reasoning, and the boundaries of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle and Core Example
Read the passage carefully. The principle is that contradiction in lineage is excused. The core example: if someone sells a slave, then later claims the slave is his son, the claim is heard and the sales are invalidated.
The author follows al-Ayni's illustration from Sharh al-Kanz.
2
Analyze the Rationale
Why is lineage given this leniency? Because lineage is based on conception (uluq), a hidden event that may not be known at the time of the earlier statement. Therefore, a later contradictory statement can be true.
The hiddenness of conception makes the initial denial excusable.
3
Determine the Scope of the Rule
The principle applies only to 'roots and branches' (asl and far') — i.e., parent-child, grandparent-grandchild relationships. It does NOT extend to siblings, cousins, or other relations.
The author explicitly states: 'it appears that lineage in the author's words is specific to roots and branches.'
4
Contrast with Other Relationships
For brotherhood, the rule is stricter. If someone denies brotherhood when asked for maintenance, then later claims it for inheritance, the claim is not heard unless he had previously claimed a right. A bare claim of brotherhood is not accepted because it effectively asserts lineage against the father, who is absent.
The passage extracts detailed rules from Al-Bazzaziyyah on this point.
5
Apply to Detailed Scenarios
The passage lists exceptions and comparisons: claims of father/son, husband/wife, master/slave are accepted even without a property right. Also, a fatwa is cited: if someone first denies being a son, then later claims to be a son, the claim is heard.
These examples reinforce that the rule is generous for lineal relationships but strict for other kin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التناقض المعفو في النسب فصورته : لو باع عبداً ولو عنده وباعه المشتري ، آخر ثم ادعاه البائع الأول أنه ابنه فتسمع دعواه ويبطل الشراء الأول والثاني لأن النسب ينبني على العلوق فيخفى فيعذر في التناقض ; هكذا صوره العيني في شرح الكنز، وظاهره أن النسب في كلام المصنف خاص بالأصول والفروع، وأما تناقض ما عداهم فإنه يمنع من أنه إذا أنكر أخوته عند طلب الانفاق عليه فمات فادعى بعده أنه أخوه طالباً ميراثه لم تسمع ورجوعه إلى التناقض في دعوى الملك لكونه لا يصح الدعوى بأنه أخوه إلا إذا ادعى حقاً ولذا قال في البزازية من العاشر في النسب والإرث من كتاب الدعوى : ادعى على آخر أنه أخوه لأبويه إن ادعى إرثاً أو نفقة وبرهن تقبل ويكون قضاء على الغائب أيضاً حتى لو حضر الأب وأنكر لا تقبل ولا يحتاج إلى إعادة البينة لأنه لا يتوصل إليه إلا بإثبات الحق على الغائب، وإن لم يدع مالاً بل ادعى الأخوة المجردة لا تقبل لأن هذا في الحقيقة إثبات البنوة على أبي المدعى عليه والخصم فيه هو الأب لا الأخ، وكذا لو ادعى أنه ابن ابنه أو أبو أبيه والابن والأب غائب أو ميت لا يصح ما لم يدع مالاً فإن ادعى مالاً فالحكم على الحاضر والغائب جميعاً كما مر بخلاف ما إذا ادعى على رجل أنه أبوه أو ابنه، أو على امرأة أنها زوجته، أو ادعت عليه أنه زوجها أو ادعى العبد على عربي أنه مولاه عتاقة، أو ادعى عربي على آخر أنه معتقة، أو ادعت على رجل أنها أمته أو كان الدعوى في ولاء الموالاة وأنكره المدعى عليه فبرهن المدعي على ما قاله تقبل ادعى به حقاً أو لا، بخلاف دعوى الأخوة لأنه دعوى الغير ألا ترى أنه لو أقر أبوه أو ابنه أو زوجه أو زوجته صح، أو بأنه أخوه لا لكونه حمل النسب على الغير وتمامه فيها. ولو قال هذا الولد ليس مني ثم تلاعنا ثم قال لخفاء العلوق فاندفع ما لو قال هذه الدار ليست لي ثم ادعاها كما مر; كذا فيها أيضاً. وفي جامع الفصولين : قال لست وارثاً ثم ادعى أنه وارثه وبين الجهة تسمع لأن التناقض في النسب معفو عنه ا هـ . وعلى هذا أفتيت فيمن أقر أنه ليس ابن فلان ثم ادعى أنه ابنه أنها تسمع
As for the excused contradiction in lineage, its form is: if he sold a slave while he had him, and the buyer sold him to another, then the first seller claimed that he is his son, his claim is heard and the first and second sales are invalidated because lineage is based on conception, so it is hidden, and contradiction is excused. Thus al-Ayni illustrated it in Sharh al-Kanz. It appears that lineage in the author's words is specific to roots and branches. As for contradiction regarding others, it prevents: if he denied his brotherhood when asked for maintenance, then died and afterwards claimed he is his brother seeking inheritance, it is not heard, and his return is a contradiction in claim of ownership because his claim of brotherhood is not valid unless he claims a right. For this reason it is stated in al-Bazzaziyyah, the tenth chapter on lineage and inheritance from the book of claims: if he claims against another that he is his full brother, if he claims inheritance or maintenance and provides proof, it is accepted and the judgment applies also to the absentee, even if the father appears and denies, it is not accepted and no need to repeat the evidence because it cannot be achieved except by establishing the right against the absentee. If he does not claim property but merely claims brotherhood, it is not accepted because this is in reality establishing sonship against the father of the defendant, and the opponent is the father, not the brother. Similarly, if he claims that he is his son's son or his father's father, and the son or father is absent or dead, it is not valid unless he claims property; if he claims property, the judgment is against both the present and the absent as previously. Contrary to if he claims against a man that he is his father or his son, or against a woman that she is his wife, or she claims against him that he is her husband, or a slave claims against an Arab that he is his master by manumission, or an Arab claims against another that he is his manumitted slave, or a woman claims against a man that she is his slave, or the claim is about wala al-muwalah and the defendant denies, then the claimant provides proof on what he said, it is accepted whether he claims a right or not. Different from the claim of brotherhood because it is a claim for another; do you not see that if his father or son or husband or wife confesses, it is valid, but if he confesses that he is his brother, it is not because it attributes lineage to another. And its completion is there. If he says 'this child is not from me' then they mutually curse, then he says 'due to hiddenness of conception', then it is repelled as if he said 'this house is not mine' then claimed it as previously; likewise there. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: he said 'I am not an heir' then claimed that he is his heir and clarifies the basis, it is heard because contradiction in lineage is excused. End quote. And upon this I gave a fatwa: if someone confessed he is not the son of so-and-so then claimed he is his son, it is heard.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage discusses the principle of excused contradiction in matters of lineage (nasab) according to the Hanafi school. The author explains that when a person makes contradictory statements about lineage, the later claim may still be accepted because lineage is hidden—often unknown to the person making the claim. The primary example: if someone sells a slave, and the buyer resells him, and then the original seller claims the slave is his son, the claim is heard and both sales are invalidated. This is because the seller may not have known the relationship at the time of sale. The author clarifies that this principle applies specifically to lineage of ancestors and descendants (asl and far'). For other relationships like brotherhood, the rule is stricter: if someone denies brotherhood when sought for maintenance, then after death claims brotherhood for inheritance, the claim is not heard unless he had previously claimed a right. The text then extracts detailed rules from Al-Bazzaziyyah regarding claims of brotherhood: if the claimant seeks inheritance or maintenance and provides proof, the claim is accepted and binding even on absent parties. However, a bare claim of brotherhood without a financial right is not accepted, as it effectively establishes lineage against the father who is not present. The passage also notes that contradictions in other matters (like property) are not excused. The author cites a fatwa that if someone first denies being someone's son, then later claims to be his son, the claim is heard because of the excusable nature of lineage.

Key Terms
نسب (nasab): Lineage, specifically parent-child and grandparent-grandchild relationships.
تناقض (tanaqud): Contradiction in statements or claims by the same person.
علوق (uluq): Conception; the hidden event of becoming pregnant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because lineage is based on conception which is hidden
B) Because people often lie about lineage
C) Because lineage is not important in Islamic law
D) Because the buyer always accepts the seller's claim

Key Takeaway
Contradiction in lineage claims is excused in Hanafi law, but only for direct lineal relationships (parents, children, grandparents, grandchildren) and subject to certain conditions for other kin.

The Principle of Excused Contradiction in Hidden Matters
Extension of excused contradiction to property, lease, agency, and usurpation cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of excused contradiction in claims when the underlying facts were hidden from the claimant at the time of the earlier statement.

Core Idea
Contradiction is excused when hiddenness prevents knowledge.

This lesson expands the principle previously limited to lineage to property, lease, agency, and other claims. The key insight is that a claimant who later discovers hidden facts may advance a claim that contradicts an earlier statement made in ignorance. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents numerous examples and the underlying rationale: what is built on hiddenness (al-khafa') is excused for contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradictory statements
In each case, note the two assertions the claimant makes: an earlier action or statement (e.g., leasing a house) and a later claim (e.g., ownership).
Contradiction is only apparent; the earlier action may not have been based on full knowledge.
2
Assess the claimant's knowledge at the time of the first act
Determine whether the claimant plausibly could have known the truth when acting. If the facts were hidden (e.g., father's secret purchase for son, item wrapped in cloth), ignorance is likely.
Hiddenness can be due to third-party actions (father, seller) or physical concealment.
3
Apply the excusable contradiction rule
If hiddenness exists, the later claim is accepted despite the contradiction. The earlier act does not constitute a binding admission.
The principle is rooted in istihsan: strict logic would cause injustice.
4
Distinguish cases where knowledge was possible
If the item could have been known at the time (e.g., a veiled but visible slave girl), the claim is not accepted unless the opponent confirms ignorance. Imam Muhammad's baseline: if the item is knowable, contradiction is not excused.
Knowability depends on custom and physical possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الذخيرة: ادعاه مطلقاً فدفعه المدعى عليه بأنك كنت ادعيته قبل هذا مقيد أو برهن عليه فقال المدعي ادعيته الآن بذلك . وفيها قوله : (اعلم أن المتناقض الخ قال في النهر : وفي هذا الاستخراج تأمل فتدبره اهـ. As for divorce, al-Ayni illustrated it as follows: if a woman obtained khula' from her husband, then she brought evidence that he had divorced her three times before the khula', then her evidence is accepted, and she may reclaim the khula' compensation, even though she is contradictory, because the husband independently pronounced triple divorce upon her without her knowledge. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: she claimed divorce and he denied, then he died, she does not have the right to claim inheritance. End quote. The purpose is not to limit what is excused for contradiction, but rather that whatever is based on hiddenness is excused for contradiction. Among that is what is in al-Zuhayriyyah: he bought the money for him, then the guarantor claimed against the debtor that he was guarantor by his order and proved it; it is accepted in our school, and he may claim from the guaranteed what he guaranteed because he has become legally contradicted by the judgment. End quote. [PAGE_239] The Book of Sales, Chapter of Establisment. Then the father bought a house for his minor son from himself and had witnesses to that; then the son grew up and did not know what his father did; the father sold to a man and handed it over to him; then the son leased the house from the buyer; then he learned what his father did, so he claimed the house against the buyer and said: 'My father bought this house for me from himself when I was minor, and it is my property,' and he presented evidence. The defendant said in defense: 'You are contradictory in this claim because your leasing this house from me is an admission that the house is not yours, so your claim of the house afterwards is a contradiction.' He said: The correct view is that this does not serve as a defense against the plaintiff's claim, even if it is a contradiction, because this contradiction does not prevent the validity of the claim, because there is hiddenness: the father independently buys for the minor, and from the minor for himself, and the son has no knowledge of that. End quote. And in al-Bazzaziyyah, citing al-Sughra: he bought a cloth in a wrapper, then claimed he did not know it; he said: it is accepted. And in al-Dhakhirah: it was said it is not accepted in all such questions. And in al-Uyun: he arrived in a town and bought or leased a house, then claimed it saying it was his father's house that died and left as inheritance to him, and he did not know it at the time of the lease; it is not accepted. He said: acceptance is more correct. And in al-Munyah: two divided an estate, then one of them claimed that his father had designated for him a specific item that was part of the division; if he said it was while he was minor, it is accepted; if absolute, no. Al-Witar mentioned: he took over administration of a waqf or guardianship of an estate after it became clear to be an estate, or divided an estate among heirs, then claimed it for himself, it is not heard. He bought a slave girl in a veil and then claimed her, claiming he did not know her, it is not accepted. And if he bought a cloth in a wrapper then claimed it is his, it is not accepted. Muhammad said: look at that item: if it is something that could be known at the time of bargaining, like a standing veiled slave girl in front of him, it is not accepted unless the defendant confirms his ignorance of her, then it is accepted; if it is something that cannot be known, like a cloth in a wrapper or a sitting slave girl with a cover on her head from which nothing can be seen, it is accepted. Because of this difference, there are different opinions of scholars on acceptance or not in these questions. End quote. Also: he leased an animal from another, then claimed it was his, bought for him by his father when minor, and provided proof; it is accepted because contradiction is excused in matters involving hiddenness, as the father alone buys for the son and from the son. End quote. Among what contradiction is excused is what is in al-Bazzaziyyah: the owner claimed the value of the item from the usurper for its destruction, then claimed it is still existent and provided proof; it is accepted because it is a place of hiddenness. End quote. Then know that the contradictory person whose claim is not heard, if he says 'I abandon one of the two statements', it is accepted from him. It is stated in al-Bazzaziyyah citing al-Dhakhirah: he claimed absolutely, then the defendant repelled saying 'you previously claimed it with a restriction' or proved it, then the plaintiff said 'I now claim it in that way'. And there: his statement: 'Know that the contradictory...' Al-Nahr said: there is contemplation in this extraction, so ponder it. End quote. Because the absolute claim does not contradict the restricted claim initially; so ponder and look at what we will mention from al-Ramli in the scattered parts of judiciary at his statement: he claimed a house in the hand of a man, but there it is mentioned from al-Bazzaziyyah: he claimed ownership absolutely, then claimed before the judge with a reason; it is accepted and his proof is heard; contrary to the reverse unless the reverser says he meant by the second absolute the first restricted.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage extends the principle of excusable contradiction to matters beyond lineage, specifically where knowledge is hidden from the claimant. The author begins with the example of divorce (talaq): if a woman obtains khula' (divorce with compensation) from her husband, then later proves that he had divorced her three times before the khula', her evidence is accepted and she can reclaim the compensation. This is because the husband pronounces divorce independently, and the woman may not have known. Next, the passage addresses various property claims. A key case involves a father who buys a house for his minor son, then later sells it. The son, unaware, later leases the same house from the buyer. When the son discovers the truth, he claims the house. Despite having previously leased it (which implies the house belongs to the lessor), his claim is accepted because the father's actions were hidden from him. Similarly, if someone buys a good in a wrapper (like a cloth) and later claims it as his own, his claim may be accepted depending on whether the item could have been identified at the time of purchase. Imam Muhammad distinguishes: if the item could have been known (like a visible slave girl), the claim is not accepted unless the defendant confirms ignorance; if the item could not have been known (like a cloth in a wrapper), the claim is accepted. The passage also covers cases of division of estate, agency, and usurpation. In each case, the common thread is that the contradiction arises from a situation where the claimant could have been genuinely ignorant due to the hidden nature of the facts. The author concludes with a general rule: even if a person's claim is dismissed due to contradiction, if he abandons one of his contradictory statements, the remaining statement may be accepted. This is further refined by later commentators like al-Ramli.

Key Terms
خلع (khul'): A divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation.
كفيل (kafil): A guarantor who takes responsibility for a debt or obligation of another.
غصب (ghasb): Unlawful usurpation of property; the usurper (ghasib) is liable for return or compensation.
تناقض معفو (tanaqud ma'fu): A contradiction in a claimant's statements that is legally excused due to hiddenness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a son's claim to a house accepted even though he previously leased it from the buyer?
A) Because leasing is not an admission of ownership
B) Because the son was minor at the time of leasing
C) Because the father's purchase and sale were hidden from the son
D) Because the buyer committed fraud

Key Takeaway
Contradictory claims are admissible when the claimant's earlier statement was based on incomplete knowledge due to hidden circumstances.

The Requirement of a Fixed Time in Salam and Dual Contract Rulings
Explaining the necessity of a set time in salam and permissibility of dual rulings in one contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why a fixed time (ajal) is a condition for the validity of a salam contract, explain why khiyar al-shart is prohibited, and identify scenarios where one contract can have dual rulings by analogizing to the examples given in the text.

Core Idea
A salam contract without a fixed time is invalid; option to cancel is prohibited because it prevents complete receipt.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the necessity of setting a time (ajal) in salam contracts. Without it, the contract is invalid because the commodity is only owed in the dhimma through salam, making time a condition. The text also addresses a nuanced case where two subject matters (a garment and a slave) are involved in one transaction, illustrating how a single contract can combine the rulings of two different types of contracts. Furthermore, it explains why khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) is not allowed in salam and discusses the applicability of khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection) and khiyar al-ayb (option of defect).

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for salam validity
The first sentence states that if no time is set for the garment, it is invalid. So the condition is a fixed time (ajal).
This condition distinguishes salam from an ordinary sale.
2
Understand the rationale for the condition
The garment is owed in the dhimma only through salam, so time is a condition. This is because the deferred commodity is not yet in existence and must be guaranteed by a known future date.
3
Analyze the dual rulings scenario
The contract involves a garment (salam) and a slave (sale). Their separation before receiving the slave does not invalidate the contract because it is treated as a sale regarding the slave. This is an example of one contract having two rulings, analogized to gift with compensation and conditional manumission.
The analogy is from established cases in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Examine the options (khiyar) that apply or not
The author states that khiyar al-shart is not allowed because it prevents complete receipt (qabd). Khiyar al-ru'ya is not established in the muslam fihi, but khiyar al-ayb is. For the capital (ra's al-mal), both options apply if it is a determinate good, but not for currency.
Understanding each option's scope is crucial for contract drafting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يضرب للثوب أجلاً لا يجوز لأن الثوب لا يجب في الذمة إلا سلماً فالأجل شرط فلو ضرب الأجل جاز لوجود شرطه فلو افترقا قبل قبض العبد لا يبطل العقد لأن هذا العقد في حق الثوب بيعاً في حق العبد، ويجوز أن يعتبر في عقد واحد حكم عقدين كما في الهبة بشرط العوض وكما في قول المولى لعبده إذا أديت إلي ألفاً فأنت حر، اعتبر فيه حكم اليمين وحكم المعاوضة .اهـ. وأشار المصنف رحمه الله إلى أنه لا يدخله خيار الشرط لأنه يمنع تمام القبض قالوا ولا يثبت في المسلم فيه خيار رؤية ويثبت فيه خيار العيب، ويثبتان في رأس المال إذا كان مما يتعين وإلا فخيار الرؤية لا يثبت في النقود.
If he did not set a time for the garment, it is not valid, because the garment is only owed in the dhimma as salam, so the time is a condition. If he set the time, it is valid due to the existence of its condition. If they separate before receiving the slave, the contract is not invalidated, because this contract with respect to the garment is a sale with respect to the slave. And it is permissible to consider in a single contract the rulings of two contracts, as in a gift with a condition of compensation, and as in the master's statement to his slave: 'If you pay me a thousand, you are free,' in which the ruling of an oath and the ruling of a transaction are considered. End. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated that option of a condition does not enter it because it prevents complete receipt. They said: The option of inspection is not established in the muslam fihi, and the option of defect is established in it, and both are established in the capital if it is determinate; otherwise, the option of inspection is not established in currency.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the requirement of a fixed time (ajal) in salam contracts. The author states that if no time is set for delivery of the garment, the salam is invalid because a time is a condition for validity. If a time is set, it becomes valid. The text then addresses a unique scenario where the salam involves both a garment and a slave: separation before receiving the slave does not invalidate the contract because it is treated as a sale regarding the slave. This illustrates the principle that one contract can have dual rulings, analogous to a gift with compensation (hiba bi shart al-iwad) and the master's conditional manumission, which combines elements of an oath and a transaction. The author also notes that khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation) does not enter salam because it prevents complete delivery. Furthermore, khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection) is not valid for the muslam fihi (the deferred commodity) but khiyar al-ayb (option of defect) is. For the capital (ra's al-mal), both options apply if it is a determinate good, but not for currency.

Key Terms
الأجل (ajal): The fixed time period for delivery of the muslam fihi in a salam contract.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The option to cancel the contract within a stipulated period.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option to reject a good upon inspection after purchase unseen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following options is NOT established in the muslam fihi (the deferred commodity) in a salam contract?
A) Khiyar al-ayb (option of defect)
B) Khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation)
C) Khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection)
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
A fixed time is a condition for a valid salam contract, and option to cancel (khiyar al-shart) is prohibited because it undermines complete receipt.

Ownership of Game on Private Land
Commentary on a matn ruling with proof and expansions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership of game found on private land and apply the two conditions that determine whether the landowner or the taker owns the game.

Core Idea
Game belongs to the taker, not the landowner, unless the land is prepared for hunting or the owner is within arm's reach.

In this lesson, we examine a key Hanafi principle regarding the ownership of unowned (mubah) property found on someone else's land. The source text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that game such as birds or deer that appear on private land belong to the person who actually takes them, not automatically to the landowner. However, two critical conditions can shift ownership to the landowner. We will dissect the proof from the hadith and work through numerous subsidiary cases to see how intent (qasd) and proximity affect possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling and its proof
Read the opening statement: game belongs to the taker. Note the hadith cited as proof: 'الصيد لمن أخذه'. Understand that eggs are analogized to game.
The matn is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq.
2
Locate the two conditions
The commentary immediately qualifies the ruling with two conditions: (1) the land is not prepared for hunting, (2) the landowner is not close enough to take the game. If either condition is met, the landowner owns the game.
Condition 1 relates to intent; Condition 2 relates to proximity.
3
Analyze the role of intent
Examine the examples: setting a net for drying vs. hunting, digging a well for water vs. hunting. The ruling turns on the intention (qasd) of the person. Intent to hunt makes the taker the owner; absence of intent leaves the game unowned until someone else takes it.
Intent is the effective cause (illa) in the qiyas.
4
Apply to subsidiary cases
Work through the cases: fish in an enclosure, wool on a roof, game caught in nets that escape, dogs chasing game into another's property. For each, determine who owns based on intent and proximity.
These illustrate the principle across various scenarios.
5
Distinguish between valid and invalid takings
Note the difference between a net owner whose catch escapes before confirmation and after confirmation. The ruling changes depending on whether seizure (ihraz) was firmly established.
This shows the importance of tahqiq al-manat (verification of the cause) in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text

قصد به الاصطياد ملكه ووجب عليه الجزاء إن كان محرماً. وعلى هذا التفصيل لو دخل صيد داره أو وقع ما نثر من الدراهم في ثيابه بخلاف معسل النحل في أرضه حيث يملكه وإن لم تكن أرضه معدة لذلك لأنه من إنزال الأرض حتى يملكه تبعاً لها كالأشجار النابتة والتراب المجتمع فيها بجريان الماء وإن لم تكن معدة ولهذا يجب في العسل العشر إذا أخذه من أرض العشر. الثاني في الذخيرة من كتاب الصيد : وهذا إذا كان صاحب الأرض بعيداً بأرضه ا هـ. ومثله في شرح الطحاوي. وقوله تكنس ظبي أي دخل في كناسه وهو بالكسر بيته، وكنس الظبي كنوساً من باب نزل دخل كناسه ؛ كذا في المصباح. ولم يذكر تكنس. وفي المغرب : كنس الظبي دخل في الكناس كنوساً من بالتلف ليعم حكم ما إذا أنفقها بالأولى قال في النهر فيه نظر. قوله: (من باب طلب) قال الرملي صوابه من باب جلس. قوله ويحترز به عما لو كسره رجل) إنما يتم ... ا هـ . . ثم قال : ومن جنس هذه المسائل لو اتخذ في أرضه حظيرة فدخل الماء والسمك ملكه ولو اتخذت الحاجة أخرى فمن أخذ السمك فهو له، وكذا في حفر الحفيرة إن حفرها للصيد فهو له أو لغرض آخر فهو للآخذ، وكذا صوف وضع على سطح بيت فابتل بالمطر فعصره رجل، فإن كان وضعه للماء فهو لصاحبه وإلا فالماء للآخذ وفي الذخيرة : إن أغلق الباب على الصيد ولم يعلم به لم يصر آخذاً مالكاً له حتى لو خرج الصيد بعد ذلك فأخذه غيره .ملكه. وفي المنتفى : رجل نصب حباله فوقع فيها صيد فاضطرب وقطعها وانفلت فجاء آخر وأخذ الصيد فالصيد للآخذ ولو جاء صاحب الحبالة ليأخده فلما دنى منه بحيث يقدر على أخذه فاضطرب وانفلت فأخذه آخر فهو لصاحب الحبالة. والفرق أن فيهما صاحب الحبالة وإن صار آخذاً له إلا أنه في الأول بطل الأخذ قبل تأكده، وفي الثاني بطل بعد تأكده وكذا صيد البازي والكلب إذا انفلت فهو على هذا التفصيل. وفي الأصل : إذا رمى صيداً فصرعه فاشتد رجل وأخذه فهو لمن رماه لأنه لما رماه صار آخذاً له فصار ملكاً، ولو رمى صيداً فأصابه وأثخنه بحيث لا يستطيع براحاً فرماه آخر فقتله فالصيد للأول، وإن كان يتحامل ويطير مع ما أصابه من السهم الأول فرماه الثاني فقتله فهو للثاني. وفي الأصل أيضاً : لو أرسل كلبه على صيد فاتبعه الكلب حتى أدخله في أرض رجل أو داره كان لصاحب الكلب لأن الكلب إنما يرسل للأخذ فيعتبر بما لو أخذه بیده ، وكذا لو اشتد على صيد حتى أخرجه فأدخله دار إنسان فهو له لأنه أخرجه واضطره فقد أخذه، وعن أبي يوسف رجل اصطاد طائراً في دار رجل فإن اتفقا على أنه على أصل الإباحة فهو للصائد، وسواء كان اصطاده من الهواء أو على الشجر، لأن الصيد إنما يملك بالاستيلاء والإحراز وحصوله على حائط رجل أو شجرة ليس بإحراز فيكون للآخذ. وإن اختلفا فقال رب الدار كنت اصطدته قبلك أو ورثته وأنكر الصائد. فإن كان أخذه من الهواء فهو له لأنه لا يد لصاحب الدار على الهواء، وإن أخذه من حائطه أو شجره فالقول لصاحب الدار لأخذه من محل هو في يده، فإن اختلفا في أخده من الهواء أو من الدار أو الشجرة فالقول لصاحب الدار لأن الظاهر أن ما في دار الإنسان يكون له ا هـ .
If a bird hatches chicks or lays eggs, or a deer enters its den in a man's land, it belongs to the one who takes it. This is nullified by a corrupt condition, and there is the special opinion of Abu Yusuf as we have presented (end). In al-Dhakhira from the end of the book of currency exchange: Abu Hanifa said: There is no harm in selling adulterated [silver] if it is disclosed and it is apparent, and that is the opinion of Abu Yusuf. He said concerning a man who has silver [mixed with] copper: he should not sell it until he discloses [the impurity]. There is no harm in buying a 'bistuqa' if disclosed. I think that the sultan should break them [i.e., such coins] so that they do not fall into the hands of those who do not disclose. And Bishr narrated in al-Imla' from Abu Yusuf: I dislike for a man to give counterfeit, worn, 'bistuqa', 'mukahhala', and 'bukhariyya' [coins] even if he discloses that, but they are permissible to accept because spending them harms the common people, and whatever causes general harm is disliked but not a sin, and the consent of these two present [parties] is feared from falling into the hands of fraudsters upon the ignorant and from the merchant who does not avoid sin. He said: Everything not permissible should be cut and its owner punished if he spends it knowing (end). His statement: (And if a bird hatches chicks or lays eggs or a deer enters its den in a man's land, it belongs to the one who takes it) because it is permissible and his hand preceded to it, so he is more entitled to it due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Game belongs to the one who takes it,' and eggs are game, and therefore the pilgrim must pay compensation for breaking them. He made it absolute but it is restricted by two restrictions: First, as the commentator mentioned, that his land is not prepared for that; if it is prepared for hunting, then it is his, because the ruling is not attributed to a valid cause except by intention. Do you not see that one who sets up a net for drying and game gets caught in it, or digs a well for water and game falls into it, does not own it nor must he pay compensation if he is a pilgrim; but if he intended hunting, he owns it and must pay compensation if a pilgrim. Similarly, if game enters his house or scattered dirhams fall into his clothes, unlike bees' honey on his land, which he owns even if the land is not prepared for it, because it comes from the land such that he owns it as a subsidiary to it, like trees that grow and soil that accumulates by water flow even if not prepared, and therefore zakat of one-tenth is due on honey if taken from tithe land. Second, in al-Dhakhira from the book of hunting: This is if the landowner is far from the game such that he cannot take it if he extends his hand; but if the landowner is close to the game such that he can take it if he extends his hand, then the game belongs to the landowner because he became a taker of it constructively due to his ability to take it actually, even if he is not taking it in his land (end). Similar is in Sharh al-Tahawi. His statement 'takanasa zaby' means he entered his den (kinas), which is his home. 'Kanasa al-zabiy' from the pattern of nazala: he entered his den; thus in al-Misbah. He did not mention 'takanasa'. In al-Maghrib: 'kanasa al-zabiy' entered the den, from the pattern of talaba, and 'takanasa' is similar, and from it: 'if game takanasa in a man's land' meaning it hides, and it is narrated 'taksara' and 'inkasara' (end). In Fath al-Qadir: In some copies 'taksara' meaning it fell and broke, and it cautions against what if a man broke it therein, then it belongs to that man... (his statement: from the pattern of talaba; al-Ramli said: correct is from the pattern of jalasa. His statement: and it cautions against what if a man broke it) is only complete (end). Then he said: From the same category are these issues: if one makes an enclosure in his land and water and fish enter, he owns them; but if another makes an enclosure for a need, whoever takes the fish owns it. Similarly, digging a pit: if he dug it for hunting, it is his; otherwise it belongs to the taker. Similarly, wool placed on a roof and it becomes wet from rain, then a man squeezes it: if he placed it for [collecting] water, the water is his; otherwise the water belongs to the taker. In al-Dhakhira: If he closed the door on game and did not know about it, he does not become a taker owning it; even if the game later exits and another takes it, the other owns it. In al-Muntafa: A man set his nets and game fell into them, struggled, cut them and escaped, then another came and took the game: the game belongs to the latter. If the owner of the net came to take it, and when he approached such that he could take it, the game struggled and escaped, then another took it: it belongs to the owner of the net. The difference is that in both, the net owner became a taker, but in the first, the seizure was invalidated before it was confirmed, while in the second, after confirmation. Similarly for game of falcon and dog if they escape: it follows this detail. In al-Asl: If one shoots game and knocks it down, then another man rushes and takes it, it belongs to the shooter because when he shot, he became a taker of it, so it became his property. If one shoots game and hits it and weakens it so it cannot escape, then another shoots and kills it, the game belongs to the first. If it could barely fly despite being hit by the first arrow, then the second shoots and kills it, it belongs to the second. Also in al-Asl: If one sends his dog after game, and the dog follows it until it enters a man's land or house, it belongs to the dog's owner because the dog is sent for taking, so it is considered as if he took it with his hand. Similarly, if one chases game until it exits and enters someone's house, it belongs to him because he made it exit and forced it, so he took it. From Abu Yusuf: A man hunted a bird in another's house: if they agree that it was originally permissible [i.e., unowned], it belongs to the hunter, whether he caught it from the air or from a tree, because game is only owned by seizure and taking possession (ihraz), and its being on a wall or tree is not possession, so it belongs to the taker. If they disagree, and the house owner says 'I caught it before you' or 'I inherited it', and the hunter denies, then if he caught it from the air, it is his because the house owner has no hand over the air; if he caught it from his wall or tree, the statement is of the house owner because he took it from a place under his control. If they disagree whether he caught it from the air or from the house/tree, the statement is of the house owner because the apparent is that what is in a man's house belongs to him (end).
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the ruling on ownership of game found on someone's private property. The core ruling is: if a bird lays eggs or hatches chicks, or a deer takes shelter in its den on a man's land, the game belongs to the person who actually takes it (the catcher), not automatically to the landowner. The proof is the hadith 'Game belongs to the one who takes it', and eggs are included in game. However, the commentator immediately qualifies this absolute ruling with two conditions. First, the land must not be prepared for hunting; if it is a prepared hunting ground, the game belongs to the landowner because he intended it. The analogy is made: if someone sets a net to dry clothes and game gets caught, he does not own it; but if he set it for hunting, he does. Second, the landowner must be far from the game; if he is close enough to take it by extending his hand, he owns it constructively. The passage then provides many subsidiary cases: fish in an enclosure, wool on a roof, game caught in a net that escapes, shooting game, dogs chasing game into others' property, and disputes between house owner and hunter. The commentator also includes a separate discussion on selling adulterated coins (mixed silver and copper) where disclosure is required; this is from al-Dhakhira and al-Imla', and it is mentioned here as a related miscellaneous ruling. The overall teaching is that ownership of mubah (unowned) property like game is established by actual or constructive taking possession (ihraz), not by mere ownership of land, unless the land is specifically prepared for that purpose or the owner is within immediate reach.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): Wild animals or birds that are permissible to hunt and are initially unowned (mubah).
إحراز (ihraz): Taking possession of an unowned item, which establishes ownership.
كناس (kinas): The den or hiding place of a deer.
حظيرة (hadirah): An enclosure, often for animals or water.
حبالة (hibalah): A net or snare used for hunting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a deer enters its den (takanasa) on a man's land, who owns the deer?
A) The landowner, because the den is on his property.
B) The person who actually takes the deer, unless the landowner is prepared for hunting or is near enough to take it.
C) The first person to see the deer.
D) The government, since game is public property.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, unowned game on private land belongs to the taker, unless the land is prepared for hunting or the landowner is within immediate reach.

Selling Mixed Coins with Disclosure
Fatwa-style commentary on selling mixed coins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling adulterated coins and explain when disclosure is required or when it becomes disliked due to public harm.

Core Idea
Selling mixed coins is allowed if disclosed, but if spending them harms the public, it becomes disliked even with disclosure.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling on buying and selling coins mixed with copper or other metals. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf initially allow the sale if the buyer is informed and the mixture is apparent. However, a later narration from Abu Yusuf indicates that he disliked giving such coins in payment even with disclosure, due to the harm to the common people. Understanding this ruling requires balancing the principle of contract freedom (ibaha) with the public interest (maslaha).

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Abu Hanifa states: 'There is no harm in selling adulterated silver if it is disclosed and the impurity is apparent.' This is also the opinion of Abu Yusuf in one narration.
The ruling is based on the permissibility of trade with mutual consent and transparency.
2
Note the two opinions of Abu Yusuf
Initially, Abu Yusuf agrees with Abu Hanifa. Later, in al-Imla', he says: 'I dislike for a man to give mixed coins even if he discloses, because spending them harms the common people.' This shows a shift toward public welfare.
Abu Yusuf's later view is makruh tanzih (disliked).
3
Understand the public policy rationale
The harm is general: the common people may not recognize the mixture and could be cheated. The sultan is empowered to break such coins to prevent fraud. This demonstrates the Hanafi use of maslaha (public interest) in muamalat.
The principle: 'ما كان ضرراً عاماً فهو مكروه' (what causes general harm is disliked).

آخذاً له إلا أنه في الأول بطل الأخذ قبل تأكده، وفي الثاني بطل بعد تأكده وكذا صيد البازي والكلب إذا انفلت فهو على هذا التفصيل. وفي الأصل : إذا رمى صيداً فصرعه فاشتد رجل وأخذه فهو لمن رماه لأنه لما رماه صار آخذاً له فصار ملكاً، ولو رمى صيداً فأصابه وأثخنه بحيث لا يستطيع براحاً فرماه آخر فقتله فالصيد للأول، وإن كان يتحامل ويطير مع ما أصابه من السهم الأول فرماه الثاني فقتله فهو للثاني. وفي الأصل أيضاً : لو أرسل كلبه على صيد فاتبعه الكلب حتى أدخله في أرض رجل أو داره كان لصاحب الكلب لأن الكلب إنما يرسل للأخذ فيعتبر بما لو أخذه بیده ، وكذا لو اشتد على صيد حتى أخرجه فأدخله دار إنسان فهو له لأنه أخرجه واضطره فقد أخذه، وعن أبي يوسف رجل اصطاد طائراً في دار رجل فإن اتفقا على أنه على أصل الإباحة فهو للصائد، وسواء كان اصطاده من الهواء أو على الشجر، لأن الصيد إنما يملك بالاستيلاء والإحراز وحصوله على حائط رجل أو شجرة ليس بإحراز فيكون للآخذ. وإن اختلفا فقال رب الدار كنت اصطدته قبلك أو ورثته وأنكر الصائد. فإن كان أخذه من الهواء فهو له لأنه لا يد لصاحب الدار على الهواء، وإن أخذه من حائطه أو شجره فالقول لصاحب الدار لأخذه من محل هو في يده، فإن اختلفا في أخده من الهواء أو من الدار أو الشجرة فالقول لصاحب الدار لأن الظاهر أن ما في دار الإنسان يكون له ا هـ .
If a bird hatches chicks or lays eggs, or a deer enters its den in a man's land, it belongs to the one who takes it. This is nullified by a corrupt condition, and there is the special opinion of Abu Yusuf as we have presented (end). In al-Dhakhira from the end of the book of currency exchange: Abu Hanifa said: There is no harm in selling adulterated [silver] if it is disclosed and it is apparent, and that is the opinion of Abu Yusuf. He said concerning a man who has silver [mixed with] copper: he should not sell it until he discloses [the impurity]. There is no harm in buying a 'bistuqa' if disclosed. I think that the sultan should break them [i.e., such coins] so that they do not fall into the hands of those who do not disclose. And Bishr narrated in al-Imla' from Abu Yusuf: I dislike for a man to give counterfeit, worn, 'bistuqa', 'mukahhala', and 'bukhariyya' [coins] even if he discloses that, but they are permissible to accept because spending them harms the common people, and whatever causes general harm is disliked but not a sin, and the consent of these two present [parties] is feared from falling into the hands of fraudsters upon the ignorant and from the merchant who does not avoid sin. He said: Everything not permissible should be cut and its owner punished if he spends it knowing (end). His statement: (And if a bird hatches chicks or lays eggs or a deer enters its den in a man's land, it belongs to the one who takes it) because it is permissible and his hand preceded to it, so he is more entitled to it due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Game belongs to the one who takes it,' and eggs are game, and therefore the pilgrim must pay compensation for breaking them. He made it absolute but it is restricted by two restrictions: First, as the commentator mentioned, that his land is not prepared for that; if it is prepared for hunting, then it is his, because the ruling is not attributed to a valid cause except by intention. Do you not see that one who sets up a net for drying and game gets caught in it, or digs a well for water and game falls into it, does not own it nor must he pay compensation if he is a pilgrim; but if he intended hunting, he owns it and must pay compensation if a pilgrim. Similarly, if game enters his house or scattered dirhams fall into his clothes, unlike bees' honey on his land, which he owns even if the land is not prepared for it, because it comes from the land such that he owns it as a subsidiary to it, like trees that grow and soil that accumulates by water flow even if not prepared, and therefore zakat of one-tenth is due on honey if taken from tithe land. Second, in al-Dhakhira from the book of hunting: This is if the landowner is far from the game such that he cannot take it if he extends his hand; but if the landowner is close to the game such that he can take it if he extends his hand, then the game belongs to the landowner because he became a taker of it constructively due to his ability to take it actually, even if he is not taking it in his land (end). Similar is in Sharh al-Tahawi. His statement 'takanasa zaby' means he entered his den (kinas), which is his home. 'Kanasa al-zabiy' from the pattern of nazala: he entered his den; thus in al-Misbah. He did not mention 'takanasa'. In al-Maghrib: 'kanasa al-zabiy' entered the den, from the pattern of talaba, and 'takanasa' is similar, and from it: 'if game takanasa in a man's land' meaning it hides, and it is narrated 'taksara' and 'inkasara' (end). In Fath al-Qadir: In some copies 'taksara' meaning it fell and broke, and it cautions against what if a man broke it therein, then it belongs to that man... (his statement: from the pattern of talaba; al-Ramli said: correct is from the pattern of jalasa. His statement: and it cautions against what if a man broke it) is only complete (end). Then he said: From the same category are these issues: if one makes an enclosure in his land and water and fish enter, he owns them; but if another makes an enclosure for a need, whoever takes the fish owns it. Similarly, digging a pit: if he dug it for hunting, it is his; otherwise it belongs to the taker. Similarly, wool placed on a roof and it becomes wet from rain, then a man squeezes it: if he placed it for [collecting] water, the water is his; otherwise the water belongs to the taker. In al-Dhakhira: If he closed the door on game and did not know about it, he does not become a taker owning it; even if the game later exits and another takes it, the other owns it. In al-Muntafa: A man set his nets and game fell into them, struggled, cut them and escaped, then another came and took the game: the game belongs to the latter. If the owner of the net came to take it, and when he approached such that he could take it, the game struggled and escaped, then another took it: it belongs to the owner of the net. The difference is that in both, the net owner became a taker, but in the first, the seizure was invalidated before it was confirmed, while in the second, after confirmation. Similarly for game of falcon and dog if they escape: it follows this detail. In al-Asl: If one shoots game and knocks it down, then another man rushes and takes it, it belongs to the shooter because when he shot, he became a taker of it, so it became his property. If one shoots game and hits it and weakens it so it cannot escape, then another shoots and kills it, the game belongs to the first. If it could barely fly despite being hit by the first arrow, then the second shoots and kills it, it belongs to the second. Also in al-Asl: If one sends his dog after game, and the dog follows it until it enters a man's land or house, it belongs to the dog's owner because the dog is sent for taking, so it is considered as if he took it with his hand. Similarly, if one chases game until it exits and enters someone's house, it belongs to him because he made it exit and forced it, so he took it. From Abu Yusuf: A man hunted a bird in another's house: if they agree that it was originally permissible [i.e., unowned], it belongs to the hunter, whether he caught it from the air or from a tree, because game is only owned by seizure and taking possession (ihraz), and its being on a wall or tree is not possession, so it belongs to the taker. If they disagree, and the house owner says 'I caught it before you' or 'I inherited it', and the hunter denies, then if he caught it from the air, it is his because the house owner has no hand over the air; if he caught it from his wall or tree, the statement is of the house owner because he took it from a place under his control. If they disagree whether he caught it from the air or from the house/tree, the statement is of the house owner because the apparent is that what is in a man's house belongs to him (end).
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It addresses the ruling on ownership of game found on someone's private property. The core ruling is: if a bird lays eggs or hatches chicks, or a deer takes shelter in its den on a man's land, the game belongs to the person who actually takes it (the catcher), not automatically to the landowner. The proof is the hadith 'Game belongs to the one who takes it', and eggs are included in game. However, the commentator immediately qualifies this absolute ruling with two conditions. First, the land must not be prepared for hunting; if it is a prepared hunting ground, the game belongs to the landowner because he intended it. The analogy is made: if someone sets a net to dry clothes and game gets caught, he does not own it; but if he set it for hunting, he does. Second, the landowner must be far from the game; if he is close enough to take it by extending his hand, he owns it constructively. The passage then provides many subsidiary cases: fish in an enclosure, wool on a roof, game caught in a net that escapes, shooting game, dogs chasing game into others' property, and disputes between house owner and hunter. The commentator also includes a separate discussion on selling adulterated coins (mixed silver and copper) where disclosure is required; this is from al-Dhakhira and al-Imla', and it is mentioned here as a related miscellaneous ruling. The overall teaching is that ownership of mubah (unowned) property like game is established by actual or constructive taking possession (ihraz), not by mere ownership of land, unless the land is specifically prepared for that purpose or the owner is within immediate reach.

Key Terms
مغشوش (maghshush): Adulterated or mixed with impurities, especially coins mixed with base metals.
زيوف (zuyuf): Counterfeit coins.
نبهرجة (nabharajah): Worn or counterfeit coins, possibly a corruption of 'nabharaj'.
ستوقة (bistuqa): A type of coin, possibly from 'bistuq' (a weight).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what did Abu Yusuf say about giving mixed coins in payment even if disclosed?
A) It is permissible and recommended.
B) It is disliked (akrah) because of general harm.
C) It is haram and punishable.
D) It is only allowed if the sultan permits.

Key Takeaway
Selling mixed coins is allowed with disclosure, but Abu Yusuf's later view considers it disliked to prevent public harm, showing the balance between contractual freedom and maslaha.
