Sound and Mixed Dirhams: Quantity Over Quality in Same-Genus Exchange
Ruling on exchanging sound and mixed dirhams with equal quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on exchanging dirhams of different quality but equal quantity, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on karaha.

Core Idea
Equal quantity in same-genus exchange overrides quality differences.

When exchanging silver for silver, is it valid if the coins are of different purity but the same count? The Hanafi school says yes, as long as weight or number matches. The leading imams, however, differed on whether such a transaction is disliked. This lesson unpacks that dispute and extends the principle to related cases like buying silver dust.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Exchange Transaction
The passage describes a specific exchange: one sound dirham and two mixed dirhams (total three) for two sound dirhams and one mixed dirham (also three). Both sides have equal quantity (number) but different composition (sound vs. mixed).
Sound dirhams are pure silver; mixed (ghalla) are worn or alloyed.
2
Apply the Principle of Genus Unity
Since both sides are silver, the genus is the same. Hanafi fiqh requires equality in quantity (weight or count) for same-genus exchanges to avoid riba. Quality is irrelevant. Thus, the sale is valid.
The principle is 'al-mithlu bi'l-mithli' – like for like.
3
Navigate the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
While the sale is valid, Imam Muhammad considered it disliked (makruh tanzihi) if the value difference is not compensated by an extra item. Abu Hanifa saw no harm. The relied‑upon view is Abu Hanifa's.
The dislike is preventive, to avoid habitual use of stratagems.
4
Extend to the Silver Dust Case
The passage then applies the same logic to buying silver dust. If the dust may contain no real silver, the sale is prohibited (gharar). If bought with gold dust (different genus), it is permissible with a right of option.
Dust of the same genus creates uncertainty; different genus removes the riba concern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ودرهم صحيح ودرهمين غلة بدرهمين صحيحين ودرهم غلة) أي يصح بيع الاتحاد في الجنس فيعتبر التساوي في القدر دون الوصف. والغلة المقطعة، وقيل ما يرده بيت المال ويأخذه التجار ولا تنافي لاحتمال أن تكون هي المقطعة . وفي الهداية : ولو تبايعا فضة بفضة أو ذهباً بذهب ومع أقلهما شيء آخر تبلغ قيمته باقي الفضة جاز البيع من غير كراهة فإن لم تبلغ فمع الكراهة وإن لم يكن له قيمة لا يجوز البيع لتحقق الربا إذا الزيادة لا يقابلها عوض فيكون ربا .اهـ. وصرح في الإيضاح بأن الكراهة قول محمد، وأما أبو حنيفة فقال : لا بأس به. وفي المحيط : إنما كرهه محمد خوفاً أن يألفه من الناس ويستعملوه فيما لا يجوز . وقيل : لأنهما باشرا الحيلة لإسقاط الربا كبيع العينة فإنه مكروه. وفي فتح القدير: اشترى تراب الفضة بفضة لا يجوز لأنه إن لم يظهر في التراب شيء فظاهر، وإن ظهر فهو بيع الفضة بالفضة مجازفة ولهذا لو اشتراه بتراب فضة لا يجوز لأن البدلين هما الفضة لا التراب ولو اشتراه بتراب ذهب جاز لعدم لزوم العلم بالمماثلة لاختلاف الجنس، فلو ظهر أن لا شيء في التراب لا يجوز، وكلما جاز فمشتري التراب بالخيار إذا رأى لأنه اشترى ما لم يره اهـ .
His statement: (And one sound dirham and two mixed dirhams for two sound dirhams and one mixed dirham) means that the sale is valid due to unity of genus, so equality in quantity is considered, not quality. And al-ghalla is [silver] coins, or said to be what the treasury returns and merchants take; there is no contradiction because it is possible that they are the same. In al-Hidayah: If two persons exchange silver for silver, or gold for gold, and with the lesser of the two there is something else whose value reaches the rest of the silver, the sale is permissible without dislike; if it does not reach [the value], then it is with dislike; if it has no value, the sale is not permissible because riba is established since the increase is not met with a countervalue, so it is riba. End. And it is stated in al-Idah that dislike is the statement of Muhammad, while Abu Hanifa said: There is no harm in it. In al-Muhit: Muhammad only disliked it out of fear that people might become accustomed to it and use it for what is not permissible. And it was said: Because they have directly practiced a stratagem (hila) to eliminate riba, like bay' al-'inah, which is disliked. In Fath al-Qadir: He bought silver dust with silver – it is not permissible; if nothing appears in the dust, it is obvious, and if something appears, it is a sale of silver for silver by approximation. Hence, if he buys it with silver dust, it is not permissible because the two countervalues are silver, not dust. If he buys it with gold dust, it is permissible because equality in kind is not required due to difference of genus. If it turns out that there is nothing in the dust, it is not permissible. Whenever it is permissible, the buyer of the dust has an option when he sees it because he bought what he has not seen. End.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage addresses the exchange of dirhams where the quantity on both sides is equal (three dirhams each) but the quality differs (one sound and two mixed vs. two sound and one mixed). The ruling: the contract is valid because equality in quantity (number) suffices for same genus; quality (sound vs. mixed) does not affect validity. The author then defines 'ghalla' (mixed dirhams) and cites al-Hidayah on the ruling for silver-for-silver sales with an extra item. If the extra item makes up the difference in value, the sale is valid without dislike. If not, it is disliked according to Muhammad al-Shaybani, while Abu Hanifa saw no harm. The reason for Muhammad's dislike is to prevent habitual use of stratagems that could lead to riba. The passage then discusses buying silver dust (which may contain no actual silver) with silver – prohibited – but with gold dust, permissible due to different genus, with buyer having the right of sight option.

Key Terms
غلة (ghalla): Coined dirhams or silver currency that is mixed or worn, used by merchants and accepted by the treasury.
كراهة (karaha): Dislike (makruh) – a ruling less than prohibition but discouraged.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Invalid due to quality difference
B) Valid but disliked (makruh)
C) Valid and permissible without dislike
D) Only valid if the mixed dirhams are of the same weight

Key Takeaway
In same-genus currency exchanges, equality in quantity (weight/count) is sufficient for validity; quality differences do not invalidate the contract.

Dinar for Debt or Absolute Dirhams with Set-off: Istihsan vs Qiyas
Ruling on exchanging a dinar for debt/absolute dirhams with set-off

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute between Zufar and the majority on the validity of a sarf contract involving set-off, and apply the istihsan reasoning to determine the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A set-off effectively creates a new sarf contract; istihsan permits it, while qiyas prohibits.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses two scenarios of exchanging a dinar for ten dirhams. The first, where the dirhams are a debt, is valid by consensus. The second, where the dirhams are absolute and later set off against a debt, generates an intra-Hanafi dispute. Understanding the interplay of qiyas and istihsan here is key to grasping Hanafi flexibility in muamalat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First: dinar for a debt of ten dirhams (valid by consensus). Second: dinar for absolute ten dirhams with subsequent set-off (disputed).
The text uses 'عليه' for debt and 'مطلقة' for absolute dirhams.
2
Note the consensus on the first scenario
There is no riba concern in debt because specification by possession is unnecessary. This is agreed upon.
The consensus proof: absence of risk in debt.
3
Identify the qiyas position of Zufar
Zufar holds the second scenario invalid by qiyas because the set-off constitutes istibdal (replacement of the exchange consideration) in a sarf contract, which requires immediate possession and specification.
Qiyas here analogizes to a standard sarf.
4
Analyze the istihsan reasoning of the majority
The set-off dissolves the original contract and creates a new sarf contract attached to the debt. This attachment is permissible by implication, similar to renewing a sale at a higher price.
Istihsan prioritizes ease and flexibility over strict analogy.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Zufar requires a new explicit contract; the majority validates the set-off. The mufta bihi is the majority (istihsan) position.
Relied-upon view as per al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ودينار بعشرة عليه أو بعشرة مطلقة ودفع الدينار وتقاصا العشرة بالعشرة) أي صح بيع أما إذا قابل الدينار بالعشرة التي عليه ابتداء فلأنه جعل ثمنه دراهم لا يجب قبضها ولا تعيينها بالقبض وهو جائز إجماعاً لأن التعيين للاحتراز عن الربا ولا ربا في دين وإنما الربا في دين يقع الخطر في عاقبته ولذا لو تصارفا دراهم ديناً بدنانير ديناً صح لفوات الخطر. وأما الثانية وهو ما إذا باعه بعشرة مطلقة ثم تقاصا فالمذكور هنا إستحسان، والقياس عدم الجواز وهو قول زفر لكونه استبدالاً ببدل الصرف وجه الاستحسان أنهما لما تقاصا انفسخ الأول وانعقد صرف آخر مضافاً إلى الدين فتثبت الإضافة اقتضاء كما لو جدد البيع بأكثر من الثمن الأول. وفي فتح القدير : ونحن نقول موجب العقد عشرة مطلقة تصير متعينة بالقبض وبالإضافة بعد العقد إلى العشرة الدين صارت كذلك غير أنه بقبض سابق ولا سقط
His statement: (And a dinar for ten dirhams of debt or for absolute ten dirhams, and he paid the dinar, and they set off the ten dirhams against the ten dirhams) meaning the sale is valid. As for when he initially matches the dinar with the ten dirhams of debt, it is because he makes its price dirhams that are not required to be possessed nor specified by possession, and that is permissible by consensus because specification is to avoid riba, and there is no riba in debt; riba only occurs in a debt where risk occurs in its outcome. Hence, if two persons exchange dirhams as a debt for dinars as a debt, it is valid because the risk is avoided. As for the second scenario, when he sold it for absolute ten dirhams and then they set off, what is mentioned here is istihsan, while qiyas is that it is not permissible – that is the statement of Zufar – because it is a replacement of the exchange consideration. The reason for istihsan is that when they set off, the first contract is dissolved and another sarf contract is concluded added to the debt, so the addition is established by implication, as if he renewed the sale for more than the first price. In Fath al-Qadir: We say the requirement of the contract is absolute ten dirhams; they become specified by possession, and by addition after the contract to the ten dirhams of debt, they become so, except that there is no previous possession and no fall...
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage discusses two scenarios for exchanging a dinar for ten dirhams. The first: the dinar is exchanged for a debt of ten dirhams (i.e., the buyer already owes the seller ten dirhams). This is valid by consensus because there is no riba in debt; the debt is not subject to the same specification rules. The second: the dinar is sold for absolute (unidentified) ten dirhams, and then later the parties set off this obligation against another debt. According to qiyas (analogy), this is invalid because it is essentially a substitution of the exchange consideration, which could lead to riba. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) and deems it valid. Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl (a prominent Hanafi student) held the qiyas view. The istihsan reasoning: when the parties set off, the original sarf contract is effectively cancelled, and a new sarf contract is formed that is attached to the existing debt. This is allowed because the attachment is by implication, similar to renewing a sale at a higher price. The passage ends with a quote from Fath al-Qadir explaining that the absolute dirhams become specified by possession and by addition to the debt, though the text is cut off.

Key Terms
إستحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that departs from strict qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling based on circumstances or ease.
تقاص (taqas): Set-off; mutual cancellation of debts between two parties.
استبدال (istibdal): Replacement or substitution of one consideration for another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (except Zufar), what is the ruling when someone sells a dinar for absolute ten dirhams and then sets off against an existing debt?
A) Invalid by both qiyas and istihsan
B) Valid by istihsan
C) Valid only if the debt is in dirhams
D) Valid only if the dinar is paid immediately

Key Takeaway
Set-off can transform an absolute sarf contract into a valid transaction attached to a debt, applying istihsan over strict qiyas.

Selling Mixed Dirhams and a Dinar: The Rule of Matching Currencies
Explains validity of mixed-currency sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sale combining same and different currencies and explain why the contract is valid.

Core Idea
In mixed-currency sales, same-currency portion must be equal; different-currency portion has no equality requirement.

This lesson examines a key ruling in the Hanafi school regarding transactions that mix dirhams of different qualities with a dinar. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains why such a sale is valid despite the apparent inequality in the total amount. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the principles of riba in currency exchange and the conditions for a valid sarf contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction
What are the two items sold and what is the price? In the example, 10 dirhams are sold for 5 dirhams and a dinar.
2
Isolate the same-currency component
Separate the payment into the part that matches the seller's currency: 5 dirhams are exchanged for 5 dirhams.
3
Verify equality condition
Check that the same-currency exchange is equal in quantity. Here, 5 = 5. This satisfies the condition for riba-free exchange.
4
Identify the different-currency component
The remaining 5 dirhams are exchanged for the dinar, which is a different currency. No equality requirement applies.
5
Conclude validity
Since the same-currency part is equal and the different-currency part is allowed, the entire contract is valid (sahih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصرف ودرهمين غلة بدرهمين صحيحين ودرهم غلة ودينار بعشرة عليه أو بعشرة مطلقة ودفع الدينار هي الدراهم مثاله : باع عشرة دراهم بخمسة دراهم ودينار يصح العقد فإن الخمسة بالخمسة والخمسة الأخرى بالدينار، وكذا لو قابل جنسين بجنسين كما في مسألة الكتاب اهـ.
Book of Sarf: And two bad dirhams for two good dirhams, and a bad dirham and a dinar for ten owed or ten absolute, and paying the dinar, it is the dirhams. Example: He sold ten dirhams for five dirhams and a dinar; the contract is valid, because the five are exchanged for the five, and the other five for the dinar. Likewise if he matched two types with two types, as in the issue of the book.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author states the general rule for a transaction involving different qualities of dirhams and a dinar. The example clarifies: selling 10 dirhams (presumably mixed) for 5 dirhams and a dinar is valid because the 5 dirhams are exchanged for 5 dirhams (same currency, equal), and the remaining 5 dirhams are exchanged for the dinar (different currency, no requirement of equality). This establishes the principle that when there is a combination of same and different currencies, the same-currency part must be equal, while the different-currency part is free.

Key Terms
درهم غلة (dirham ghalla): A substandard or alloyed dirham, as opposed to a pure one
درهم صحيح (dirham sahih): A pure, good-quality dirham

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone sells 10 dirhams for 5 dirhams and a dinar, why is the contract valid according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because the buyer pays more than the seller
B) Because the 5 dirhams match in quantity and the dinar is a different currency
C) Because the contract is a sale of money for money with no conditions
D) Because the dinar is considered equivalent to the remaining dirhams

Key Takeaway
A sale of mixed currencies is valid if the same-currency exchange is equal; the rest is treated as a separate exchange with no equality requirement.

Selling Mixed Currencies in Sarf: The Principle of Equality
Explains a similar ruling with 11 dirhams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi sarf rule that a same-currency exchange must be equal, and a surplus can be compensated by exchange with a different currency.

Core Idea
Same-currency equality is mandatory; surplus can be in other currency.

This lesson examines a specific sarf transaction: selling 11 dirhams for 10 dirhams and a dinar. The Hanafi ruling clarifies that the contract is valid because the 10 dirhams are exchanged equally, and the remaining dirham is exchanged for a different currency (dinar). This exemplifies the broader principle that in currency exchange, the same-currency component must be equal, while differences can be settled with other currencies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction components
Break down the sale into two parts: the 10 dirhams for 10 dirhams (same currency) and the 1 dirham for 1 dinar (different currency).
2
Apply the sarf rule to the same-currency part
In sarf, exchange of same currency must be equal (mithl bi mithl). Here 10 for 10 satisfies this condition.
Equality is required for validity; otherwise riba would occur.
3
Apply the sarf rule to the different-currency part
Exchanging dirhams for dinars (different currency) is allowed even if quantities differ, as long as possession is immediate (a separate Hanafi condition not detailed here).
The general principle: idha ikhtalafa al-jins, fa-la ba'sa bi al-tafadhul.
4
Conclude the ruling
Since the same-currency part is equal and the remainder is exchanged for a different currency, the entire sale is valid (sahih).
This demonstrates the Hanafi method of deconstructing mixed-currency transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونظير المسألة المسألة التي تلي هذه وهي. قوله: (وأحد عشر درهما بعشرة دراهم ودينار أي صح بيع فتكون العشرة بمثلها والدينار بالدرهم تصحيحاً للعقد على ما بينا وإنما ذكر هذه بعد التي قبلها وإن كانت قد علمت مما قبلها لبيان أن الصرف إلى خلاف الجنس لا يتفاوت في الجميع أو جزء واحد؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج
And similar to the issue is the issue that follows it, which is his statement: (And eleven dirhams for ten dirhams and a dinar) i.e., the sale is valid, so the ten are for their like, and the dinar for the dirham, to correct the contract as we explained. He only mentioned this after the previous one, even though it could have been known from before, to show that exchange (sarf) with a different gender does not differ whether in whole or in part; as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the commentary. It repeats the principle: selling 11 dirhams for 10 dirhams and a dinar is valid because the 10 dirhams are exchanged for the 10 dirhams (equal), and the one remaining dirham is exchanged for the dinar. The author notes that this was mentioned to clarify that the rule applies whether the difference is on the whole or a single part.

Key Terms
صرف (sarf): The exchange of gold and silver currencies

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling 11 dirhams for 10 dirhams and a dinar valid in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Because the dinar is worth more than the missing dirham
B) Because the 10 dirhams are equal and the 1 dirham is exchanged for the dinar
C) Because the seller gives a discount
D) Because the contract is a loan

Key Takeaway
In sarf, exchange of same currency must be equal; any surplus must be compensated by exchange with a different currency to avoid riba.

Same Currency Exchange with Added Commodity: Ruling and Dislike (Karaha)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dislike in same-currency exchange with added commodity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on exchanging same currency with an added commodity and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on dislike (karaha).

Core Idea
In same-currency exchange, equality in quantity matters, not quality; adding a commodity can save from riba but may invite karaha.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a common transaction: exchanging good dirhams for a mix of good and bad dirhams. The Hanafi school permits it as long as quantity matches, but when an extra commodity is added to one side, its value determines whether the exchange is pure or disliked. The great imams Abu Hanifa and Muhammad differ on the dislike.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
The text states that when exchanging the same type (silver for silver, gold for gold), only equality in quantity is required; quality is irrelevant.
This is the default rule for ribawi items.
2
Recognize the problematic case
When a commodity (something else) is added to the lesser side of a same-currency exchange, the ruling depends on its value relative to the difference.
The added item breaks the pure same-type exchange.
3
Determine the value condition
If the commodity's value fully covers the difference, the sale is valid without dislike. If it partially covers, the sale is valid but disliked (karaha). If it has no value, the sale is invalid (riba).
The dislike is attributed to Imam Muhammad.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that there is no dislike at all in such transactions. Muhammad dislikes it out of precaution to prevent people from slipping into riba or using legal tricks.
The dispute is on the existence of karaha, not validity.
5
Understand the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Hanifa (no dislike), as his view is more straightforward and the precaution is not strong enough to declare dislike.
The tarjih is based on the absence of strong evidence for karaha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ودرهم صحيح ودرهمين غلة بدرهمين صحيحين ودرهم غلة) أي يصح بيع الاتحاد في الجنس فيعتبر التساوي في القدر دون الوصف. والغلة المقطعة، وقيل ما يرده بيت المال ويأخذه التجار ولا تنافي لاحتمال أن تكون هي المقطعة . وفي الهداية : ولو تبايعا فضة بفضة أو ذهباً بذهب ومع أقلهما شيء آخر تبلغ قيمته باقي الفضة جاز البيع من غير كراهة فإن لم تبلغ فمع الكراهة وإن لم يكن له قيمة لا يجوز البيع لتحقق الربا إذا الزيادة لا يقابلها عوض فيكون ربا .اهـ. وصرح في الإيضاح بأن الكراهة قول محمد، وأما أبو حنيفة فقال : لا بأس به. وفي المحيط : إنما كرهه محمد خوفاً أن يألفه من الناس ويستعملوه فيما لا يجوز . وقيل : لأنهما باشرا الحيلة لإسقاط الربا كبيع العينة فإنه مكروه. وفي فتح القدير: اشترى تراب الفضة بفضة لا يجوز لأنه إن لم يظهر في التراب شيء فظاهر، وإن ظهر فهو بيع الفضة بالفضة مجازفة ولهذا لو اشتراه بتراب فضة لا يجوز لأن البدلين هما الفضة لا التراب ولو اشتراه بتراب ذهب جاز لعدم لزوم العلم بالمماثلة لاختلاف الجنس، فلو ظهر أن لا شيء في التراب لا يجوز، وكلما جاز فمشتري التراب بالخيار إذا رأى لأنه اشترى ما لم يره اهـ.
His statement: (And one good dirham and two bad dirhams for two good dirhams and one bad dirham) i.e., the sale of same type is valid, so equality in quantity is considered without regard to quality. And bad dirhams are the cut ones, and it is said what the treasury returns and merchants take, and there is no contradiction because they might be the cut ones. And in al-Hidayah: If two people exchange silver for silver or gold for gold, and with the lesser of the two there is something else whose value reaches the rest of the silver, the sale is valid without dislike; if it does not reach, then with dislike; and if it has no value, the sale is not valid because riba is realized since the increase is not offset by a consideration, so it becomes riba. End. And in al-Idah it states that the dislike is the view of Muhammad, but Abu Hanifa said: There is no harm in it. And in al-Muhit: Muhammad only disliked it out of fear that people would become accustomed to it and use it in what is not permissible. And it is said: because they engaged in a trick to eliminate riba, like a sale of cina, which is disliked. And in Fath al-Qadir: If one buys silver dust with silver, it is not valid; if nothing appears in the dust, it is obvious; if something appears, it is a sale of silver for silver with uncertainty; therefore if he buys it with silver dust it is not valid because the two compensations are silver, not dust. If he buys it with gold dust, it is valid because equivalence in type is not required due to different types; if it appears that there is nothing in the dust, it is not valid; and whenever it is valid, the buyer of dust has an option when he sees it because he bought what he did not see.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage contains a ruling statement and an intra-Hanafi dispute. First, the ruling: when selling good and bad dirhams of the same currency, equality in quantity is required; quality does not matter. The term 'ghalla' is defined. Then, from al-Hidayah, a scenario is discussed: if something else (like a commodity) is added to the lesser side of a same-currency exchange, its value determines the ruling. If the added item's value covers the difference, the exchange is valid without dislike; if it covers only part, it is valid but disliked; if it has no value, it is riba and invalid. The dislike is attributed to Imam Muhammad, while Abu Hanifa permits it absolutely. The reason for Muhammad's dislike is to prevent a trick similar to cina (double sale). Finally, Fath al-Qadir discusses buying silver dust with silver: it's invalid because the dust is essentially silver, so it's like selling silver with uncertainty; buying with gold dust is valid because types differ, and the buyer has an option if the dust turns out empty.

Key Terms
غلة (ghalla): Substandard dirhams, often broken or returned by the treasury
كراهة (karaha): Dislike, a gradation below haram; in this context, it is makruh tanzihi according to Muhammad

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on exchanging silver for silver with an added commodity that does not fully cover the difference?
A) Valid without dislike
B) Valid but disliked according to Muhammad
C) Invalid due to riba
D) Valid only if the commodity is gold

Key Takeaway
In same-currency exchange, adding a commodity can prevent riba, but if its value is insufficient, the exchange is disliked according to Imam Muhammad, though the mufta bihi is no dislike.

6 min
Buying Silver Dust: Validity and Option of Sight
Ruling on buying silver dust with silver or gold, and option of sight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on buying silver dust (soil containing silver) with silver or gold, and identify when the buyer has an option.

Core Idea
Buying silver dust with silver is invalid due to uncertainty; buying with gold is valid, with option of sight upon inspection.

This passage from Fath al-Qadir, quoted in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, addresses a specific transaction: purchasing soil that may contain silver particles. The Hanafi ruling highlights the importance of certainty and avoiding riba in same-type exchanges, while allowing cross-type exchange with a safeguard for the buyer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object and countervalue
The object is silver dust (turab al-fidda), which may contain silver particles. The countervalue is either silver or gold dust.
The dust is considered a mix of soil and potentially silver.
2
Evaluate same-type exchange (silver for silver)
If both items are essentially silver, the exchange must be equal in quantity and certain. Since the dust's silver content is unknown, it involves gharar (uncertainty) and potential riba; hence the sale is invalid.
The text says it is not valid: if no silver appears, it's obvious; if it appears, it's a sale with uncertainty.
3
Evaluate cross-type exchange (silver dust for gold dust)
Since gold and silver are different types, equality in quantity is not required. The uncertainty about the dust's silver content does not affect validity, as long as the exchange is not pure silver for silver.
The sale is valid because the types differ, so there is no riba concern.
4
Consider the option of sight
The buyer has khiyar al-ru'ya (option of sight) because he bought an unseen item. If after inspection the dust contains no silver, the sale becomes invalid (since the object is worthless), but otherwise the buyer can rescind or confirm.
This protects the buyer from deception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

8 min
Set-off in Currency Exchange: Istihsan vs. Qiyas
Explains set-off permissibility via istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on set-off in currency exchange, explain the istihsan reasoning that overrides qiyas, and identify the practical difference between Zufar's and Abu Hanifa's views.

Core Idea
Set-off in exchange is valid by istihsan because it creates a new contract, not a debt-for-debt swap.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: is set-off (taqas) valid when a dinar is sold for absolute dirhams and later offset against a debt? While qiyas (analogy) would invalidate it, the majority follow istihsan (juristic preference) to uphold its validity. We will trace the reasoning and see how this ruling applies in practice.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two cases
First, identify the two scenarios in the text: (1) selling a dinar for a specific debt of ten dirhams (valid by consensus), and (2) selling for ten absolute dirhams followed by set-off (disputed).
The first case is straightforward because specification is not required in debts.
2
Identify the ikhtilaf
Note the dispute: Zufar follows qiyas and says set-off is invalid because it is a substitution for the consideration of the exchange, which resembles exchanging a debt for a debt.
The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) follow istihsan.
3
Understand the istihsan reasoning
Analyze the basis of istihsan: set-off effectively rescinds the first contract and creates a new exchange contract appended to the debt. This is analogous to renewing a sale for a higher price.
The new contract avoids the prohibited debt-for-debt exchange directly.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the mufta bihi view, set-off allows settling exchange transactions without physical transfer; under Zufar's view, it would require a new contract with possession.
This affects daily financial settlements among Hanafi followers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ودينار بعشرة عليه أو بعشرة مطلقة ودفع الدينار وتقاصا العشرة بالعشرة) أي صح بيع أما إذا قابل الدينار بالعشرة التي عليه ابتداء فلأنه جعل ثمنه دراهم لا يجب قبضها ولا تعيينها بالقبض وهو جائز إجماعاً لأن التعيين للاحتراز عن الربا ولا ربا في دين وإنما الربا في دين يقع الخطر في عاقبته ولذا لو تصارفا دراهم ديناً بدنانير ديناً صح لفوات الخطر. وأما الثانية وهو ما إذا باعه بعشرة مطلقة ثم تقاصا فالمذكور هنا إستحسان، والقياس عدم الجواز وهو قول زفر لكونه استبدالاً ببدل الصرف وجه الاستحسان أنهما لما تقاصا انفسخ الأول وانعقد صرف آخر مضافاً إلى الدين فتثبت الإضافة اقتضاء كما لو جدد البيع بأكثر من الثمن الأول. وفي فتح القدير : ونحن نقول موجب العقد عشرة مطلقة تصير متعينة بالقبض وبالإضافة بعد العقد إلى العشرة الدين صارت كذلك غير أنه بقبض سابق ولا ... سقط، الفساد أي صرف الجنس إلى خلاف جنسه قوله : أطلق) في العشرة الدين فشمل الخ) هذا كتاب الصرف ... يبالى به لحصول المقصود من التعيين بالقبض بالمساواة. وعلى هذا التقرير لا حاجة إلى اعتبار فسخ العقد الأول بالإضافة إلى العشرة الدين بعد العقد على الإطلاق بخلاف ما إذا باع بألف ثم بألف وخمسمائة فإن الفسخ لازم لأن أحدهما لم يصدق على الآخر بخلاف العشرة مطلقاً هذه العشرة للصدق لأن الإطلاق ليس قيداً في العقد بها وإلا لم يمكن قضاؤها أصلاً إذ مع لا وجود للمطلق بقيد الإطلاق وعلى هذا مشوا، وتقريره أنهما لما غيرا موجب العقد فقد فسخاه إلى عقد آخر اقتضاء .اهـ. أطلق في العشرة الدين فشمل ما إذا كانت عليه قبل عقد الصرف أو حدثت بعده وقيل : لا يجوز التقاص بدين حادث بعده والأول أصح لأن التقاص هو المتضمن لفسخ الأول وإنشاء صرف آخر فيكتفي بالدين عند التقاص بخلاف رأس مال السلم حيث لا يجوز جعله قصاصاً بدين آخر مطلقاً متقدماً كان أو متأخراً لأن المسلم فيه دين، ولو صحت المقاصة برأس مال السلم لافترقا عن دين بدين ولذا لا يجوز إضافته إلى الدين ابتداء بأن يجعل الدين الذي على المسلم إليه رأس مال السلم بخلاف الصرف. وقال الفقيه أبو الليث في شرح الجامع الصغير : إذا استقرض بائع الدينار عشرة من المشتري أو غصب منه فقد صار قصاصاً ولا يحتاج إلى التراضي لأنه قد وجد منه القبض اهـ. وقوله «وتقاصا» راجع إلى الثانية وأما الأولى فتقع المقاصصة بنفس العقد. والحاصل أن الدين إذا حدث بعد الصرف فإن كان بقرض أو غصب وقعت المقاصة وإن لم يتقاصا، وإن حدث بالشراء بأن باع مشتري الدينار من بائع الدينار ثوباً بعشرة إن لم يجعلاه قصاصاً لا قصاصاً باتفاق الروايات، وإن جعلاه قصاصاً ففيه روايتان؛ كذا في الذخيرة. ومن يصير مسائل المقاصات وإن لم تكن من الصرف ما في المنتقى : له وديعة وللمودع على صاحبها دين من جنسها لم تصر قصاصاً بالدين قبل الاتفاق عليه وإذا اجتمعا عليه لا تصير الوديعة قصاصاً ما لم يرجع إلى أهله ،فيأخذها وإن كانت في يده فاجتمعا على جعلها قصاصاً لا يحتاج إلى غير ذلك، وحكم المغصوب كالوديعة سواء، والدينان إذا كانا من جنسين لا تقع بينهما ما لم يتقاصا، وكذا إذا كان أحدهما حالاً والآخر مؤجلاً، وكذا إذا كان أحدهما غلة والآخر صحيحاً. كذا في الذخيرة أيضاً من كتاب الصرف. وذكر في كتاب المداينات أن الدينين إذا كانا مؤجلين لا تقع المقاصة حتى يتقاصا وكذر قبله أن التفاوت في الوصف المقاصة بنفسه ولا يمنع إذا جعلاه قصاصاً .اهـ. وفي الصحاح : تقاص القوم إذا قاص كل واحد منهما صاحبه في حساب أو غيره .اهـ. وإذا اختلف الجنس وتقاصا كأن كان له عليه وللمديون مائة دينار عليه فإذا تقاصا تصير قصاصاً بمائة يمنع راجع إلى قوله «أو بعشرة مطلقة إذ لا يتصور في المقيدة أن يكون الدين حادثاً بعد عقد الصرف .
His statement: (And a dinar for ten owed or ten absolute, and paying the dinar, and they set off the ten with the ten) i.e., the sale is valid. As for when he initially matches the dinar with the ten owed, it is because he made its price dirhams that are not required to be taken possession of nor specified by taking possession, and that is permissible by consensus, because specification is to avoid riba, and there is no riba in debt; rather, riba occurs in a debt where there is risk in its outcome. Hence if they exchange dirhams as a debt for dinars as a debt, it is valid because the risk is absent. As for the second case, when he sold it for ten absolute and then they set off, what is mentioned here is istihsan; qiyas would be non-permissibility, which is the view of Zufar, because it is a substitution for the consideration of the exchange. The basis of istihsan is that when they set off, the first contract is rescinded and another exchange contract is concluded, appended to the debt, so the addition is established by implication, just as if they renewed the sale for more than the first price. And in Fath al-Qadir: We say that the subject of the contract is ten absolute dirhams; they become specified by taking possession and by the after-contract addition to the ten debt. They become so, but with prior taking possession... The corruption means exchanging a type for a different type. His statement: 'absolute' in the ten debt includes... This is the book of Sarf... It matters not because the purpose of specification by taking possession with equality is achieved. Based on this, there is no need to consider rescission of the first contract by adding to the ten debt after the contract absolutely, unlike if he sold for one thousand and then for one thousand five hundred, then rescission is necessary because one does not correspond to the other. Unlike ten absolute, this ten corresponds because absoluteness is not a restriction in the contract; otherwise it could not be fulfilled at all, since there is no existence of the absolute with the restriction of absoluteness. This is their approach. His explanation: when they changed the subject of the contract, they rescinded it into another contract by implication. End. 'Absolute in the ten debt' includes whether the debt existed before the exchange contract or arose after. It is said: Set-off is not permissible with a debt arising after, but the first is more correct because set-off entails rescission of the first and creation of another exchange, so the debt at the time of set-off is sufficient, unlike the capital of salam, where it cannot be made as set-off with another debt absolutely, whether prior or subsequent, because the salam commodity is a debt; if set-off with the capital of salam were valid, they would separate from a debt for a debt. Hence it is not permissible to append it to a debt initially, i.e., to make the debt owed by the salam seller the capital of salam, unlike exchange. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: If the seller of the dinar borrows ten from the buyer or seizes them by force, it becomes set-off without need for mutual agreement because taking possession has occurred. End. His statement 'and they set off' refers to the second case; as for the first, set-off occurs by the contract itself. The conclusion: if the debt arises after the exchange, if it was by loan or seizure, set-off occurs even if they did not agree; if it arose by purchase (e.g., the buyer of the dinar sold a garment to the seller for ten), if they do not make it set-off, there is no set-off by consensus; if they make it set-off, there are two narrations; as in al-Dhakhira. And from the issues of set-off, even if not from exchange, from al-Muntaqa: He has a deposit, and the depositor has a debt of the same type; it does not become set-off before agreement; if they both agree, the deposit does not become set-off until they return to its owner and take it; even if it is in his hand, if they both agree to make it set-off, no more is needed. The same rule applies to a seized item. Two debts of different types do not set off without mutual agreement; similarly if one is due and the other deferred; similarly if one is ghalla and the other sahih. As in al-Dhakhira from the book of Sarf. And in Kitab al-Madayanat, two deferred debts do not set off without mutual agreement, and before it, difference in quality does not prevent set-off by itself if they make it set-off. In al-Sihah: 'Ta'qas al-qawm' means that each one set off with the other in a calculation or otherwise. And if the types differ and they set off, like if he has against him and the debtor has a hundred dinars against him, then when they set off, it becomes set-off with a hundred... The statement returns to his saying 'or ten absolute' because it is not conceivable in the restricted case that the debt arises after the exchange contract.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This is a detailed discussion of set-off (taqas) in currency exchange, combining ruling statements and an intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar. First, two cases are distinguished: selling a dinar for a specific debt of ten dirhams (valid by consensus because specification is not needed in debt), and selling for ten absolute dirhams followed by set-off. The latter is valid by istihsan (juristic preference), while qiyas (analogy) would forbid it, as per Zufar. The istihsan reasoning is that set-off effectively rescinds the original contract and creates a new exchange contract where the dirhams are now tied to the debt, similar to renewing a sale at a higher price. The text then explains that the debt can be pre-existing or arising later; the stronger view allows set-off with a later debt. Unlike the capital of salam, which cannot be set off with another debt because that would be a debt-for-debt exchange. Various scenarios are covered: loan or seizure automatically results in set-off; purchase requires agreement and has different narrations. General rules for set-off are given from other works: deposits, seized items, different types, due dates, and qualities.

Key Terms
تقاص (taqas): Set-off; mutual cancellation of debts or obligations
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; deviating from qiyas for a more equitable solution
صرف (sarf): Currency exchange; sale of gold, silver, or money for money of the same or different type
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; extending a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a common effective cause

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the basis for permitting set-off (taqas) when a dinar is sold for absolute dirhams and later set off against a debt?
A) Consensus (ijma')
B) Analogy (qiyas)
C) Juristic preference (istihsan)
D) Custom (urf)

Key Takeaway
Set-off in currency exchange is valid by istihsan because it rescinds the original contract and creates a new exchange appended to the debt, avoiding the prohibition of debt-for-debt exchange.

Majority Content in Silver and Gold Coins: Hanafi Ruling and Exception
Explaining the Hanafi ruling on coins based on majority content and the exception by the Mashayikh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coins based on majority content, apply it to determine when disparity is allowed in sarf, and explain the Mashayikh's restriction.

Core Idea
A coin's legal treatment in sarf depends on its majority: if pure metal dominates, treat as pure; if alloy dominates, disparity allowed but hand-to-hand required.

In the Hanafi school, the exchange of silver and gold coins (sarf) is governed by strict rules to prevent riba. However, coins are rarely pure; they contain alloy (ghish). This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies how the school determines the ruling based on the majority component—whether the pure metal or the alloy is dominant—and introduces an important practical exception from the Mashayikh of Transoxiana. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly applying sarf rules in transactions with mixed-content coins.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Recognize that the treatment of a coin in sarf is determined by its majority component by weight. If silver or gold is the majority, the coin is legally treated as pure metal.
The phrase 'غالب الفضة والذهب فضة وذهب' establishes this rule.
2
Apply to coins with majority pure metal
When the pure metal is majority, the coin is treated as pure. Thus, exchanging such a coin for another coin of the same type requires equality in weight and immediate possession (taqabud). Disparity or deferment would constitute riba al-fadl or riba al-nasi'ah.
Borrowing such coins is also only valid by weight, not count.
3
Apply to coins with majority alloy
When the alloy is majority, the coin is not considered a dirham or dinar in ruling. Therefore, it may be sold for its own kind with disparity in weight or count. However, immediate possession is still required because the coin contains some pure metal.
The ruling for zakah also differs: the minority silver/gold is counted for the threshold.
4
Recognize the Mashayikh's exception
Despite the general permissibility, the Mashayikh of Transoxiana did not allow disparity in certain valuable coins like 'adali and ghatarifah, even though their alloy content was higher. Their reason was to prevent opening the door to explicit riba due to the high value of these coins.
This shows that fatwa may be adjusted based on local circumstances and maqasid.
5
Understand the rationale for the distinction
When the pure metal is minority, it still exists in reality (by weight) and potentially (by melting), so it is considered legally. When alloy is minority, it is treated as non-existent. The Mashayikh's restriction was pragmatic, not a change in the underlying principle.
The rationale relies on the ability to recover metal and the risk of widespread riba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغالب الفضة والذهب فضة وذهب يعني فلا يصح بيع الخالصة بها ولا بيع بعضها ببعض إلا متساوياً وزناً ولا يصح الاستقراض بهما إلا وزناً لأنهما لا يخلوان عن قليل غش إذ هما لا ينطبعان عادة بدونه، وقد يكون خلقياً فيعسر التمييز فصار كالرديء وهو والجيد سواء عند المقابلة بالجنس فيجعل الغش معدوماً فلا اعتبار له أصلاً بخلاف ما إذا غلب الغش فإن للمغلوب اعتباراً كما سيأتي. وغالب الغش ليس في حكم الدراهم والدنانير فيصح بيعها بجنسها متفاضلاً أي وزناً وعدداً لأن الحكم للغالب فلا يضر التفاضل لجعل الغش مقابلاً بالفضة أو الذهب الذي في الآخر، ولكن يشترط التقابض قبل الافتراق لأنه صرف في البعض لوجود الفضة أو الذهب من الجانبين. والفرق أن الفضة أو الذهب المغلوب موجود حقيقة حالاً بالوزن ومآلاً بالإذابة لكونهما يخلصان منه بالإذابة فكانا موجودين حقيقة وحكماً حتى يعتبرا في نصاب الزكاة بخلاف الغش المغلوب لأنه يحترق ويهلك ولا لون له. وفي الهداية: ومشايخنا - يعني مشايخ ما وراء النهر من بخارى وسمرقند - لم يفتوا بجواز ذلك أي ببيعها بجنسها متفاضلاً في العدالي والغطارفة مع أن الغش فيها أكثر من الفضة لأنها أعز الأموال في ديارنا، فلو أبيح التفاضل فيها ينفتح باب الربا الصريح.
And the majority of silver and gold is [treated as] silver and gold, meaning it is not valid to sell the pure [coins] with them, nor to sell some of them with each other except equally by weight, nor is borrowing them valid except by weight, because they are not free of a little alloy, since they are not normally minted without it, and it may be natural, making distinction difficult, so it becomes like inferior [quality], and it and good [quality] are equal when exchanged with its own kind, so the alloy is considered non-existent, and no consideration is given to it at all, unlike when alloy predominates, then the overpowered [metal] has consideration as will come. And the majority of alloy is not in the ruling of dirhams and dinars, so selling them with their own kind with disparity is valid, i.e., by weight and count, because the ruling is for the majority, so disparity does not harm, making the alloy correspond to the silver or gold that is in the other [coin], but possession-taking before separation is required because it is currency exchange in part due to the existence of silver or gold from both sides. And the difference is that the overpowered silver or gold exists in reality currently by weight and eventually by melting, because the silver or gold is extracted from it by melting, so they exist in reality and in ruling such that they are considered in the zakah threshold, unlike the overpowered alloy because it burns and perishes and has no color. And in Al-Hidayah: And our Mashayikh—meaning the Mashayikh of Transoxiana from Bukhara and Samarkand—did not give a fatwa permitting that, i.e., selling them with their own kind with disparity in the 'adali and ghatarifah [coins], even though the alloy in them is more than the silver, because they are the most precious wealth in our lands, so if disparity was permitted, the door of explicit riba would open.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the Hanafi ruling on coins that contain a mixture of pure metal and alloy (ghish). The key principle is that the legal treatment of a coin in sarf (currency exchange) is determined by its majority component. If silver or gold constitutes the majority of the coin's weight, the coin is legally treated as pure silver or gold. Consequently, it cannot be sold for pure silver or gold coins unless the exchange is equal in weight and immediate (hand-to-hand), because any disparity would constitute riba al-fadl. Similarly, such coins can only be loaned by weight, not by count, because the value is in the metal content. Conversely, if the alloy is the majority, the coin is not considered a dirham or dinar in the ruling; it may be sold for its own kind (i.e., other alloyed coins) with disparity in weight or count, but immediate possession (taqabud) before separation is still required because the transaction still involves an element of sarf due to the presence of some pure metal. The commentary then provides the rationale: when the pure metal is the minority, it is still considered existent in reality and in ruling because it can be recovered by melting, hence it is counted for zakah threshold. When the alloy is the minority, it is treated as non-existent. However, the Mashayikh of Transoxiana (e.g., Bukhara and Samarkand) did not issue fatwa permitting disparity in certain coins like the 'adali and ghatarifah, even though their alloy content exceeded silver, because these were high-value coins and permitting disparity would open the door to explicit riba. This shows that local custom and economic conditions can influence fatwa within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
غالب (ghalib): The majority component by weight in a coin; determines the legal ruling.
غش (ghish): Alloy or impurity mixed with silver or gold in a coin.
صرف (sarf): The exchange of gold for gold, silver for silver, or gold/silver for the other, either hand-to-hand or with disparity rules.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it permissible to sell a silver coin containing alloy for another silver coin containing alloy with disparity in weight?
A) When the alloy is the majority in both coins
B) When the silver is the majority in both coins
C) In all cases, if the exchange is hand-to-hand
D) It is never permissible

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sarf, the majority component of a coin determines its legal treatment; but local scholars may restrict the general ruling to prevent riba in practice.

Nature of Kafala: Addition of Liability in Debt vs. Demand
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafala's nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the nature of kafala, explain the evidence for each, and identify the practical consequences that follow from each view.

Core Idea
Kafala does not transfer the debt; it only makes the guarantor subject to demand.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores a foundational intra-Hanafi dispute: does a guarantor (kafil) become a debtor himself, or merely someone from whom the debt can be demanded? The answer affects core legal consequences like release, death, and oaths. Let's dissect the arguments and settle on the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing positions
State clearly what each view claims: one says kafala adds liability in the debt itself (the debt enters the guarantor's dhimma); the other says it only adds liability in demand (mutalaba).
The passage uses 'ضم الذمة في الدين' vs 'ضم الذمة في المطالبة'.
2
Examine the evidence for each view
For the debt view: validity of gifting the debt to the guarantor, and ability of creditor to take from guarantor's estate. For the demand view: analogies with agents, trustees, and the double usurper case.
The double usurper analogy shows one debt can be owed by two persons without being collected twice.
3
Evaluate the Hanafi response to the debt view's evidence
The passage refutes the gift argument: it is a legal concession (hukman) not proof of original debt. The estate argument is disputed; on the correct view, the creditor cannot take from the estate.
The author sides with the demand view.
4
List the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf)
On the correct view: guarantor's death does not make debt due from his estate; release of principal releases guarantor; guarantor's oath 'I have no debt' is true. On the weak view: opposite.
Know these consequences—they are the reason the dispute matters.
5
Identify the tarjih and the mufta bihi
The matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) and the author (Ibn Nujaym) adopt the demand view. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
Later Hanafi scholars generally follow this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣٤٢ كتاب الكفالة الكفالة له وعليه. وفي التحرير والذمة وصف شرعي به الأهلية لوجوب ماله وعليه. وفسرها فخر الإسلام بالنفس والرقبة التي لها عهد والمراد أنها العهد فقولهم في ذمته أي في نفسه باعتبار عهدها من باب إطلاق الحال وإرادة المحل .اهـ. والمطالبة من طالبته مطالبة وطلاباً من باب قاتل ؛ كذا في المصباح. وحاصله أن الكفيل والمكفول عنه صارا مطلوبين للمكفول له، سواء كان المطلوب من أحدهما هو المطلوب من الآخر كما في الكفالة بالمال أو لا كما في بالنفس فإن المطلوب من الأصيل المال ومن الكفيل إحضار النفس، ولفظ المطالبة بإطلاقه ينتظمهما، هذا على رأي بعضهم وجزم مسكين بأن المطلوب منهما واحد وهو تسليم النفس فإن المطلوب عليه تسليم نفسه والكفيل قد التزمه. وقيد بالمطالبة لدفع قول من قال إنها الضم في الدين فيثبت الدين في ذمة الكفيل من غير سقوط عن الأصيل. ولم يرجح في المبسوط أحد القولين على الآخر وما يظن مانعاً من لزوم صيرورة الدين الواحد دينين على هذا القول في المبسوط بأنه لا مانع لأنه لا يستوفي إلا من أحدهما كالغاصب مع غاصب الغاصب فإن كلاً ضامن للقيمة وليس حق المالك إلا في قيمة واحدة لأنه لا يستوفي إلا من واختياره تضمين أحدهما يوجب براءة الآخر فكذا هنا لكن هنا بالقبض لا ومما يدل على ثبوت الدين في ذمة الكفيل أنه لو وهب الدين للكفيل صح ويرجع الكفيل به على الأصيل مع أن هبة الدين من غير من عليه الدين لا تصح . دفعه أحدهما، بمجرد اختياره والحاصل أن ثبوت الدين في الذمة اعتبار من الاعتبارات الشرعية فجاز أن يعتبر الشيء الواحد في ذمتين إنما يمتنع في عين تثبت في زمن واحد في طرفين حقيقيين، ولكن المختار ما ذكره المصنف أنه في مجرد المطالبة لا الدين لأن اعتباره في ذمتين وإن أمكن شرعاً لا يجب الحكم بوقوع كل ممكن إلا بموجب ولا . لا لأن التوثق يحصل بالمطالبة وهو موجب يستلزم، ولا بد من ثبوت اعتبار الدين في الذمة كالوكيل بالشراء يطالب بالثمن وهو في ذمة الموكل ؛ كذا في فتح القدير وكذا الوصي والولي والناظر يطالبون بما لزم دفعه ولا شيء في ذمتهم، وكذا كل أمين يطالب برد الأمانة ولا شيء في ذمته، وكذا سيد العبد المأذون المديون مطالب ببيعه أو فدائه ولا دين عليه. وأما الجواب عن الهبة والإبراء فإنا جعلناه في حكم دينين تصحيحاً لتصرف صاحب الحق وذلك عنده، أما قبله فلا ضرورة ولا داعي إلى ذلك. المجمع لابن ملك ما صورته : وفائدة كون الكفالة ضم الذمة إلى الذمة في المطالبة لا في الدين على القول الأصح أنه إذا مات الكفيل بعد تعذر الاستيفاء من الأصيل يأخذ المطالب الدين من تركة الكفيل على قول بعض المشايخ لأنه مديون على قولهم ولا يأخذ على الأصح لأن حق الطالب على الكفيل في المطالبة فحسب، وكذا إذا أبرأ الطالب الأصيل برئ الكفيل من غير عكس. هذا على القول الصحيح، وأما على قول بعض المشايخ فلا يبرأ كل واحد من الكفيل والأصيل بإبراء الطالب  كتاب الكفالة ٣٤٣ الخلاف نشأ وفي فتح القدير : ولا يخفى أن ما نقل من قول أبي حنيفة أن الدين فعل يقتضي أن يكون في ذمة الكفيل أيضاً كما هو في ذمة الأصيل إذ فعل الأداء واجب عليه اهـ. وقد يقال إنما وجب عليه لإسقاط المطالبة عنه وأبو حنيفة إنما جعله فعلاً لسقوطه عن الميت إذ لا يتأتى الفعل منه فلم تصح الكفالة عن ميت ،مفلس، وليس مراده أن حقيقته الفعل لأنه وصف قائم بالذمة، وإنما مراده أن المقصود منه الفعل كما لا يخفى. وقد صرحوا في مواضع بأنه وصف ولذا قالوا : الديون تقضى بأمثالها لأن ما في الذمة لا يمكن تسليمه. وفي الإيضاح أخذاً من الغاية أن تعريفها بالضم في الدين لا ينتظم الكفالة بالنفس والكفالة بالعين والكفالة بالفعل اهـ . قلت : نعم لا يشمل لكن المعرف لها بذلك إنما أراد تعريف الكفالة بالمال فإن أصل من أن الكفيل هل يثبت في ذمته المال أو لا . ثم رأيت صاحب البدائع أشار إلى ذلك في بيان حكمها ولم يذكر الشارحون لهذا الاختلاف ثمرة فإن الاتفاق على أن الدين لا يستوفي إلا من أحدهما وأن الكفيل مطالب وأن هبة الدين له صحيحة ويرجع به على الأصيل ولو اشترى الطالب بالدين شيئاً من الكفيل صح مع أن الشراء بالدين من غير من عليه الدين ويمكن أن يقال : إنها تظهر فيما إذا حلف الكفيل أن لا دين عليه فعلى الأصح لا يصح . يحنث، وعلى الضعيف يحنث وجهد المقل دموعه وسيأتي عند قوله وبطل تعليق البراءة من الكفالة بالشرط ما يقتضي أن يكون ثمرة . وفي الخانية : رجل ادعى على غيره أنه ضمن له فلان الغائب كذا كذا درهماً قال الشيخ الإمام يحلفه بالله ما له عليك هذا المال من الوجه الذي يدعي. وعن أبي يوسف إن عرض المدعى عليه للقاضي فإنه يحلفه بالله ما له عليك هذا من الوجه الذي يدعي وإن لم يعرض حلفه بالله ما ضمن والتعريض أن يقول المدعى عليه أن الرجل قد يضمن مالاً ثم يؤدي أو يبرئه الطالب أو يؤديه المضمون عنه فيبرأ الضامن اهـ. وينبغي أن يكون قول الشيخ الإمام مفرعاً على أنها للضم في الدين، وما عن أبي يوسف مفرع على الأصح كما لا يخفى. ومما يضعف أنها الضم في الدين أن المديون لو دفع الدين ثم كفل به إنسان قالوا لا يصح مع قولهم ببقاء الدين بعد الدفع وأن الساقط المطالبة بالألفاظ عن المال . " • عن أحدهما بل له الأخذ من الكفيل إذا أبرأ الأصيل، وكذا عكسه لأن كل واحد مديون ومطالب اهـ بلفظه لكنه لم يعزه إلى كتاب فليحرر من الكتب المعتمدة والله تعالى أعلم اهـ؛ كذا في حاشية الرملي. أقول : وجهه ظاهر وسيأتي متناً أنه لو أبرئ الأصيل أو أخر عنه برئ الكفيل وتأخر عنه ولا مع أن المصنف مشى على القول بأنها الضم في المطالبة، وسيأتي هناك عن الغزي أيضاً أن الذي في الكتب المعتمدة أن المال يحل بموت الكفيل وأنه يؤخذ من تركته . ينعكس قوله : (وينبغي أن يكون قول الشيخ الإمام الخ) قال في النهر : هذا وهم منشؤه توهم أن قوله ماله عليك هذا المال يفيد ما ادعاه وليس كذلك إذ معناه ماله عليك المطالبة به، وكيف يصح ما ادعاه
The book of suretyship. Suretyship: for him and upon him. In al-Tahrir: al-dhimma is a legal attribute by which one has the capacity for obligations for his property and upon him. Fakhr al-Islam interpreted it as the self and the neck that has a covenant, meaning it is the covenant; so their saying 'in his dhimma' means in himself considering his covenant, from the angle of referring to the state and intending the place. End. al-Mutalaba (demand) is from 'talabtuhu mutalabatan wa tilaban' from the pattern of qatala; like in al-Misbah. The gist is that the guarantor and the principal debtor both become demanded by the creditor, whether what is demanded from one is the same as from the other as in surety for property, or not as in surety for the person: what is demanded from the principal is property, from the guarantor is producing the person; the term 'mutalaba' generally includes both. This is according to some; Miskin stated that what is demanded from both is one thing: handing over the person, for the principal is required to hand over himself and the guarantor has undertaken it. It was restricted to 'mutalaba' to refute the one who said it is the addition of liability in the debt, so the debt is established in the guarantor's dhimma without dropping from the principal. al-Mabsut did not favor one of the two views over the other. What is thought to be an obstacle to one debt becoming two debts according to this view in al-Mabsut is that there is no obstacle because it is only collected from one of them, like the usurper and the usuper's usurper: each is liable for the value, and the owner's right is only in one value because he only collects from one, and his choosing to hold one liable causes the acquittal of the other. So here, but here it is by taking possession, not by mere choice. Among what indicates the establishment of the debt in the guarantor's dhimma is that if the creditor gifts the debt to the guarantor, it is valid, and the guarantor may recourse to the principal for it, even though gifting a debt from someone not liable for it is not valid. Its refutation: one of them, by mere choice. The upshot: the establishment of debt in the dhimma is a legal consideration, so it is permissible to consider a single thing in two dhimmas; it is only impossible in a specific entity established at one time in two real directions. But the chosen view is what the author mentioned: it is restricted to mere demand, not the debt, because considering it in two dhimmas, even if possible legally, we do not rule every possible occurs except with a cause. No, because the security is achieved by demand, which is a sufficient cause, and it does not necessitate establishing the debt in the dhimma. Like the agent for purchase: he is demanded for the price while it is in the dhimma of the principal; likewise in Fath al-Qadir—similarly the executor, guardian, and overseer are demanded for what they must pay while nothing is in their dhimma; similarly, every trustee is demanded to return the trust while nothing is in his dhimma; similarly, the master of a permited slave who is in debt is demanded to sell him or ransom him while no debt is upon him. As for the response concerning the gift and release, we have made it in the ruling of two debts to validate the act of the right-holder, and that is according to him; before that, there is no necessity or need. Al-Majma' by Ibn Malik: the benefit of kafala being addition of dhimma to dhimma in demand, not in debt, on the correct view, is that if the guarantor dies after the impossibility of collection from the principal, the creditor takes the debt from the guarantor's estate according to some shaykhs because he is a debtor according to them; and he does not take according the correct view because the creditor's right against the guarantor is only in demand. Similarly, if the creditor releases the principal, the guarantor is acquitted, not the reverse. This is on the correct view. As for the view of some shaykhs, each of the guarantor and principal is not acquitted by the creditor's release of one of them... [PAGE_343] ... the disagreement arose. In Fath al-Qadir: it is not hidden that what is transmitted from Abu Hanifa that 'the debt is an act' necessitates that it should be in the guarantor's dhimma as it is in the principal's dhimma, since the act of payment is obligatory upon him. End. It may be said: it became obligatory upon him to remove the demand from him, and Abu Hanifa only made it an act because it drops from the deceased, for an act cannot be performed by him, so kafala on behalf of a deceased insolvent is not valid. He does not intend that its reality is an act, because it is a description subsisting in the dhimma; rather, he intended that its purpose is the act, as is not hidden. They have stated in places that it is a description, and therefore they said: 'debts are paid with their likes' because what is in the dhimma cannot be delivered. In al-Idah, taken from the goal: defining it as addition in debt does not incorporate kafala for the person, kafala for a specific object, and kafala for an act. End. I say: yes, it does not include them, but the one who defined it intended to define kafala for property, for the origin is whether property is established in the guarantor's dhimma or not. Then I saw that the author of al-Bada'i' indicated that in explaining its ruling, and the commentators did not mention a practical consequence of this difference, for they agreed that the debt is only collected from one of them, that the guarantor is demanded, that gifting the debt to him is valid and he recurs to the principal, and that if the creditor buys something from the guarantor with the debt, it is valid even though buying with the debt from someone not liable for it is not. It may be said: it appears when the guarantor swears that he has no debt: on the correct view he does not break his oath; on the weak view he breaks it. And 'the exertion of a poor man is his tears.' And it will come at his statement: 'and the suspension of acquittal from kafala by a condition is invalid' what suggests a consequence. In al-Khaniyya: a man claimed against another that he stood surety for a certain absent person for such-and-such dirhams. Shaykh al-Imam said: he makes him swear by God: 'There is no right for this money against you from the aspect he claims.' From Abu Yusuf: if the defendant presents the case to the judge, he makes him swear: 'You have no right for this money against me from the aspect he claims'; if he does not present, he makes him swear: 'I did not stand surety.' And the presentation is that the defendant says: 'Sometimes a person stands surety for property then pays, or the creditor releases him, or the principal pays him, so the guarantor is acquitted.' End. It should be that the saying of Shaykh al-Imam is derived from the view that it is addition in the debt, and what is from Abu Yusuf is derived from the correct view, as is not hidden. And among what weakens that it is addition in the debt is that if the debtor pays the debt and then someone stands surety for it, they said it is not valid, even though they say the debt remains after payment and that what is dropped is the demand. By words: of the property. Rather, from one of them; rather, he may take from the guarantor if he released the principal, and likewise the opposite, because each is a debtor and demanded. End. But he did not attribute it to a book, so let it be verified from relied-upon books. And Allah knows best. End, like in al-Ramli's hashya. I say: its point is clear, and it will come in the matn that if the principal is released or delayed, the guarantor is acquitted and delayed, and it does not reverse, even though the author proceeded on the view that it is addition in demand. And there it will come from al-Ghazzi also that what is in the relied-upon books is that the property becomes due upon the guarantor's death and is taken from his estate. It does not reverse. His statement: 'It should be that the saying of Shaykh al-Imam etc.' said in al-Nahr: this is a mistake, whose source is the misconception that his statement 'you have no right to this money' proves what he claimed, but it is not so, for its meaning is 'you have no right to demand it'. And how can what he claimed be valid?
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage deals with the nature of kafala (suretyship) in Hanafi law, specifically a key intra-madhhab dispute: does the guarantor become liable for the debt itself, or only become subject to a right of demand (mutalaba)? The text opens by defining the term kafala and the concept of dhimma (legal capacity), then introduces the core disagreement. Some scholars (referred to as 'some shaykhs') hold that kafala is the addition of liability in the debt itself (ضم الذمة في الدين), meaning the debt is established in the guarantor's dhimma as well, without dropping from the principal. Others, including the author's chosen view, hold that it is only an addition of liability in the demand (ضم الذمة في المطالبة), so the guarantor is merely a party from whom the debt can be demanded, not a debtor in the full sense. The passage examines arguments for both sides, using analogies like the double usurper (ghasib ma'a ghasib al-ghasib) to show that it is legally possible for one value to be owed by two persons, and examples of agents (wakil), executors (wasi), etc., who are demanded to pay or return something without being personally indebted. The practical consequences are highlighted: on the correct view, if the guarantor dies, the creditor does not take from his estate; on the other view, he does. Similarly, if the creditor releases the principal, the guarantor is acquitted on the correct view but not on the other. The passage also discusses the effect on oaths (if the guarantor swears he has no debt, he breaks his oath only on the weak view) and the validity of gifting the debt to the guarantor. The text further cites al-Khaniyya and a transmission from Abu Yusuf regarding how to administer oaths in a claim of suretyship, noting that these rulings are derived from the two different positions. The author (probably Ibn Nujaym) sides with the view that kafala is only in demand, as stated in the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq). The passage ends with a critique of an earlier commentator's misinterpretation. Overall, the lesson demonstrates the complexity of kafala and the importance of understanding the precise nature of the obligation: it is a form of surety that does not transfer the debt but creates a secondary right to demand performance.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafala): A contract whereby a person (kafil) becomes liable for the obligation of another (makful 'anhu) to a third party (makful lahu), either in property, person, or specific act.
الذمة (al-dhimma): A legal attribute (wasf shar'i) describing a person's capacity to be subject to rights and obligations. In kafala, the question is whether the debt becomes established in the kafil's dhimma.
المطالبة (al-mutalaba): The right to demand payment or performance. In the context of kafala, the correct view restricts the kafil's obligation to being subject to this demand only, not being a debtor himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
On the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, what is the nature of the obligation created by kafala?
A) The debt is transferred to the guarantor's dhimma, and the principal is released.
B) The debt is established in both the principal's and guarantor's dhimma, but can only be collected from one.
C) The guarantor becomes subject to the right of demand (mutalaba) only, not the debt itself.
D) The guarantor and principal become co-debtors, and the creditor can collect from both simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position is that kafala adds liability only in demand (mutalaba), not in the debt itself, which has clear consequences for estate liability, release, and oaths.

Introduction to Kafala: Definition, Types, Proofs, and the Nature of Liability
Defines kafala, its types, proofs, and key dispute on liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of kafala, distinguish its two types (bi'l-mal and bi'l-nafs), cite the primary dalil (Quran 12:72 and hadith 'al-za'im gharim'), and explain why kafala bi'l-nafs involves liability (ghurm) according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Kafala binds the guarantor to assume the principal's obligation; even guarantee of the self carries liability because the guarantor must produce the principal.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq opens the book of kafala (guarantee). It defines the contract, outlines its two types (property and personal appearance), presents direct evidence from the Quran and Sunnah, and addresses a key dispute: whether guaranteeing a person's presence entails liability. The Hanafi school affirms that it does, as the guarantor is burdened with producing the principal. You will learn the essential elements, the parties involved, and the relied-upon positions for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and scope
From the opening lines, extract what the guarantor is responsible for: paying the principal's debt, producing his person, or delivering a specific item (or its value if lost).
Note that the guarantor is pursued for the entire debt if alone, half if two.
2
Distinguish the two types
The passage explicitly mentions two types: kafala bi'l-mal (suretyship for property/debt) and kafala bi'l-nafs (suretyship for personal appearance). Each has different practical demands.
Kafala bi'l-nafs requires producing the principal; kafala bi'l-mal requires paying the debt.
3
Examine the textual proofs
The author cites Quran 12:72 (wa li man ja'a bihi himlu ba'irin wa ana bihi za'im) and the hadith 'al-za'im gharim' (reported by Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi). These establish the legitimacy and binding nature of kafala.
The hadith is general, covering both types of kafala.
4
Recognize the four parties
Identify the four essential parties: makful 'anhu (debtor/principal), makful lahu (creditor/beneficiary), kafil (guarantor), and makful bihi (the guaranteed debt or person). Note that for personal guarantee, the term is 'makful bihi' not 'makful 'anhu'.
Terminologies vary regionally (za'im in Medina, hamil in Egypt, kafil in Iraq).
5
Analyze the dispute on liability (ghurm) in personal guarantee
Some argue that suretyship of the self involves no financial loss, hence no ghurm. The Hanafi response: ghurm means 'obligation of harm' (luzum darar), not necessarily financial loss. The guarantor is compelled to produce the principal, which is a burden.
The Hanafi position relies on the general hadith and Quran 25:65 where 'gharam' means punishment/harm.
6
Identify the mufta bihi regarding taking guarantors for future obligations
Imam Muhammad (al-Thani) allowed taking a guarantor for wife's maintenance before it becomes due, out of kindness (rifq). The fatwa is upon his view, as preferred by the author of al-Muhit.
This is an example of istihsan overriding strict qiyas.

مطالبة الكفيل بما على الأصيل فإن كان عليه دين طولب بكله الكفيل أن كان واحداً، وإن كانا اثنين طولب كل واحد بنصفه وفي الكفالة بالنفس يطالب بإحضاره إن أمكن كما سيأتي . والكفيل بالعين يطالب بتسليمها حال قيامها وببدلها حال هلاكها وبالتسليم يطالب لها وبالفعل جميعاً، وقدمنا أنه اشتراط الخيار فيها أكثر من ثلاثة فيما يصح تعليقه وما لا يصح قبيل الصرف السابع في صفتها فهي عقد جائز به ،لازم وسيأتي أن له الرجوع عنها في . مسألة بايع فلاناً فما بايعته فهو علي الثامن في محاسنها ومساويها، فمحاسنها جليلة وهي تفريج كرب الطالب الخائف على ماله والمطلوب الخائف على نفسه فقد كفاهما مؤنة ما أهمهما وهو نعمة كبيرة عليهما، ولذا كانت من الأفعال العالية حتى امتن الله بها حيث قال وكفلها زکریا [آل عمران : ٣٧] في قراءة التشديد المتضمن للامتنان على مريم إذ جعل لها من يقوم بمصالحها ويقوم بها. ومساويها كما في المجتبى قال : الامتناع على التكفل أقرب إلى الاحتياط لأنه مكتوب في التوراة والزعامة أولها ملامة وأوسطها ندامة وآخرها غرامة» اهـ. التاسع في أنواعها سيأتي أنها نوعان : كفالة بالنفس وكفالة بالمال العاشر في دليلها قوله تعالى ولمن جاء به حمل بعير وأنابة زعيم [يوسف: ۷۲] وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «الزعيم غارم»(۱) رواه أبو داود والترمذي. وفي الدر المنثور : الزعيم الكفيل وغارم من الغرم وهو أداء شيء لازم اهـ. ويحتاج إلى معرفة أسامي أربعة : المكفول عنه وهو المديون، والمكفول له وهو الدائن، والكفيل وهو الملتزم، والمكفول به وهو الدين ويقال للمكفول بنفسه مكفول به ولا يقال مكفول
