Kafil Cannot Be Sued Before Payment
Ruling on kafil not being sued before payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal basis for why a kafil cannot be compelled to pay before he actually pays, and distinguish this from a direct debtor.

Core Idea
A kafil undertakes only to be sued, not to pay; he owns the debt only by paying.

In Hanafi fiqh, the contract of kafala (suretyship) creates a unique obligation: the kafil is not liable for the debt itself but only for being subject to legal claim (iltizam al-mutalaba). This lesson examines the ruling that a kafil cannot be sued for payment before he actually pays, based on the reasoning that he only becomes owner of the debt through payment. Understanding this principle is essential to distinguish kafala from other forms of guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The text states: 'The kafil is not demanded with the money before he pays it.' This is the operative legal rule.
It is a prohibition on the creditor, not an obligation on the kafil.
2
Understand the Legal Cause (Illa)
The reason given: the kafil only undertook the obligation to be sued (al-mutalaba), not the debt itself. He only becomes owner of the debt (tamalluk al-dayn) by paying.
Contrast with a debtor, who is directly liable for payment.
3
Apply the General Principle
Since the kafil does not own the debt before payment, he cannot be forced to pay or have recourse against the asil before that point.
This protects the kafil from being treated as a primary debtor.
4
Note the Basis in Zahir al-Riwayah
This ruling is firmly established in the madhhab, as it is based on the foundational texts of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
Authority_weight: zahir_riwayah

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يطالب الكفيل بالمال قبل أن يؤدي عنه لأنه إنما التزم المطالبة وإنما يتملك الدين بالأداء فلا يرجع قبل التملك. فإن قلت : هل للكفيل أخذ الرهن من الأصيل قبل أن يؤدي عنه ؟ قلت : نعم. قال في الخانية : كفل عن رجل بمال ثم إن المكفول عنه أعطى الكفيل رهناً ذكر في الأصل أنه لو كفل بمال مؤجل على الأصيل فأعطاه المكفول عنه رهناً بذلك جاز ا هـ . قيد بالكفيل لأن الوكيل بالشراء له الرجوع على الموكل قبل الأداء لما بينهما من المبادلة الحكمية حتى تحالفا لو اختلفا في مقدار الثمن وللوكيل حبس المبيع إلى استيفاء الثمن
The kafil is not demanded with the money before he pays it, because he only undertook the obligation to be sued, and he only becomes owner of the debt by payment, so he does not have recourse before ownership. If you say: Does the kafil have the right to take a pledge from the asil before he pays for him? I say: Yes. It is stated in al-Khaniya: He became surety for a man for money, then the person on whose behalf he stood surety gave the kafil a pledge. It is mentioned in al-Asl that if he became surety for deferred money on the asil, and the asil gave him a pledge for that, it is permissible. end quote. It is restricted to the kafil because the agent for purchase has recourse against the principal before payment due to the virtual exchange between them, such that they would take oaths if they disagreed about the purchase price, and the agent has the right to detain the sold object until receipt of the price.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings. First, the general rule that a kafil cannot be sued for payment before he actually pays. The rationale is that the kafil's obligation is merely to be subject to the claim (iltizam al-mutalaba), not to pay the debt itself. He only owns the debt (tamalluk al-dayn) by paying it, so he cannot claim reimbursement before that. Second, an exception: the kafil may take a pledge from the asil before paying, as reported from al-Khaniya. The text also distinguishes the kafil from a purchasing agent, who can reclaim from the principal before payment due to the virtual exchange inherent in agency. This distinction highlights the unique nature of kafala as a contract of guarantee, not of representation.

Key Terms
كفيل (kafil): The guarantor or surety.
مطالبة (mutalaba): The act of making a legal claim or being subject to a claim.
تملك الدين (tamalluk al-dayn): Ownership of the debt, acquired only by payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the kafil not required to pay before he actually pays according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the contract of kafala is invalid unless the kafil promises to pay.
B) Because the kafil only undertook the obligation to be sued and does not own the debt before payment.
C) Because the asil must pay first before any claim on the kafil.
D) Because the kafil is not a party to the original loan contract.

Key Takeaway
A kafil cannot be compelled to pay before he voluntarily pays, because his obligation is limited to being subject to a claim, not to the debt itself.

Kafil May Take Pledge from Asil Before Payment
Ruling on permissibility of kafil taking pledge from asil before payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a kafil is permitted to take a pledge from the asil before paying the debt, and distinguish this from the case of a purchasing agent.

Core Idea
A kafil may secure his future recourse by taking a pledge from the asil before payment.

The previous lesson established that a kafil cannot be sued before paying. However, this does not prevent him from taking a pledge (rahn) from the asil as security for his potential reimbursement. This lesson explores the permissibility of such a transaction, as reported in al-Khaniya, and contrasts it with the case of an agent who can also seek recourse before payment due to virtual exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Permissive Ruling
The text answers the question: 'Does the kafil have the right to take a pledge from the asil before he pays for him?' with 'Yes'.
This is a ruling of permissibility (jawaz).
2
Examine the Source Citation
The ruling is supported by a report in al-Khaniya and al-Asl, which describe a scenario where a kafil takes a pledge for a deferred debt.
This shows the ruling is based on authoritative Hanafi sources.
3
Contrast with the Purchasing Agent
The text notes that an agent for purchase can also seek recourse before payment, but due to a different reason: virtual exchange (mubadala hukmiyya) and the ability to detain the bought item.
This distinction highlights the unique basis for the kafil's permission.
4
Derive the Principle
The kafil is allowed to take a pledge because he has a potential right to reimbursement after payment, and securing it beforehand is permissible as a precaution.
It is not a right to demand payment from the asil before paying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يطالب الكفيل بالمال قبل أن يؤدي عنه لأنه إنما التزم المطالبة وإنما يتملك الدين بالأداء فلا يرجع قبل التملك. فإن قلت : هل للكفيل أخذ الرهن من الأصيل قبل أن يؤدي عنه ؟ قلت : نعم. قال في الخانية : كفل عن رجل بمال ثم إن المكفول عنه أعطى الكفيل رهناً ذكر في الأصل أنه لو كفل بمال مؤجل على الأصيل فأعطاه المكفول عنه رهناً بذلك جاز ا هـ . قيد بالكفيل لأن الوكيل بالشراء له الرجوع على الموكل قبل الأداء لما بينهما من المبادلة الحكمية حتى تحالفا لو اختلفا في مقدار الثمن وللوكيل حبس المبيع إلى استيفاء الثمن
The kafil is not demanded with the money before he pays it, because he only undertook the obligation to be sued, and he only becomes owner of the debt by payment, so he does not have recourse before ownership. If you say: Does the kafil have the right to take a pledge from the asil before he pays for him? I say: Yes. It is stated in al-Khaniya: He became surety for a man for money, then the person on whose behalf he stood surety gave the kafil a pledge. It is mentioned in al-Asl that if he became surety for deferred money on the asil, and the asil gave him a pledge for that, it is permissible. end quote. It is restricted to the kafil because the agent for purchase has recourse against the principal before payment due to the virtual exchange between them, such that they would take oaths if they disagreed about the purchase price, and the agent has the right to detain the sold object until receipt of the price.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings. First, the general rule that a kafil cannot be sued for payment before he actually pays. The rationale is that the kafil's obligation is merely to be subject to the claim (iltizam al-mutalaba), not to pay the debt itself. He only owns the debt (tamalluk al-dayn) by paying it, so he cannot claim reimbursement before that. Second, an exception: the kafil may take a pledge from the asil before paying, as reported from al-Khaniya. The text also distinguishes the kafil from a purchasing agent, who can reclaim from the principal before payment due to the virtual exchange inherent in agency. This distinction highlights the unique nature of kafala as a contract of guarantee, not of representation.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge or collateral.
وكيل بالشراء (wakil bil-shira): An agent authorized to purchase.
مبادلة حكمية (mubadala hukmiyya): Virtual exchange; a legal construct treating the agent and principal as if they were exchanging the price and item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is permitted as reported in al-Khaniya and al-Asl.
C) It is based on the principle of virtual exchange between kafil and asil.
D) It is allowed only if the creditor agrees.

Key Takeaway
A kafil may take a pledge from the asil before paying the debt, as security for his potential right to reimbursement, based on Hanafi source texts.

Kafil's Right to Constrain the Asil: Three Conditions
Ruling with three conditions for the kafil's recourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions under which a kafil may constrain the asil when the claimant constrains him, and apply them to a fact pattern.

Core Idea
Three conditions limit the kafil's right to constrain: order, due debt, no offsetting debt.

The Hanafi school grants the kafil a right to seek release from the asil when the claimant pursues him. But this right is not absolute. The text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq specifies three conditions that must all be met. Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly applying the law of kafala.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the right
The kafil, if constrained (mulzam) by the claimant, may in turn constrain the asil to secure his release. This right is established in the text.
The arrow indicates causal relationship: claimant → kafil → asil.
2
Condition 1: Suretyship by the asil's order
The kafala must have been at the asil's request (bi-amrihi). The text from al-Bazzaziyya states that without this order, the kafil cannot constrain the asil because he did not bring him in to release himself.
The asil's permission is the basis of the kafil's right of recourse.
3
Condition 2: Debt due on the asil
The debt must be due (hal) on the asil as it is on the kafil. If the debt is not yet due on the asil, the kafil cannot constrain him.
This condition is stated: 'when the money is due on the asil as it is on the kafil'.
4
Condition 3: No similar debt owed by kafil to asil
The kafil must not owe the asil a debt similar to the amount for which he is kafil. If he does, he cannot constrain the asil, as per al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
This prevents offsetting and ensures fairness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن لوزم لازمه) أي إن لازم الكفيل الطالب لازم الأصيل ليخلصه من هذه العهدة. وأشار إلى أنه لو حبس الكفيل حبس المطلوب، وقدمنا عن البزازية أنه مقيد بما إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره وإلا فلا يلازم الأصيل لأنه ما أدخله ليخلصه، وقدمنا أن للطالب حبسهما . وينبغي أن يقيد أيضاً بما إذا كان المال حالاً على الأصيل كالكفيل وإلا فليس له ملازمته وسيأتي بيان الحلول على الكفيل وحده. في السراج الوهاج أيضاً بما إذا لم يكن على الكفيل للمطلوب دين مثله وإلا فلا يلازمه. وأشار المؤلف إلى أن المحال عليه إذا لوزم وكانت الحوالة بأمر المحيل كان له أن يلازم المحيل ليخلصه عن ملازمة المحال له وإذا حبسه كان له أن يحبسه إلا أن يكون للمحيل على المحال مثله وقد احتال بماله عليه مقيداً فليس للمحال عليه أن يلازم المحيل إذا لوزم ولا يحبسه إذا حبس ا هـ . قوله: (وينبغي أن يقيد أيضاً بما إذا كان المال حالاً الخ يقيد أيضاً بما في القهستاني حيث قال : وإن حبس حبس هو المكفول عنه إلا إذا كان كفيلاً عن أحد الأبوين أو الجدين فإنه إن حبس لم يحبسه به يشعر قضاء الخلاصة .اهـ. وفي حاشية أبي السعود وقيده في الشرنبلالية بما إذا لم يكن من أصول الدائن، فإذا كان المدين أصلاً لا يحبس كفيله ولا يلازم لما يلزم من فعل ذلك بالأصيل وهو ممتنع اهـ. أقول : في دعوى اللزوم نظر بدليل ما في القهستاني وساق عبارته ثم قال : فهذا صريح في أن الكفيل لا يمتنع وإن كان المدين من أصول رب الدين إنما الممتنع حبس الأصيل فقط
He said: 'If he is constrained, he constrains' meaning if the claimant constrains the kafil, the kafil constrains the asil to release him from this liability. And he indicated that if the kafil is imprisoned, the one sought (asil) is imprisoned. And we previously transmitted from al-Bazzaziyya that it is restricted to when the suretyship was by his (asil's) order; otherwise he does not constrain the asil because he did not bring him in to release him. And we previously transmitted that the claimant has the right to imprison both of them. It should also be restricted to when the money is due on the asil as it is on the kafil; otherwise he does not have the right to constrain him, and the statement of the due date solely on the kafil will come later. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj also, it is restricted to when the kafil does not have a debt similar to that owed to him by the asil; otherwise he does not constrain him. And the author indicated that the one to whom a hawala is made (muhal 'alayh), if he is constrained and the hawala was by order of the muhil, he has the right to constrain the muhil to release him from the constraint of the muhal lahu, and if he is imprisoned, he has the right to imprison him, unless the muhil has the equivalent of that debt on the muhal 'alayh and he has drawn a hawala for his money on him as a restricted hawala; then the muhal 'alayh does not have the right to constrain the muhil if he is constrained, nor to imprison him if he is imprisoned. End quote. He said: 'It should also be restricted to when the money is due...' He also restricts it according to what is in al-Qahastani where he said: If he is imprisoned, the person on whose behalf he became surety (mukful 'anhu) is imprisoned, unless he is a surety for one of the parents or grandparents; then if he is imprisoned, he does not imprison him by it, as indicated by the ruling of al-Khulasa. End quote. And in the Hashiya of Abu al-Su'ud, and he restricted it in al-Shurunbulaliyya to when he is not from the ancestors of the creditor; if the debtor is an ancestor, his kafil is not imprisoned nor constrained, because that would necessitate doing that to the ancestor, which is prohibited. End quote. I say: There is consideration in the claim of necessity, as evidenced by what is in al-Qahastani, and he quoted his statement and then said: This is explicit that the kafil is not prevented, even if the debtor is from the ancestors of the owner of the debt; the only thing prevented is imprisoning the ancestor himself.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling that if the kafil is constrained (by the claimant), he may in turn constrain the asil to secure his release. The text outlines three conditions: (1) the suretyship must have been at the asil's request (كفالة بأمره); (2) the debt must be due from the asil (حال) as it is from the kafil; (3) the kafil must not owe the asil a similar debt (otherwise he would be offsetting). The passage also draws an analogy with hawala, where a similar right exists for the muhal 'alayh but with exceptions. Additionally, a special exception from al-Qahastani is introduced: if the asil is a parent or grandparent of the claimant, the kafil cannot be imprisoned if the asil is an ancestor, to avoid indirectly harming the ancestor. However, the author (likely Ibn al-Humam) disagrees with the necessity of this exception, citing al-Qahastani's explicit statement that the kafil is not prevented, but only the imprisonment of the ancestor is prohibited. This shows that the kafil can still be imprisoned even if the asil is an ancestor, but the ancestor himself cannot be imprisoned. This is a subtle point of istihsan vs. strict qiyas.

Key Terms
لوزم (luzim): Being constrained or pursued legally; the kafil is said to be 'mulzam' when the claimant demands payment from him.
المحال عليه (al-muhal 'alayh): The person on whom a transfer of debt (hawala) is made; the transferee.
إذن (idhn): Permission or authorization; a key condition for the kafil's right to constrain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a condition for the kafil to have the right to constrain the asil when he himself is constrained?
A) The kafala must have been without the asil's order.
B) The debt must be due on the asil as it is on the kafil.
C) The kafil must owe a similar debt to the asil.
D) The asil must be an ancestor of the claimant.

Key Takeaway
The kafil may constrain the asil only when the suretyship was by the asil's order, the debt is due on the asil, and the kafil does not owe the asil a similar debt.

10 min
Exceptions and the Hawala Analogy in Kafala
Expansion on exceptions and analogy in kafala ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the hawala analogy for the kafil's right, identify the exception when the kafil owes a similar debt, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on the ancestor case.

Core Idea
The kafil's right mirrors hawala, but exceptions arise when debts offset or filial piety forbids ancestor harm.

The author extends the kafil's right to the analogous case of hawala (transfer of debt). But even in hawala, an exception exists when the muhil has an equivalent claim against the muhal 'alayh. Moreover, a special exception occurs when the asil is an ancestor of the claimant — some Hanafi authorities prohibit constraining the kafil to avoid indirect harm to the ancestor. The present author, however, critically examines this exception.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hawala analogy
The author states that if the muhal 'alayh (transferee) is constrained, he has the right to constrain the muhil (transferor) just as the kafil constrains the asil — provided the hawala was at the muhil's order.
This is a qiyas between kafala and hawala based on a similar underlying relationship.
2
Identify the exception in hawala
The exception: if the muhil has a debt equal to the hawala amount against the muhal 'alayh and the hawala was restricted, then the muhal 'alayh cannot constrain the muhil.
This mirrors the third condition in kafala (no offsetting debt).
3
Analyze the ancestor exception
Al-Qahastani states that if the asil is a parent or grandparent of the claimant, and the kafil is imprisoned, the kafil cannot imprison the asil (ancestor) because that would show disrespect. Some later authorities extend this to forbidding even constraining the kafil.
The exception is based on filial piety and avoiding indirect harm to ancestors.
4
Evaluate the author's critique
The author (Ibn al-Humam) questions the necessity of the blanket exception. He cites al-Qahastani: the kafil can still be imprisoned; only the ancestor's own imprisonment is prohibited. Thus the kafil's right to be released is not affected.
The author favours a narrow application: the exception only prevents imprisoning the ancestor, not the kafil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن لوزم لازمه) أي إن لازم الكفيل الطالب لازم الأصيل ليخلصه من هذه العهدة. وأشار إلى أنه لو حبس الكفيل حبس المطلوب، وقدمنا عن البزازية أنه مقيد بما إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره وإلا فلا يلازم الأصيل لأنه ما أدخله ليخلصه، وقدمنا أن للطالب حبسهما . وينبغي أن يقيد أيضاً بما إذا كان المال حالاً على الأصيل كالكفيل وإلا فليس له ملازمته وسيأتي بيان الحلول على الكفيل وحده. في السراج الوهاج أيضاً بما إذا لم يكن على الكفيل للمطلوب دين مثله وإلا فلا يلازمه. وأشار المؤلف إلى أن المحال عليه إذا لوزم وكانت الحوالة بأمر المحيل كان له أن يلازم المحيل ليخلصه عن ملازمة المحال له وإذا حبسه كان له أن يحبسه إلا أن يكون للمحيل على المحال مثله وقد احتال بماله عليه مقيداً فليس للمحال عليه أن يلازم المحيل إذا لوزم ولا يحبسه إذا حبس ا هـ . قوله: (وينبغي أن يقيد أيضاً بما إذا كان المال حالاً الخ يقيد أيضاً بما في القهستاني حيث قال : وإن حبس حبس هو المكفول عنه إلا إذا كان كفيلاً عن أحد الأبوين أو الجدين فإنه إن حبس لم يحبسه به يشعر قضاء الخلاصة .اهـ. وفي حاشية أبي السعود وقيده في الشرنبلالية بما إذا لم يكن من أصول الدائن، فإذا كان المدين أصلاً لا يحبس كفيله ولا يلازم لما يلزم من فعل ذلك بالأصيل وهو ممتنع اهـ. أقول : في دعوى اللزوم نظر بدليل ما في القهستاني وساق عبارته ثم قال : فهذا صريح في أن الكفيل لا يمتنع وإن كان المدين من أصول رب الدين إنما الممتنع حبس الأصيل فقط
He said: 'If he is constrained, he constrains' meaning if the claimant constrains the kafil, the kafil constrains the asil to release him from this liability. And he indicated that if the kafil is imprisoned, the one sought (asil) is imprisoned. And we previously transmitted from al-Bazzaziyya that it is restricted to when the suretyship was by his (asil's) order; otherwise he does not constrain the asil because he did not bring him in to release him. And we previously transmitted that the claimant has the right to imprison both of them. It should also be restricted to when the money is due on the asil as it is on the kafil; otherwise he does not have the right to constrain him, and the statement of the due date solely on the kafil will come later. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj also, it is restricted to when the kafil does not have a debt similar to that owed to him by the asil; otherwise he does not constrain him. And the author indicated that the one to whom a hawala is made (muhal 'alayh), if he is constrained and the hawala was by order of the muhil, he has the right to constrain the muhil to release him from the constraint of the muhal lahu, and if he is imprisoned, he has the right to imprison him, unless the muhil has the equivalent of that debt on the muhal 'alayh and he has drawn a hawala for his money on him as a restricted hawala; then the muhal 'alayh does not have the right to constrain the muhil if he is constrained, nor to imprison him if he is imprisoned. End quote. He said: 'It should also be restricted to when the money is due...' He also restricts it according to what is in al-Qahastani where he said: If he is imprisoned, the person on whose behalf he became surety (mukful 'anhu) is imprisoned, unless he is a surety for one of the parents or grandparents; then if he is imprisoned, he does not imprison him by it, as indicated by the ruling of al-Khulasa. End quote. And in the Hashiya of Abu al-Su'ud, and he restricted it in al-Shurunbulaliyya to when he is not from the ancestors of the creditor; if the debtor is an ancestor, his kafil is not imprisoned nor constrained, because that would necessitate doing that to the ancestor, which is prohibited. End quote. I say: There is consideration in the claim of necessity, as evidenced by what is in al-Qahastani, and he quoted his statement and then said: This is explicit that the kafil is not prevented, even if the debtor is from the ancestors of the owner of the debt; the only thing prevented is imprisoning the ancestor himself.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling that if the kafil is constrained (by the claimant), he may in turn constrain the asil to secure his release. The text outlines three conditions: (1) the suretyship must have been at the asil's request (كفالة بأمره); (2) the debt must be due from the asil (حال) as it is from the kafil; (3) the kafil must not owe the asil a similar debt (otherwise he would be offsetting). The passage also draws an analogy with hawala, where a similar right exists for the muhal 'alayh but with exceptions. Additionally, a special exception from al-Qahastani is introduced: if the asil is a parent or grandparent of the claimant, the kafil cannot be imprisoned if the asil is an ancestor, to avoid indirectly harming the ancestor. However, the author (likely Ibn al-Humam) disagrees with the necessity of this exception, citing al-Qahastani's explicit statement that the kafil is not prevented, but only the imprisonment of the ancestor is prohibited. This shows that the kafil can still be imprisoned even if the asil is an ancestor, but the ancestor himself cannot be imprisoned. This is a subtle point of istihsan vs. strict qiyas.

Key Terms
لوزم (luzim): Being constrained or pursued legally; the kafil is said to be 'mulzam' when the claimant demands payment from him.
المحال عليه (al-muhal 'alayh): The person on whom a transfer of debt (hawala) is made; the transferee.
إذن (idhn): Permission or authorization; a key condition for the kafil's right to constrain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's preferred view, what is the effect of the ancestor exception?
A) The kafil cannot be imprisoned at all.
B) The kafil cannot imprison the ancestor, but can still be imprisoned by the claimant.
C) The suretyship is invalid.
D) The kafil can imprison the ancestor if the debt is due.

Key Takeaway
The kafil's right in kafala is analogous to hawala, but exceptions arise when debts offset or when the asil is an ancestor — in the latter case, only the ancestor's imprisonment is forbidden, not the kafil's.

Discharge of the Kafil by the Asil's Payment or by the Claimant's Discharge
Ruling on discharge with proof from consensus and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three ways a kafil is discharged, identify the exception when the asil proves prior payment, and apply the consensus proof for the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
The kafil is discharged by the asil’s payment, by the claimant’s discharge, or by a delay—but the asil’s prior payment does not discharge the kafil.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the conditions under which a kafil (surety) is released from his obligation. The general rule is that the kafil is discharged when the asil (principal debtor) pays the debt, and also when the claimant (creditor) discharges the asil or grants a delay. However, exceptions exist: if the asil proves he paid before the kafala contract, only the asil is discharged. The passage also addresses a controversial issue regarding imprisonment when the asil is an ancestor of the claimant, reconciling apparent contradictions between Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modes of discharge
From the text, list the three actions that discharge the kafil: (1) asil's payment, (2) claimant's discharge of asil, (3) claimant's grant of delay.
These are the primary ways the kafil's obligation ends.
2
Understand the consensus proof
The text asserts that the kafil is discharged by the asil's payment by consensus (ijma), refuting any claim that the kafil's debt is independent.
This ijma is the strongest proof against the Shafi'i view.
3
Distinguish exceptions to the asil's discharge affecting the kafil
Recognize that the asil's discharge does not always discharge the kafil. The key exception is when the asil proves he paid before the kafala contract.
In that case, the debt never existed at the time of kafala.
4
Analyze the special case of inheritance
If the claimant dies and the asil is his heir, the kafil is discharged because the debtor and creditor become the same person.
The debt merges in the asil's liability.
5
Reconcile the rulings on imprisonment when the asil is an ancestor
The claimant (descendant) may imprison the kafil even if this indirectly imprisons his ancestor (asil), but the kafil cannot imprison his own ancestor. This is consistent with the rule that an ancestor is not imprisoned for a descendant's debt.
Al-Shurunbulali and al-Qahastani are reconciled by the relationship of the parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لازمه وبرئ بأداء الأصيل ولو أبرأ الأصيل أو أخر عنه برئ الكفيل وتأخر عنه ولا ينعكس هكذا ذكر الشارح تبعاً للهداية. وظاهره أن القائل بأن الكفيل عليه دين لا يبرأ بأداء الأصيل وليس كذلك بل يبرأ إجماعاً لأن تعدد الدين عند القائل به حكمي فيسقط بأداء واحد ا هـ . قوله : (ولو أبرأ الأصيل أو أخر عنه برئ الكفيل وتأخر عنه لما قدمناه أنه يلزم من إبراء الأصيل إبراؤه والتأخير إبراء موقت فتعتبر بالإبراء المؤيد. وإنما قال أبرأ الأصيل أي أبرأ الطالب ولم يقل لو برئ الأصيل لأنه لا يلزم من براءته براءته لما في الخانية ضمن له ألفاً على فلان فرهن فلان أنه كان قضاه إياها قبل الكفالة فإنه يبرأ الأصيل دون الكفيل، ولو برهن أنه قضاه بعدها يبر آن ا هـ. فقد برئ الأصيل في الوجه الأول فقط ولكن يخرج عنه حينئذ مسألة في الخانية لو مات الطالب والأصيل وارثه برئ الكفيل أيضاً لكون المطلوب ملك في ذمته فبرأ هي وبراءته توجب براءته، فعلى هذا لو عبر ببريء الشملها. ويجاب عما ذكرناه من فرع الخانية السابق بأنه ليس من باب البراءة وإنما تبين أن لا دين على الأصيل والكفيل عومل بإقراره كما استثناء فلا يعول على ما في الشرنبلالية وإن تبعه بعضهم لكونه مخالفاً للمنقول .اهـ. قلت: وبالله التوفيق لا مخالفة بينهما عند التحقيق لأن ما في القهستاني فيما إذا كان الدائن أجنبياً والمكفول أصلاً للكفيل وهو من حبس الكفيل للمكفول إذا حبسه الطالب وما في الشرنبلالية فيما إذا كان الكفيل أجنبياً والمكفول أصلاً للدائن وهو استثناء من ملازمة الدائن وهو الطالب للكفيل. وحاصل الكلام حينئذ الطالب له ملازمة الكفيل إلا إذا كان المكفول أصلاً للطالب لما يلزم من ملازمته له وحبسه إياه أصله بدينه بواسطة حبسه للكفيل وهذا ظاهر، وقد ذكره الشرنبلالي تفقهاً منه، وله في ذلك حبس رسالة خاصة سماها «النعمة المجددة بكفيل «الوالدة ومبناها على سؤال صورته في امرأة استدانت من ابنها مالاً وكفلها بإذنها فيه أجنبي ثم إن الابن أراد حبس كفيل أمه فهل له ذلك؟ قال: فأجبت بأنه ليس له حبسه إذ يلزم من حبسه حبس الأم وأنه لا يجوز ولكني أعجب من العلامة الشرنبلالي حيث فهم مخالفة كلام القهستاني لكلامه فأورده سؤالاً على ما قرره ثم أجاب بأني لم أر في الخلاصة ما يفيده، ومن ادعى إفادته فعليه البيان وأنت قد علمت عدم المنافاة لأنه إذا كان الدائن أجنبياً وحبس الكفيل عن أصله أي أصل الكفيل لا يلزم محذور نعم المحذور في حبس الكفيل مكفوله الذي هو أصله فلذا استثناه. هذا ما ظهر لي بعون الله تعالى فتأمله يظهر لك حقيقته إن شاء الله تعالى. وسيأتي في باب الحبس من كتاب القضاء عند قول المتن ويحبس الرجل في نفقة زوجته لا في دين ولده» عن الخير الرملي أنه وقع الاستفتاء فيما ذكره الشرنبلالي من الصورة وذكر الرملي هناك أن للكفيل حبس المديون الذي هو أصل الدائن لأنه إنما حبس لحق الكفيل ولذلك يرجع عليه بما أدى فهو محبوس بدينه الذي يثبت له أو سيثبت على قول من يجعلها ضماً في الدين وعلى قول من يجعلها ضماً في المطالبة فلم يدخل في قولهم لا يحبس أصل في دين فرعه لأنه إنما حبسه أجنبي فيما ثبت له عليه اهـ. ومفاده أن للدائن الذي هو فرع المديون حبس الكفيل الأجنبي وإن لزم منه حبس أصله
He is constrained and is discharged by the payment of the asil, and if the claimant discharges the asil or grants him a delay, the kafil is discharged and the delay applies to him, and the reverse is not true. Thus the commentator said following al-Hidaya: Its apparent meaning is that the one who says that the kafil owes a debt does not become discharged by the asil's payment, but that is not the case; rather, he becomes discharged by consensus, because the multiplicity of debts according to that proponent is legal (hukmi), so it falls by a single payment. End quote. He said: 'And if the claimant discharges the asil or grants him a delay, the kafil is discharged and the delay applies to him' because we have previously established that the discharge of the asil necessitates his discharge, and granting a delay is a temporary discharge, so it is treated like a permanent discharge. And he only said 'discharges the asil' meaning the claimant discharges the asil, and he did not say 'if the asil becomes discharged', because the asil's discharge does not necessarily entail his discharge, as in al-Khaniya: He guaranteed one thousand for a man against so-and-so, and the man gave a pledge that he had paid it before the suretyship; then the asil is discharged but not the kafil. And if he gave a pledge that he paid it after, both are discharged. End quote. So the asil is discharged in the first case only, but then a case from al-Khaniya arises: if the claimant dies and the asil is his heir, the kafil is also discharged because the debt becomes property in the asil's liability, so his discharge entails the kafil's discharge. On this basis, if he had used 'bari'a' it would include both. And the reply to what we mentioned from the earlier branch of al-Khaniya is that it is not a case of discharge, but rather it becomes clear that there is no debt on the asil, and the kafil is treated according to his acknowledgment as an exception. So no reliance is placed on what is in al-Shurunbulaliyya, even if some followed it, because it contradicts transmitted texts. End quote. I say: With Allah's help, there is no contradiction between them upon investigation, because what is in al-Qahastani is when the creditor is a third party and the person guaranteed is an ancestor of the kafil, and it is about the kafil imprisoning the asil if the claimant imprisons him. And what is in al-Shurunbulaliyya is when the kafil is a third party and the asil is an ancestor of the creditor, and it is an exception from the claimant's right to constrain the kafil. The conclusion is: the claimant has the right to constrain the kafil unless the asil is an ancestor of the claimant, because constraining and imprisoning the kafil would lead to constraining his ancestor by means of his debt, which is not allowed. And al-Shurunbulali has a special treatise on this called 'The Renewed Blessing regarding the Surety of the Mother', based on a question: a woman borrowed money from her son and a third party became surety for her with her permission; then the son wanted to imprison the surety of his mother; is he allowed? He answered: He is not allowed to imprison him because it would lead to imprisoning the mother, which is not permissible. But I am surprised at 'Allamah al-Shurunbulali who thought that the statement of al-Qahastani contradicts his statement, so he raised it as an objection to what he had established, then answered that I have not seen in al-Khulasa anything that supports it. And you have known that there is no contradiction, because when the creditor is a third party and the kafil imprisons his ancestor, there is no harm; rather, the harm is in the kafil imprisoning his own ancestor, so he made an exception for that. This is what appeared to me with the help of Allah. And it will come in the chapter of imprisonment from the book of Qada, at the author's statement: 'A man is imprisoned for his wife's maintenance but not for a debt of his child.' Al-Khair al-Ramli said that a fatwa was sought on the picture mentioned by al-Shurunbulali, and al-Ramli mentioned there that the kafil has the right to imprison the debtor who is the ancestor of the creditor because he is imprisoning for his own right as a kafil, for which he will recourse against the asil; he is imprisoned for a debt that is established for him, whether now or in the future according to the opinion that kafala is a joining in the debt (damm fi al-dayn) or a joining in the claim (damm fi al-mutalaba). Therefore, it does not fall under the saying 'An ancestor is not imprisoned for a debt of his descendant' because it is a third party imprisoning for a right that is established on the ancestor. Its meaning is that the creditor who is a descendant of the debtor can imprison the kafil even if it leads to imprisoning his ancestor.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage discusses the discharge of the kafil from his obligation. It first establishes the general rule: the kafil is discharged when the asil pays the debt, and also when the claimant discharges the asil or grants a delay. This is because the kafil's obligation is derivative; it ends when the principal obligation ends. The text then addresses an objection: some scholars who consider the kafil a debtor (like the Shafi'is) might say that the asil's payment does not discharge the kafil. However, the author refutes this by stating that there is consensus (ijma') that the kafil is discharged, because the double debt is merely legal (hukmi) and falls with one payment. Next, the text discusses a nuance: the asil's discharge does not always discharge the kafil. For instance, if the asil proves he had already paid the debt before the kafala contract, the asil is discharged but the kafil remains liable. However, if the claimant dies and the asil is his heir, the kafil is discharged because the debt merges in the asil's liability. The passage then reconciles apparent contradictions between al-Qahastani and al-Shurunbulali regarding the imprisonment of the kafil when the asil is an ancestor of the claimant. The reconciled view is that the claimant (descendant) may imprison the kafil even if the asil is his ancestor, because the imprisonment is for the kafil's own right, not for the ancestor's debt. The ancestor is not directly imprisoned. This is illustrated by the fatwa of al-Khair al-Ramli in which a son cannot imprison his mother's surety because it would indirectly imprison the mother. The overall conclusion is that the rules are consistent: the kafil's right to constrain the asil or the claimant's right to imprison the kafil depend on the relationships involved, but the general principle is that the kafil is discharged by the asil's payment and the claimant's actions.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra'): Discharge or release of a debt by the creditor.
تأخير (ta'khir): Postponement or grant of a delay.
ضمان (daman): Surety or guarantee; synonymous with kafala in this context.
أصل (asl): Ancestor or principal; here used for parent/grandparent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The kafil is discharged when the asil pays the debt. Which of the following is an exception to this rule?
A) When the kafala was without the asil's order.
B) When the asil proves he paid before the kafala contract.
C) When the claimant is a descendant of the asil.
D) When the debt is not yet due.

Key Takeaway
The kafil is discharged by the asil’s payment, by the claimant’s discharge, or by a delay, but not if the asil proves prior payment—this is supported by consensus.

Validity of Suspending Immunity in Kafala with Property
Explains that suspending bara'a is valid only if condition is conventional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on suspending immunity in kafala bil-mal and explain the distinction between conventional and unconventional conditions.

Core Idea
Suspending immunity in kafala is valid only if the condition is customary.

This lesson examines the validity of a condition that suspends the guarantor's immunity in monetary guarantees. There are two conflicting transmissions within the Hanafi school. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq reconciles them by distinguishing between conventional and unconventional conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the discussion is about suspending the guarantor's immunity (bara'a) upon a condition in kafala bil-mal.
2
Note the two transmissions
There are two riwayat: one says it is invalid, another says it is valid.
The text does not name the transmitters.
3
Understand the reconciliation
The author reconciles by stating that the invalidity transmission applies to unconventional conditions, while the validity transmission applies to conventional ones.
The key term is 'shart ghayr muta'arif' (unconventional condition).
4
Apply the ruling
Thus, suspending immunity is valid only if the condition is customary ('urf).
5
Distinguish from kafala bi'l-nafs
This ruling is specific to kafala bil-mal; kafala bi'l-nafs has separate rules.
Refer to al-Khaniyya for details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالمال، وكذا إذا علق البراءة باستيفاء البعض يجوز أو علق البراءة عن البعض بتعجيل البعض يجوز؛ ذكره في مبسوط شيخ الإسلام. فعلم أن المراد بالشرط الشرط الغير المتعارف، واختلاف الروايتين في صحة التعليق محمول على هذا فرواية عدم الجواز فيما إذا كان غير متعارف ورواية الجواز فيما إذا كان متعارفاً ا هـ . فعلى هذا فكلام المؤلف محمول على شرط غير متعارف. وأراد من الكفالة الكفالة بالمال احترازاً عن كفالة النفس فإنه يصح تعليق البراءة منها على تفصيل مذكور في الخانية
With property, and likewise if he suspends immunity upon collecting part, it is permissible, or he suspends immunity from part upon expediting part, it is permissible; it is mentioned in the Mukhtasar of Shaykh al-Islam. Thus it is understood that what is meant by the condition is the unconventional condition, and the difference between the two transmissions regarding the validity of suspension is carried upon this: so the transmission of non-permissibility is when it is unconventional, and the transmission of permissibility is when it is conventional. End. So upon this, the author's statement is carried upon an unconventional condition. And he intended by kafala, kafala bil-mal, to exclude kafala bi'l-nafs, for suspending immunity from it is valid according to details mentioned in al-Khaniyya.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage type is a ruling statement with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that the validity of suspending the immunity (bara'a) of a guarantor in kafala bil-mal depends on whether the condition is conventional ('urf). There are two transmissions: one says it is invalid, another says it is valid. The text reconciles them by stating that the transmission of invalidity refers to unconventional conditions, and the transmission of validity refers to conventional ones. Therefore, the author's (al-Mawsili's) statement in Kanz al-Daqa'iq is interpreted to mean unconventional conditions. This is distinguished from kafala bi'l-nafs, which has its own detailed rules.

Key Terms
تعليق البراءة (ta'liq al-bara'a): Suspending the immunity of the guarantor from liability upon fulfillment of a condition
شرط غير متعارف (shart ghayr muta'arif): A condition that is not customary or conventional in such transactions
كفالة بالمال (kafala bi'l-mal): Guarantee of a monetary debt or property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is it valid to suspend the immunity (bara'a) of a guarantor in kafala bil-mal?
A) It is always valid.
B) It is valid only if the condition is customary.
C) It is never valid.
D) It depends on the amount of the debt.

Key Takeaway
Suspending immunity in kafala bil-mal is valid only if the condition is conventional; this reconciles the two riwayat.

Three Scenarios for Suspending Bara'a in Kafala bi'l-Nafs
Details three scenarios for suspending bara'a in kafala with person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three scenarios for suspending bara'a in kafala bi'l-nafs and state the ruling for each scenario, including the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
In kafala bi'l-nafs, bara'a may be valid even if the attached condition is invalid, depending on the nature of the guarantee.

This lesson examines three scenarios from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq where the release (bara'a) of a guarantor of person (kafala bi'l-nafs) is suspended upon a condition. The rulings hinge on whether the guarantee includes both person and property or only the person. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi contract law in surety arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act
The act is suspending the guarantor's release (bara'a) upon a condition. Recognize that the condition is a stipulation attached to the release.
2
Outline the three scenarios
List each scenario based on the type of guarantee (person only or person and property) and the nature of the condition (payment of money with or without recourse).
Scenario 1: guarantee of person only, condition to pay money for release. Scenario 2: guarantee of person and property, condition to pay property for release. Scenario 3: guarantee of person only, condition to pay money and have recourse.
3
Analyze the ruling for each scenario
For each scenario, determine whether the bara'a is valid and whether the condition is valid. In scenario 1: bara'a valid, condition invalid. Scenario 2: both valid. Scenario 3: both invalid.
The validity depends on the scope of the guarantee and the nature of the condition.
4
Examine the internal ikhtilaf
In scenario 1, note the conflicting riwayat: one says bara'a is valid without payment; another says bara'a is not valid unless payment is made. Identify the sources of these riwayat.
The ikhtilaf is within the Hanafi school, as mentioned in the text.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand how the ikhtilaf affects the guarantor's liability. Under the first riwaya, the guarantor is released without paying; under the second, he must pay to be released.
The practical consequence is whether the guarantor must pay or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : إذا علق براءة الكفيل بالنفس بشرط فهو على وجوه ثلاثة : في وجه تجوز البراءة ويبطل الشرط نحو أن يكفل رجل بنفسه رجل فأبرأه الطالب عن الكفالة على أن يعطيه الكفيل عشرة دراهم جازت البراءة ويبطل الشرط، وإن صالح الكفيل المكفول له على مال ليبرئه عن الكفالة لا يصح الصلح، ولا يجب المال على الكفيل ولا يبرأ عن الكفالة في رواية الجامع وإحدى روايتي الحوالة والكفالة وفي رواية أخرى يبرأ عن الكفالة. وفي وجه تجوز البراءة والشرط وصورة ذلك : رجل كفل بنفس رجل وبما عليه من المال فشرط الطالب على الكفيل أن يدفع المال إلى الطالب ويبرئه عن الكفالة بالنفس جازت الكفالة والشرط. وفي وجه لا يجوز كلاهما وصورة ذلك : رجل كفل بنفس رجل خاصة فشرط الطالب على الكفيل أن يدفع إليه المال ويرجع بذلك على المطلوب فإنه يكون باطلاً اهـ.
He said: If he suspends the immunity of the guarantor of person upon a condition, then it has three aspects: One aspect: the immunity is valid and the condition is invalid, such as a man guarantees the person of a man, and the claimant releases him from the guarantee on condition that the guarantor gives him ten dirhams; the release is valid and the condition is invalid. And if the guarantor settles with the guaranteed-for for property to release him from the guarantee, the settlement is not valid, and the property is not obligatory on the guarantor, and he is not released from the guarantee according to the transmission of al-Jami' and one of the two transmissions of al-Hawala wa al-Kafala, and in another transmission he is released from the guarantee. Another aspect: both the immunity and the condition are valid, and its example: a man guarantees the person of a man and what is upon him of property; then the claimant stipulates on the guarantor to pay the property to the claimant and releases him from the guarantee of person; the guarantee and the condition are valid. Another aspect: both are not valid, and its example: a man guarantees the person of a man only; then the claimant stipulates on the guarantor to pay him property and to have recourse for that against the demanded; then it is invalid. End.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage details the three scenarios for suspending bara'a in kafala bi'l-nafs (guarantee of person). The text is from al-Khaniyya. In the first scenario, the condition is to pay money for release; the release is valid but the condition is void, so the guarantor is not obligated to pay. There is an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether the guarantor is released from the guarantee itself. In the second scenario, when the guarantee includes both person and property, a condition to pay the property and be released from the person guarantee is valid. In the third scenario, if the guarantee is only for the person, a condition to pay money and have recourse against the debtor is entirely invalid.

Key Terms
كفالة بالنفس (kafala bi'l-nafs): Guarantee of the person of the debtor, i.e., to produce him
براءة (bara'a): Immunity/discharge from liability

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario of kafala bi'l-nafs, what is the ruling on the condition that the guarantor pays money to the creditor for his release?
A) The condition is valid and the money must be paid.
B) The condition is invalid but the bara'a is valid.
C) Both are invalid.
D) Both are valid.

Key Takeaway
In kafala bi'l-nafs, a condition to pay money for release is generally void, but the release itself may still be valid unless the guarantee also includes property.

Kafala with Mabi', Marhun, and Amana: Invalid Due to Lack of Personal Guarantee
Explains why kafala with mabi', marhun, and amana is invalid with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for valid kafala with physical items, explain why kafala with sold, mortgaged, and trust items is invalid, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Kafala requires the item to be personally guaranteed on the principal, not by something else.

In this passage, Al-Haskafi explains that kafala (guarantee) with certain items—sold goods (mabi'), mortgaged property (marhun), and trust items (amana)—is invalid in the Hanafi school. The key condition is that the guaranteed item must be personally owed by the principal; if it is only guaranteed by another consideration (like the price or the debt), kafala is not valid. This ruling differs from the Shafi'i school, which allows kafala for any guaranteed item. Understanding this distinction is crucial for muamalat involving guarantees and sureties.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the three categories of items (mabi’, marhun, amana) and the ruling: kafala with the physical item itself is invalid. Note that the ruling applies specifically to kafala for the 'ayn (the specific item), not for its delivery (taslim).
The passage also distinguishes between kafala for the item and for its delivery – the latter is valid.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i school allows kafala for any guaranteed item, regardless of whether the guarantee is personal or through another asset. State that they consider the sold item as guaranteed by the price, hence valid for kafala.
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that any item that is ultimately guaranteed (even if by a different party) can be the subject of kafala.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i school argues that since the sold item is guaranteed (if it perishes, the sale is void, and the price is returned), it is a guaranteed item, so kafala should be valid.
They see the guarantee as a general concept, not restricted to personal liability.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is based on the condition that the principal must be able to discharge liability only by paying the item or its equivalent. For mabi', the seller is not liable for the item before delivery; he only loses the price. For marhun, the mortgagee is not liable for the item; the debt is secured. For amana, the trustee is not liable for loss unless negligent. Therefore, kafala for the item is invalid because it would impose a liability that does not exist on the principal.
This is a talil (ratio legis) based on the nature of the obligation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Hanafi, a seller cannot be a guarantor for the sold item before delivery; under Shafi'i, such a guarantee would be valid. This affects contracts of sale with kafala clauses.
Also, the Hanafi school validates kafala for delivering (taslim) the item, so the disagreement is only about the item itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ومبيع ومرهون (وأمانة أي وبطلت الكفالة بالمبيع والمرهون. أما الكفالة بالمبيع للمشتري فلأن المبيع مضمون بغيره وهو الثمن، والكفالة بالأعيان المضمونة وإن كانت تصح عندنا خلافاً للشافعي لكن إنما تصح بالأعيان المضمونة بنفسها كالمبيع بيعاً فاسداً والمقبوض على سوم الشراء أو المغصوب لا بما كان مضموناً بغيره كالمبيع والمرهون لأن من شرطها أن يكون المكفول مضموناً على الأصيل بحيث لا يمكنه أن يخرج عنه إلا بدفعه أو دفع مثله، والمبيع قبل القبض ليس بمضمون على البائع حتى لو هلك لا يجب عليه شيء وإنما ينفسخ به البيع والمرهون غير مضمون على المرتهن بنفسه وإنما يسقط دينه إذا هلك فلا يمكن إيجاب الضمان على الكفيل وهو ليس بواجب على الأصيل أطلقه فشمل ما إذا ضمن الرهن عن المرتهن للراهن أو عكسه ؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين. وأما الأمانة كالوديعة ومال المضاربة والشركة والعارية والمستأجر في يد المستأجر فلا يمكن جعلها مضمونة على الكفيل وهي غير مضمونة على الأصيل وقالوا رد الوديعة ليس بواجب على المودع بل الواجب عدم المنع عند طلب المودع فلا يجب على الكفيل تسليمها قيد بالكفالة بالعين لأن الكفالة بتسليمها أمانة أو مضمونة صحيحة وفائدته حينئذ إلزام إحضار العين وتسليمها، ولو عجز بأن مات العبد المبيع أو المستأجر أو الرهن انفسخت الكفالة وزان الكفالة بالنفس سواء.
His statement: (and sold, mortgaged, and trust) i.e., the guarantee with the sold item and the mortgaged item is invalid. As for the guarantee with the sold item for the buyer, because the sold item is guaranteed by something else, namely the price. And the guarantee with guaranteed properties, although it is valid with us contrary to al-Shafi'i, it is only valid with properties that are personally guaranteed, such as an item sold in an invalid sale, an item taken on a promise to buy, or a usurped item, not with what is guaranteed by something else, like the sold item and the mortgaged item, because one of its conditions is that the guaranteed item must be guaranteed on the principal such that he cannot discharge himself from it except by paying it or its equivalent. And the sold item before delivery is not guaranteed on the seller, such that if it perishes, nothing is due from him; rather the sale is void due to it. And the mortgaged item is not personally guaranteed on the mortgagee; rather his debt falls if it perishes. Therefore it is not possible to impose liability on the guarantor while it is not obligatory on the principal. He made it general, so it includes if he guarantees the mortgage against the mortgagee for the mortgagor or vice versa; like this in Jami' al-Fusulain. As for the trust item, like a deposit, mudaraba capital, partnership capital, loan, and the leased item in the hands of the lessee, it is not possible to make it guaranteed on the guarantor while it is not guaranteed on the principal. And they said: returning the deposit is not obligatory on the depositee; rather the obligation is not to prevent when the depositor demands. So the guarantor is not obligated to deliver it. He qualified with guarantee of the very item, because the guarantee for delivering it, whether trust or guaranteed, is valid. Its benefit then is to oblige the production of the item and its delivery. And if he becomes unable, such as if the sold slave, the leased item, or the mortgage dies, the guarantee becomes void, like the guarantee of person.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage explains why kafala with sold items (mabi'), mortgaged items (marhun), and trust items (amana) is invalid in the Hanafi school. The sold item before delivery is not personally guaranteed on the seller; it is guaranteed by the price, so kafala for it is invalid. This is a point of disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who allows kafala for any guaranteed item. The Hanafi position is that kafala is only valid for items personally guaranteed on the principal, such as items in invalid sales, items taken on promise to buy, or usurped items. Similarly, a mortgaged item is not personally guaranteed on the mortgagee; his debt is secured but he is not liable for the item. Trust items like deposits are not guaranteed on the trustee; he is only obligated not to prevent the owner's access. However, the text distinguishes between kafala for the item itself (invalid) and kafala for delivering the item (valid). If the item perishes, the kafala becomes void.

Key Terms
مبيع (mabi'): Sold item, specifically before delivery
مرهون (marhun): Mortgaged item
أمانة (amana): Trust item, such as deposit, loan, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is kafala with a sold item (mabi') invalid?
A) Because the sold item is not yet owned by the seller.
B) Because the sold item is not personally guaranteed on the principal (seller), but rather by the price.
C) Because kafala cannot be for physical items, only for debts.
D) Because the buyer cannot guarantee something before receipt.

Key Takeaway
Kafala with the physical item itself is invalid unless the item is personally guaranteed on the principal; delivery guarantees are valid.

Valid Subjects of Kafala: Personally Guaranteed Items
Lists four valid subjects of kafala with rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories of items for which Hanafi kafala is valid and explain the unifying principle of personal guarantee (damān al-dhimma).

Core Idea
Kafala is valid for any item personally guaranteed, whether debt or specific property.

In Hanafi law, kafala (suretyship) transfers liability from the principal to the guarantor. Not all items qualify. This passage specifies four categories—price (thaman), usurped property (maghsus), property taken on a promise to buy (maqbud 'ala sawm al-shira'), and property sold in an invalid sale (mabi' fasad)—all unified by the principle that they are personally guaranteed (mawthuq bi al-dhimma). Understanding this principle helps you distinguish valid from invalid suretyship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
The passage states that kafala is valid for: 1) price (thaman), 2) usurped property, 3) property taken on a promise to buy, 4) property sold in an invalid sale. Note that these are not the only valid subjects, but they illustrate the rule.
The Arabic terms: ثمن، مغصوب، مقبوض على سوم الشراء، مبيع فاسد.
2
Understand the rationale for price
The price (thaman) is a valid debt (dayn sahih) owed by the buyer. Debts are inherently personally guaranteed, so suretyship for a debt is always valid.
3
Understand the rationale for the other three
Usurped property, property taken on a promise to buy, and invalidly sold property are all personally guaranteed because if the item perishes, the taker must pay its value (qimah). This liability makes them suitable for kafala.
Key phrase: 'مضمونًا بنفسه على الأصيل' – personally guaranteed on the principal.
4
Apply the principle to the branch case
The branch from Nur al-'Ayn shows: If the owner of goods takes a guarantor from a borrower or usurper for the return of the goods, it is valid. The guarantor then has recourse for the cost of his work. This confirms that items in the possession of a borrower or usurper are personally guaranteed.
Distinguish: an agent (wakil) is not compelled to return because he acts gratuitously; a guarantor is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وصح لو ثمناً ومغصوباً ومقبوضاً على سوم الشراء ومبيعاً فاسداً) أي صح الضمان لو كان المضمون إلى آخره. أما الثمن فلكونه ديناً صحيحاً مضموناً على المشتري، وأما ما عداه فلكونه مضموناً بنفسه على الأصيل لأنه إذا هلك وجبت قيمته وهي كهو. بالكفالة بالعين الخ) فرع ذكر في نور العين يرمز الجامع ما نصه رب المتاع لو أخذ من مستعيره أو غاصبه برده كفيلاً صح ولو رد رجع عليه بأجر مثل عمله إذ الكفيل بأمر يرجع بما ضمن وشمل عمله أجر عمله، ولو أخذ به وكيلاً لا كفيلاً لا يجبر على رده لتبرعه بخلاف الكفيل اهـ.
His statement: (and it is valid if [the guaranteed item is] price, usurped, taken on a promise to buy, and invalidly sold) i.e., the guarantee is valid if the guaranteed item is... As for the price, because it is a valid debt guaranteed on the buyer. As for what other than it, because it is personally guaranteed on the principal, for if it perishes, its value is due, and it is like it. (A branch regarding kafala with the 'ayn) mentioned in Nur al-'Ayn, referencing al-Jami', its text: 'The owner of the goods, if he takes from its borrower or usurper a guarantor for its return, it is valid. And if he returns, he (the guarantor) has recourse against him (the borrower/usurper) for the equivalent of his work, because the guarantor, by his order, has recourse for what he guaranteed, and his work includes the wage for his work. And if he takes him as an agent (wakil), not a guarantor, he is not compelled to return it because of his gratuitousness, unlike the guarantor.' End.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage states that kafala is valid for the price (thaman), usurped property (maghsus), property taken on a promise to buy (maqbud 'ala sawm al-shira'), and property sold in an invalid sale (mabi' fasad). The price is a valid debt guaranteed on the buyer. The other items are personally guaranteed on the principal because if they perish, their value is due. The text also includes a branch from Nur al-'Ayn: if the owner of goods takes a guarantor from a borrower or usurper for the return of the goods, it is valid. The guarantor then has recourse for the cost of his work. However, if the owner takes an agent instead of a guarantor, the agent is not compelled to return because he acts voluntarily.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): Price; the debt of payment in a sale
مغصوب (maghsus): Usurped property
مقبوض على سوم الشراء (maqbud 'ala sawm al-shira'): Property taken on a promise to buy
مبيع فاسد (mabi' fasad): Item sold in an invalid sale

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following items is NOT a valid subject of kafala?
A) The price (thaman) in a sale
B) Usurped property (maghsus)
C) A deposited item (wadi'a) left for safekeeping
D) Property sold in an invalid sale (mabi' fasad)

Key Takeaway
Kafala is valid for any item that is personally guaranteed (madhmun bi al-dhimma), whether it is a debt or a specific property whose loss entails liability for its value.

Dispute on Kafala for Delivering a Loaned Item ('Ariya)
Intra-Hanafi dispute with refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on kafala for delivering an 'ariyah, identify the opposing view of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, and evaluate the author's refutation using textual evidence.

Core Idea
Kafala for delivering a loaned item is valid; the obligation to return exists upon demand.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of kafala for delivering a loaned item ('ariyah). Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi held it invalid, but the author of Bahr al-Ra'iq refutes this by citing multiple authoritative texts (al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Mabsut, al-Quduri, al-Tuhfa) and arguing that the duty to return applies equally to all trusts. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to weigh conflicting positions within the school and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling on kafala for delivering an 'ariyah that the author defends. It is valid, based on explicit texts from major Hanafi works.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi (a leading Hanafi imam) held that such kafala is invalid. Note that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
Al-Sarakhsi's view is not from outside the school.
3
Analyze the opposing dalil
Understand the basis for Sarakhsi's position. The passage does not quote his proof, but implies he differentiated between types of trusts. The author refutes this differentiation.
Sarakhsi's reasoning may rely on the idea that the borrower is not liable for the item.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The author refutes Sarakhsi by citing multiple nusus (texts) and arguing that the obligation to return is common to all trusts. He also addresses the counterargument that the obligation is only to make available (takhliya), not physically return, by showing that the guarantor can undertake this.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if kafala is taken, under Sarakhsi it is void and the guarantor incurs no liability. Under the majority view, the guarantor must produce the item or pay its value if he fails.
The mufta bihi is validity (majority view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي أن الكفالة بتسليم العارية باطلة باطل فقد نص في الجامع الصغير أن الكفالة بتسليم العارية صحيحة، وكذا في المبسوط. ونص القدوري أنها بتسليم المبيع جائزة، ونص في التحفة على جميع ما أوردنا أن الكفالة بالتسليم صحيحة. والوجه عندي أن لا فرق الثلاث الأول من الوديعة ومال المضاربة والشركة وبين العارية بين وما معها من الآمانات إذ لا شك في وجوب الرد عند الطلب. فإن قال الواجب التخلية بينه وبينها لا ردها إليه فنقول : فليكن مثل هذا الواجب على الكفيل وهو أن يحصلها ويخلي بينه وبينها بعد إحضاره إليها ونحن نعني بوجوب الرد ما هو أعم هذا ومن حمل المردود من إليه. قال في الذخيرة : الكفالة بتمكين المودع من الأخذ صحيحة ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. ورده على شمس الأئمة السرخسي مأخوذ من معراج الدراية ويساعده قول الشارح ويجوز في الكل أن يتكفل بتسليم العين مضمونة أو أمانة وقيل : إن كان تسليمه واجباً على الأصيل كالعارية والإجارة جاز وإلا فلا فأفاد أن التفصيل بين أمانة وأمانة ضعيف . قوله : (والوجه عندي أن لا فرق الخ) رد على التفصيل الآتي المنقول عن الشارح الزيلعي.
And what Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned that kafala for delivering a loaned item (ariyah) is invalid is false. For it is textually stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir that kafala for delivering a loaned item is valid, and likewise in al-Mabsut. And al-Quduri stated that kafala for delivering a sold item is permissible. And it is stated in al-Tuhfa regarding all that we have presented that kafala for delivery is valid. The sound opinion in my view is that there is no difference between the first three of deposit, mudaraba capital, and partnership, and between the loan and what is with it of trusts, since there is no doubt about the obligation of return upon demand. For if it is said: the obligation is to make it available (takhliya) to him, not to return it to him, we say: let such obligation be upon the guarantor, which is that he procures it and makes it available to him after bringing it to him. And we mean by the obligation of return what is more general than this and what includes delivering the returned item to the one entitled. It is said in al-Dhakhira: kafala for enabling the depositor to take is valid; likewise in Fath al-Qadir. And his refutation of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi is taken from Mi'raj al-Diraya, and it is supported by the statement of the sharh: it is permissible in all to guarantee delivery of the item, whether guaranteed or trust. And it was said: if its delivery is obligatory on the principal, like loan and lease, it is valid; otherwise no. So it shows that the differentiation between one trust and another is weak. His statement: (and the correct view is that there is no difference, etc.) is a refutation of the following detail transmitted from al-Zayla'i.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi held that kafala for delivering an 'ariyah (loan) is invalid. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq refutes him, citing multiple authoritative texts: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Mabsut, al-Quduri, and al-Tuhfa, all of which state that kafala for delivering any item (whether trust or guaranteed) is valid. The author argues that there is no difference between various types of trusts (deposit, mudaraba, partnership, loan) because the obligation to return exists upon demand. Some claim the obligation is only to make available, not to physically return, but the author says this obligation can be imposed on the guarantor. The refutation is supported by al-Dhakhira and Fath al-Qadir. The author concludes that the attempted differentiation between different types of trusts is weak. The passage ends with a comment that the author's preferred view (that there is no difference) is a refutation of a detail from al-Zayla'i.

Key Terms
عارية ('ariyah): Loan of a permissible item for use, with the obligation to return
تسليم (taslim): Delivery or handing over of an item

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on kafala for delivering a loaned item ('ariyah) according to the author of Bahr al-Ra'iq?
A) Invalid, as held by Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi.
B) Valid, based on many Hanafi texts.
C) Valid only if the loan is for a specific period.
D) Invalid because the borrower is not liable for the item.

Key Takeaway
Kafala for delivering a loaned item is valid in the Hanafi school; the majority view relies on explicit texts and the principle that the obligation to return is common to all trusts.

Suretyship for a Terminally Ill Debtor: Validity Conditions
Ruling on kafalah from a terminally ill debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of kafalah when the debtor is terminally ill and identify the key condition that makes it valid.

Core Idea
Kafalah for a terminally ill debtor is valid only if the guarantee is stated to take effect after death, or if the sick person requests it during illness.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario in the Book of Suretyship: when a debtor is terminally ill (marid marad al-mawt) and his creditors are absent. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses whether a third party can guarantee the debt on his behalf and when such a suretyship becomes legally effective. This ruling touches upon the intersection of debt, illness, and the rights of creditors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage presents a sick person (marid) with absent creditors (ghuraba' ghayb). A third party offers to guarantee the debt.
The key factors: the debtor is terminally ill, creditors are not present, and the guarantee is made during illness.
2
Extract the primary ruling
The text states: 'المريض ذلك منهم والغرماء غيب لم يصح' — the suretyship is not valid in this default situation. Then it says: 'ولو قالوا ذلك بعد موته صحت الكفالة' — if they say that after his death, it is valid. So the condition is that the guarantee must be explicitly linked to after death.
The ruling distinguishes between a guarantee without time reference (invalid) and one specifying after death (valid).
3
Examine the narration from Abu Hanifa
There is a riwaya that Abu Hanifa permitted suretyship during illness even without the sick person's request. This is an alternative view within the school, found in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Khaniyya.
This adds another condition: if the sick person requests the guarantee, it is also valid during illness.
4
Understand the scholarly difference
Some early scholars interpret the valid guarantee as a wasiyya (bequest) to pay the debt after death, not as kafala. Others, following Abu Hanifa, maintain it is a kafala. The text explains Abu Hanifa's view by analogy: the sick person is like an interpreter for his creditors, so he can authorize suretyship.
The difference affects whether the arrangement is limited to one-third of the estate (wasiyya) or not (kafala).
5
Apply the analogy
Just as a stranger can ask the heirs to guarantee and they can do so, the sick person can also have suretyship arranged. The mufta bihi position is that the guarantee must be stated after death, or the sick person must request it during illness.
This resolves the scenario and provides a clear rule for practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة
المريض ذلك منهم والغرماء غيب لم يصح، ولو قالوا ذلك بعد موته صحت الكفالة، وروي عن أبي حنيفة جواز كفالتهم في مرضه وإن لم يطلب المريض منهم ذلك؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج والخانية. وفي البدائع وأما مسألة المريض فقد قال بعض مشايخنا : إن جواز الضمان بطريق الإيصاء بالقضاء عنه بعد موته لا بطريق الكفالة، وبعضهم أجازوه على سبيل الكفالة . ووجه ما أشار إليه أبو حنيفة في الأصل وقال هو بمنزلة المعبر عن غرمائه وشرح هذه الإشارة والله أعلم أن المريض مرض الموت يتعلق الدين بماله ويصير بمنزلة الأجنبي عنه حتى لا ينفذ منه التصرف المبطل لحق الغريم، ولو قال أجنبي للورثة اضمنو الغرماء فلان عنه فقالوا ضمنا يكتفي به فكذا المريض اهـ .
Book of Suretyship. The sick person, that among them and the creditors absent, it is not valid. But if they say that after his death, the suretyship is valid. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa the permissibility of their suretyship during his illness even if the sick person did not ask them for that; likewise in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Khaniyya. And in al-Bada'i: As for the case of the sick person, some of our scholars said: The validity of the guarantee is by way of a will (wasiyya) of payment on his behalf after his death, not by way of suretyship (kafala). And some permitted it on the basis of suretyship. And the explanation of what Abu Hanifa indicated in al-Asl, and he said: He is like the interpreter of his creditors. And the explanation of this indication, and Allah knows best, is that the terminally ill person's debt is attached to his property and he becomes like a stranger to it, so that a transaction that invalidates the creditor's right is not upheld. And if a stranger said to the heirs: 'Guarantee the creditors of so-and-so on his behalf,' and they said 'We guarantee,' it is sufficient. So likewise the sick person. End.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Suretyship with a ruling on a specific scenario: when a sick person (al-marid) has debts, and the creditors are absent (ghuraba' ghayb). The ruling states that if someone offers to guarantee on his behalf during his illness without his request, it is not valid. However, if they say that after his death (i.e., they agree to pay after his death), the suretyship becomes valid. There is a narration from Abu Hanifa that permits their suretyship during his illness even without his request. This is found in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and al-Khaniyya. Then the author discusses a difference of opinion: some early scholars said the valid guarantee is actually a bequest (wasiyya) to pay after death, not a suretyship. Others allowed it as suretyship. Abu Hanifa's view in al-Asl is that the sick person is like an interpreter (mu'abbar) of his creditors, meaning he has the authority to request suretyship. The analogy is made to a stranger asking the heirs to guarantee, and if they do, it suffices. So the sick person also can have suretyship arranged on his behalf by others.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafala): Suretyship or guarantee where a person (kafil) assumes responsibility for a debt or obligation owed by another.
مريض مرض الموت (marid marad al-mawt): A person who is terminally ill, to the extent that his death is likely. His property is considered tied to his debts, and his transactions may be restricted.
إيصاء (isiya'): Bequest or will (wasiyya) — a directive to pay a debt after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a kafalah for a terminally ill debtor valid according to the primary ruling?
A) Only if the creditors are present.
B) Only if the guarantee is stated to take effect after death.
C) Only if the debtor requests it during illness.
D) Only if the guarantor is a relative.

Key Takeaway
A suretyship for a terminally ill debtor is valid only if stated to take effect after his death, or if he requests it during his illness.
