Kafalah for a Deceased Insolvent Debtor
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding kafalah for a deceased insolvent debtor, explain the evidence on each side, and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
A debt does not survive death if no estate remains—Abu Hanifa's view; but the Sahibayn hold the debt persists for the Hereafter, making kafalah valid.

This lesson explores a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school: can a person guarantee the debt of someone who dies with no money or estate? The school's three founders differ, and the resolution turns on the nature of debt after death. Understanding this dispute sharpens your skills in applying proofs (adilla) and resolving ikhtilaf within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Core Dispute
The act is kafalah (suretyship) for a person who dies insolvent (no estate and no kafil). The dispute: is this kafalah valid or invalid?
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and Reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that kafalah is invalid because the debt is extinguished. The debtor cannot repay and has no substitute (estate or kafil), so the debt falls by necessity. A voluntary payment does not revive the debt.
He sees debt as a legal obligation (wujub) that is reality an action; without possibility of recovery, it ceases.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Evidence
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold kafalah valid. They cite the hadith of Abu Qatada who guaranteed the debt of a deceased man, and the Prophet prayed over him. They argue the debt remains established for the Hereafter, so it can be guaranteed.
The hadith is a dalil naqli; they also reason that the debt is still owed to the claimant and no extinguisher is found.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response to the Hadith
Abu Hanifa's supporters interpret the hadith as referring to a pre-existing suretyship or a voluntary promise, not a new kafalah. They argue the report of an action has no general applicability.
This shows how ta'wil (interpretation) resolves apparent proof conflicts.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and Exceptions
The mufta bihi is the position of the Sahibayn (valid). However, an exception exists: if a debt arises after death (e.g., from digging a well), kafalah for it is valid even for a deceased insolvent.
Later authorities like al-Marghinani prefer the Sahibayn's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وعن ميت مفلس أي وبطلت الكفالة عن ميت مفلس وهذا عند أبي حنيفة . وقالا . صحيحة لما روي أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أتى بجنازة رجل من الأنصار فسأل هل عليه دين؟ قالوا: نعم درهمان أو ديناران فامتنع من الصلاة فقال صلوا على أخيكم. فقام أبو قتادة فقال : هما علي يا رسول الله فصلى عليه. ولأنه كفل بدين ثابت لأنه وجب لحق الطالب ولم يوجد المسقط ولهذا يبقى في حق أحكام الآخرة ، ولو تبرع به إنسان يصح ولذا يبقى إذا كان به كفيل. وله أنه كفل بدين ساقط لأن الدين هو الفعل حقيقة ولهذا يوصف بالوجوب لأنه في الحكم مال لأنه يؤول إليه في المآل وقد عجز بنفسه وبخلفه ففات عاقبة الاستيفاء فيسقط ضرورة والتبرع لا يعتمد قيام الدين. وإذا كان له كفيل أو له مال فخلفه إذ الإفضاء إلى الأداء باق. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان الكفيل أجنبياً أو وارث الميت ولو ابنه؛ كذا في المعراج. والجواب عن الحديث أنه يحتمل الإقرار عن كفالة سابقة والإنشاء والوعد وحكاية الفعل لا عموم لها وقيد بالكفالة بعد موته لأنه لو كفل في حياته ثم مات مفلساً لم تبطل الكفالة، وكذا لو كان به رهن ثم مات مفلساً لا يبطل الرهن لأن سقوط الدين عنه في أحكام الدنيا في حقه للضرورة فتتقدر بقدرها فأبقيناه في حق الكفيل والرهن لعدم الضرورة؛ كذا في المعراج وبما قررناه علم أن الميت المفلس من مات ولا تركة له ولا كفيل عنه، ويستثنى من بطلانها مسألة في التحرير من بحث الموت من عوارض الأهلية لو تقوت الذمة بلحوق دين بعد الموت صحت الكفالة به بأن حفر بئراً على الطريق فتلف به حیوان بعد موته فإنه يثبت الدين مستنداً إلى وقت الحفر الثابت حال قيام الذمة والمستند يثبت أولاً في الحال، ويلزم اعتبار قوتها حينئذ به لكونه محل الاستيفاء اهـ
His statement: '(And concerning a deceased insolvent)' i.e., the kafalah for a deceased insolvent is invalid. And this is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is valid, because it is narrated that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was brought a funeral of a man from the Ansar, and he asked: 'Does he have any debt?' They said: 'Yes, two dirhams or two dinars.' He refrained from praying and said: 'Pray for your brother.' So Abu Qatada stood up and said: 'They are upon me, O Messenger of Allah.' So he prayed over him. And because he (the kafil) guaranteed a debt that is established, because it was obliged for the right of the claimant and no cause of extinguishment is found, and hence it remains with regard to the rulings of the Hereafter. And if a person voluntarily pays it, it is valid, and hence it remains if he has a kafil. And for him (Abu Hanifa), he guaranteed a debt that is extinguished, because the debt is really an action (the act of repayment), and hence it is described as obligatory because it is legally wealth since it leads to it eventually. And he (the debtor) has become incapable by himself and by his substitute (the estate), so the ultimate possibility of recovery is lost, so it falls by necessity. And voluntary payment does not rely on the existence of the debt. And if he has a kafil or he has property, then his substitute exists, because the means to payment remains. He (the author) gave it absolutely, so it includes whether the kafil is a stranger or the heir of the deceased, even his son; likewise in al-Mi'raj. And the answer to the hadith is that it may be admission of a previous suretyship, or a commencement, or a promise, and the report of an action has no general applicability. And it is restricted to kafalah after his death, because if he (the debtor) became a kafil during his lifetime and then died insolvent, the kafalah would not be invalid. Similarly, if he had a pledge (rahn) and then died insolvent, the pledge is not invalidated, because the waiver of the debt from him in the rulings of this world is out of necessity, so it is measured accordingly, and we leave it remaining with regard to the kafil and the pledge due to absence of necessity; likewise in al-Mi'raj. And from what we have established, it is known that the deceased insolvent is one who dies with no estate and no kafil for him. And an exception to its invalidity is a case in al-Tahrir from the discussion of death as a source of legal capacity: if the dhimma (obligation-bearing capacity) becomes strong by a debt attaching after death, the kafalah for it is valid. For example, if someone digs a well on the road and an animal is destroyed thereby after his death, the debt is established with reference to the time of digging, which was when the dhimma existed. And the reference is established first in the present, and it is necessary to consider its strength then because it is the place of recovery. End.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the validity of kafalah (suretyship) for a person who dies insolvent (mufa). Abu Hanifa holds that such a kafalah is invalid, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it valid. The two cite a hadith where Abu Qatada guaranteed the debt of a deceased person, and the Prophet prayed over him, indicating the validity of such an undertaking. They argue that the debt is still established as it was owed to the claimant, and no cause of extinguishment is found; it remains in the rulings of the Hereafter. Abu Hanifa argues that the debt is extinguished because the debtor is incapable of repayment and has no estate; the debt as a legal obligation ceases by necessity. The voluntary payment of a third party does not revive the debt. The hadith is interpreted as referring to a pre-existing suretyship or a promise, not a new guarantee. The ruling is qualified: if the debtor had a kafil or property during his life, the kafalah remains valid. The passage also includes an exception for debts that arise after death due to actions prior to death.

Key Terms
ميت مفلس (mayyit mufa): A deceased person who leaves no estate (tarika) and has no kafil (guarantor) for his debts.
كفيل (kafil): The person who undertakes the suretyship (kafalah), guaranteeing the debt of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on kafalah for a deceased insolvent according to Abu Hanifa?
A) Valid, because the debt remains for the Hereafter.
B) Invalid, because the debt is extinguished by the debtor's death and inability to pay.
C) Valid only if the kafil is an heir.
D) Invalid only if the kafil is a stranger.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi position is that kafalah for a deceased insolvent is valid, based on the hadith of Abu Qatada and the persistence of debt for the Hereafter.
